KILLS FLIES
The Red Page
Here’s to Australia!
This page has been almost swamped by
the toasts showered upon it in what
has been easily the most popular competition
held in recent years. Nearly 200 entries
were rejected before serious judgment could
begin. Incidentally, this competition brought
to light some interesting facts. Les H.
Pritchard writes: —
On Christmas Eve, 1914, in Cairo, I and
about 50 other Australians and M.L. boys
(“Anzae” was not coined then) were in
the Egyptian Cafe with a goodly number of
English soldiers, then doing garrison duty,
and French civilians. About midnight things
got rather lively, assisted by a fine orchestra-
A Frenchman asked'for “The Marseillaise,”
which was sung and toasted by the French-
men. “Rule Britannia” was then demanded,
toasted and sung, and then cheered by the
Tommies, the A. and M.L. boys assisting;
but as there was that outsider feeling to us,
my pal (who is still annoying the Huns in
France) knocked me in the ribs and said,
“Go on! toast Australia.” I had never
made a toast in my life, so I just
raised my glass and blurted out: “Aus-
tralians—Australia !” and shook hands
with those nearest me. We had no song to
follow up with,- for not even “Australia Will
be There” was known then. Well, if those
two words and the hand-shake did not imply
the real dinkum feeling and thoughts to each
one, I don’t know my pals. I do not claim
this is the first or only instance that those
two words were so reverently honored, but
I do claim all fancy phrases will never
. appeal to the Australians of Australia as
these.
And “16th Q.M.S.” writes: I don’t claim
to chip in with anything original, but the
Anzacs have to my mind a very pretty toast
much in use in London, viz., “Here’s how,
Jim, I love Australia.” Sounds tame; but
spend six months in the trenches, and a few
more in hospital, then go to Blighty on your
last furlough, booked for more strafe with
the next batch, holding no illusions about
the firing-line, ready to admit you were
crimson lucky to get off so light when that
shell burst, and you will know what it is
to hear a dinkum Billzac say,. “Here’s how
I love Australia.” Anyhow, it is Sasanof
to an issue of tinned dog you will buy him
another beer. You can’t imagine Bill say-
ing “Here’s to the dear ones at Home,” or
“The girl I left behind me,” but “Here’s
how I love Australia.” I have seen it scores
of times standing shoulder to shoulder in a
cosmopolitan crowd, that defiant. “How I
love Australia,” a toast and a challenge, and
then a pause, and that thoughtful, far-away
stare that looks clean across the seas, that
takes in, in silence, sweethearts, mothers,
wives and homes, and just a pause, and “Fill
’em up again, Mister. I guess it’s my turn.”
A “Bulletin Fiend,” whose only address
is “Yes” State, objects to any frill: To ask
for a toast in words or prose is an insult.
Words are a superfluity, and no journalist
can paint the lily. It needs but one word
shouted, when every Australian within
Marconi will upend his carcase, and dip
his beak until the glass is as dry as last
week’s sermon. For one word Billjim will
commit murder, arson or suicide. I supply
my toast free —if you doubt it send the
Religious Editor to any pubbery before
wowser-time (6 p.m..), let him walk boldly
to the counter and say:
“Gentlemen—AUSTRALIA!”
A number of competitors made the mis-
take of “saying a few kind words about
Australia.” giving us the proposer’s speech,
and not his tag. Excellent speeches were
made by “Simon,” R.G.F.. George R.
Hambidge and Bob Dexter. But what was
wanted was a toast, not a speech. The most
common error in the entries was the assump-
tion that the Anzae is solely Australian.
The Maorilander owns two letters of that
name to Australia’s one. (Incidentally jit
seems a pity that the name was not origin-
ally spelt A.M.L.A.0.) Thus a great num-
ber of toasts, in which Anzae formed part,
such as Katherine Greene’s “Australia —
Anzac’s mother,” had to be discarded, since
they would have applied equally to M.L.
Australia has no right to filch from her
brothers-in-arms, a proprietary interest in
their common title of honor. Some of the
other toasts were mere catalogues of wheat,
. wine, coal and the other goods in the Com-
monwealth’s shop-window. Many were
merely vague generalities that would have
applied to any country in the world. And
other fine toasts were too long. The toast
we want for Australia must be short enough
to be easily memorised, and one that will
apply for all time.

Of those left in for final decision, Bartlett
Adamson’s toast is a poem to be printed,
not a toast'to be spoken:—
This great Australia that our fathers won
In proud defiance of a thousand fates J
This ocean-garden sacred to the sun!
This land of home! O land where men
are mates!
Drink to your native ranges and your plains,
Men with the sunlight singing in your veins!
“Australia, the land of Sunshine and
Freedom. May there always be freedom in
her sunshine, and sunshine in her freedom,”
by “South Australian,” is a pretty play on
words, but almost tautological.
Sidney Finney’s:
To the women and men of you!
To the gold in the heart of you!
To the flag waving over you!
To the heroes who died for you!'
Sunny Australia!
might apply equally to other countries.

ravishing foe ever break,” by “Number One,”
is too literary.

tralia,” by “Two Blues,” is a toast for Aus-
tralasia.

acteristieally and solely Australian:

selves Men,
Who’re wearing • war’s blood-stained
regalia;
Here's to the mothers who sent them; and
then,
Here’s to their homeland, Australia!

The fault of O.P.K.’s toast is that some
day Australia will have a capable jockey.
L.K.’s midwifery might not be approved
by a mixed assembly. Gus, Logue is
neat but not memorable. “Royal Orange”

turn he gives to the White Australia policy.
But, gentlemen, if you don’t like this
toast the chairman will allow you to propose
any of the others.
WHAT BILLZAG REAUS.
“Inchiquin”: The Lord forbid that I
should say one word at all derogatory to
the Y.M.C.A. As a soldier I am full of
appreciation for the work they are doing
and the great convenience they are to us'.
But the other night I blew in to the one
attached to our camp, settled myself com-
fortably down at .the reading-table and
looked through the collection for something
to read. These are what I found: The
Welcome Visitor (a book containing
religious fables suitable for a kindergarten
Sunday-school class), The Bible (interest-
ing but too disjointed), London Punch of
July, 1912 (apparently in English eyes L.P.
no matter how old never fails to amuse),
The Sphere (a paper for ladies with some
interesting fashion pictures) and The
Sunday at Home.
“Sparrow”: Humorous poems and short
sketches are what our boys at the Front seem
to crave. “Garry” Roberts, a sort of godfather
to the “Sentimental Bloke,” showed me a
letter he had recently received from a cap-
tain, describing the return from a charge of
Anzacs on the German trenches. The boys
were breathing hard, but trying to look as
if this sort of thing was only part of the
day’s routine. The captain thought some-
thing was needed to take their minds off it,
anyhow, so he read out “Den’s” account of
the Sentimental Bloke’s visit to “Romeo and
Juliet” with Doreen. The effect was some-
thing to remember. Whether the boys were
worked up to a semi-hysterical pitch by
what they had just been through, or
whether they really had an extraordinarily
keen relish for Dennis’s-humor is not quite
clear; but they received the reading with
such uproarious delight that the captain
had to bring discipline to bear in order to
“moderate their exuberance,” as he put it.
-——— •
Art in Australia (edited and published
by Sydney Ure Smith, Bertram Stevens and
C. L. Jones, 55.; per Tyrrell’s, Ltd., Castle-
reagh-street, Sydney, distributing agents),
looks like becoming a permanent possession,
the representative art journal of the Com-
monwealth. The aim of this magazine, which
if it goes on as well as it has begun, should'
eventually become a monthly, is to make the
work of Australian artists better known to
the Australian public. (And ultimately, why
not to the world?) And the editors, with a
crafty blend of art enthusiasm and commer-
cial acumen, have modestly refrained from
being heavy or technical. Each of the ad-
mirable color reproductions of our artists’
work (most of which are suitable for, and
deserve, -framing) has attached to it a brief
personal and critical note of the artist and
his method. That these criticisms are appre-
ciative does not detract from their truth,
since Australians are too much given to a
depreciation of their culture, and our art
stands comparison fairly with the work of
the younger artists of any other nation. The
other articles, which include a delightful
essay by Norman Lindsay, are commendably
brief and to the point. A feature of the
publication is a number of reproductions of
charming etchings and pen-drawings; and
another feature—equally valuable in the
cause of art —is the high quality of design
and spacing of the advertisements.
The Australian Soldiers’ Gift Booh, pub-
lished by the Voluntary Workers’ Associa-
tion in aid of the fund for building homes
for N. S. Wales soldiers, Sydney, and
edited by Ethel punter and Bertram Stevens,
is excellent value for the cash and the
cause. For some amazing reason it is dedi-
cated not to the returned soldiers but to the
temporary Governor of N. S. Wales
and his wife, on the ground that these visi-
tors to Australia “have taken an active in-
terest in the Association.” A good many of
us have done that. The writers and artists
appealed to (chiefly belonging to No South
Wales) have given generously of their
talents; and the volume forms a characteris-
tically Australian gift-book that will be wel-
comed as much here as it will be when sent
to the trenches.

Look to Your Teeth.
There are no teeth like those furnished by
Nature. For service, comfort and good
health artificial teeth cannot be com-
pared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to pre-
serve their teeth as long as possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap
and water are useful, but are somewhat
ineffectual: Rough powders, such as cuttle-
fish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.
EUDENTA TOOTH PASTE is scientifi
cally prepared to clean the teeth, sweeten
the breath, harden the gums and disinfect
the mouth (thus preventing decay).
EUDENTA is therefore regarded as “the
perfection of dental luxuries.” 1/- tube every-
where. Post free, 1/3. Free sample posted.

direct influence of evil spirits, and exorcism and magic

Science has taught us wisdom. The evil spirits exist
still. We call them ‘‘Disease Germs,” and they also
must be cast out. Once lodged in the stomach or
intestines, fever with its hallucinations, or biliousness with
its aches and pains, is the result.
ENO S FRUIT SALT’
is the approved remedy for driving out disease germs.
Its action is quick and thorough. It clears the intestines,
rouses the torpid liver to new life, stimulates the mucous
membrane to a healthy action, and cleanses and invigorates
the whole digestive tract.
Be prepared for emergencies by always keeping a
bottle in the house. '

MR. GEORGE GOODMAN.
The following testimony is from Mr.
George Goodman, better known as
“Goody” in the profession, and one of
the most widely and professionally
known of a band of brilliant theatrical
managers who form an artistic link
between the theatrical past and
present.
Mr. Goodman has been associated with
the present firm of J. C. Williamson,
Ltd., for a long number of years, ex-
tending back to its inception, when
as “Williamson, Garner and Mus-
grove,” it occupied, as it does to-day,
the foremost position in the theatrical
world of Australia, and was respon-
sible for the finest imported produc-
tions of Grand Opera and versatile
drama and comedy.
Mr. Goodman’s experience with these"
productions and association with the
many “stars” imported under the Wil-
liamson management, is proverbial,
and the following letter will be of
considerable interest to the many
members of the theatrical profession
throughout the Commonwealth, who,
physically exhausted by the exacting
nature of their many r6les, will find
in the medicine Mr. Goodman advo-
cates a nerve rejuvenator and health
restorer of remarkable power and cer-
tainty. Mr. Goodman writes from his_
Sydney residence thus :
BEVERLY, BAYSWATER ROAD,
DARLINGHURST, 20/9/’l6.

To suffer with fortitude is
heroic, but to suffer needlessly
is idiotic. While most sufferers
regard pain as due to the
dispensation of Providence,
doctors see in it a certain
neglect—the neglect of simple
rules of health.
OF all rheumatic afflictions acute lumbago is the
most painful. It arises from a gradual accumu-
lation of excess uric acid in the system ; the muscles of
the lower back become impregnated until the muscu-
lar fibres stiffen and every movement causes agony.
The simple habit of taking Kruschen Salts every
morning cleanses the system of excess uric acid, braces
the nerves and gives to the user the daily enjoyment
of natural vigorous health. Get the Kruschen habit.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Challenge.
She raced and frolicked with a merry crew
And dared the devil, as some women do.
She cried, “Who catches me, and holds me
tight
Against you all, shall be my love to-night!”
Then, as she ran, her laughter down the
wind
Sobbed challenges to the mad rout behind ;
Till at her shoulder came a panting
breath....
She turned, and fell into the arms of Death.
N.S.W.
FRANK MORTON.
Dawn of Love.
Since that we met I can but dream my
dreams;
My hands fall idle from my weaving task;
For my heart’s flame my body serves as
mask,
As the dull scabbard hides the sword’s white
gleams.
And this, I muse, he looked, or this he said,
Or herent was his swift lips pressed my hair,
Leaving the strands caressed more golden
fair.
His finger-tips have rouged my cheeks rose-
red.
The day is radiant as a skylark’s flight,
The sea a jewel flung in golden space,
Pulsing to where the moon’s white shoulder
gleams
Half slipping through her robe of velvet
night,
My laughing fingers flush and inter-lace.
Since that we met I can but dream my
dreams.
N.S.W.
NORA McAULIFFE.
The Dying Tree.
The yellow'leaves are falling,
And sere the branches wave;
Ah, Grace and Joy and Beauty
Are withering for the grave.
A lingering sunbeam’s gilding
The crest of yonder tree,
Oh, may it not the farewell kiss
Of fading Summer be?
I feel as if the teardrops
Must fall like wintry rain;
This scene hath brought before me
Our parting hour again.
O love, ’twas mine to leave thee,
Thy early doom foresee;
I was the parting summer—
And thou the dying tree.
QUEENSLAND.
F. B
Cui Bono?
Out of the sunset splendor, that turned the
sky to flame,
To me in the dingy city the voice of the sea-
wind came—
Caine with a mystical message that only my
heart could hear,
Of the marvellous City of Amber, on her
mountain ridge throned clear
A thousand feet from the plains that drowse
below in the heat.
Where the bullocks grunt from the mudpools,
and the scream of a parrakeet
Shatters the noonday silence where the
peepul branches twine,
Shedding a solemn shadow about the white-
walled shrine.
Once, when the world was younger, or ere
men’s hearts grew old,
We watched from the walls of Amber the
banners of dawn unrolled,
Pomp of purple and azure, splendor of scarlet
and gold.
Does no dim memory waken of the wonder-
ful nights we knew,
When the hot white stars above us burned
deep in the boundless blue?
And your star-eyes mocked me and mad-
dened, till my passionate lips were
pressed
To the blood-red bloom of your mouth and
the wildflowers twain of your breast?
I was a slave in Amber, and you from a
monarch sprung,
But you swore that you loved me once in
days when love and the world were
young!
Over the walls of Amber the wild vine trails
and twines /
Her desolate wanton blossoms; the prowling
jackal whines
In the Hall of a Hundred Columns, where
king spoke once with king,
While the people trembled and waited the
word that the day might bring.
Never a voice in the stillness, never a step to
wake
The age-old sleep of the city that never again
shall wake.
Only the grey ape chatters amid the deodars ;
Only the crumbling ruins lie naked beneath
the stars.
Cone is the greatness of Amber ! The beauty,
the strength and the pride.
They are gone, they are lost, they are scat-
tered, as the foam of a fallen tide.
As the flush that fades with the sunset, as
the flame that dies with the dew,
They are utterly fled and forgotten—and you
have forgotten, too.
Forgotten!—but I remember the nights that
we two have known
In the perfumed hush of your chamber, when
you swore you were mine alone.
It was false as Hell, and I knew it well; but
I knew it in vain, for how
Could I cease to love you because you lied?
—as I know you lie to me now!
N.S.'W.
F. S. BIRNELL.

TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.
(In Advance, Only.)
For £1 6s. 6d. per annum you can have The
Bulletin sent regularly to you every week for a
year, including Special Christmas Number, to any
address in Australasia, post paid.
Post free to any address abroad, £ 1 10s. per annum.
Cheques, drafts, etc., which should be crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to The
Manager.
Special Notice.
Jnsufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column
all contributions submitted, whether in the form of
Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales
or Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects,
and not exceeding two columns in length, or, say,
SOOO words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin- will return all unsuitable MS. ivhen
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

after the arrival of each mail, from Messrs. Gordon
and Gotcli, Ludgate Circus.

THURSDAY, DECEMBER 28, 1916.
CURVES AND ANGLES: A NEW YEAR
SERMON.
The theory that the world moves on a zig-
zag' course, and that its zigs and its zags
occur at stated intervals, clearly defined and
properly marked on the calendar, has as
many holes in it as a fowl-run of wife-net-
ting. The circular sweep of the solar system
seems to have caught up everything in its
train. The wind goes in circles; tree-bole
and flower and grass-stem all aim at the
rounded shape; Sissy is only beautiful in
as far as she curves; and if human events
and man’s mind and the general set of the
communal and individual soul move to com-
pleteness in a circle, they are evidently fol-
lowing some sort of law which insists on the
universe and all that it contains chasing its
tail with the enthusiasm of a sportive kitten
and the despair of a religious zealot who
ea n’t get rid of his fondness for beer. Still, the
idea is firmly planted in the universal mind
that there are turning-points of a sharp and
definite kind along every track, and that by
pausing on one of these, holding on to your
boots and saying your prayers, a lot of fine
resolutions may be made which can alter
the whole shape of the future. The choice
and particular time for these efforts after
righteousness is supposed to be the New
Year, and the effect is such that for quite
one-thirtieth of January people feel a new
cleanliness of purpose under their waistcoats
which suggests a genuine reduction in the
moral laundry bill. Of course, the year
isn’t any newer on January 1 than it is
on August 16, but that doesn’t matter to
the person bent on reform. . The calendar
says there is a break there, and a new num-
ber goes at the top of the letters we write,
so the alleged kink in time is sufficiently
obvious for practical purposes. That the
next New Year may hear the same resolu-
tions made with the same penitence for the
past and zeal for the future is nothing. A
new leaf is to be turned and a lily-white
desire breathes on its first flutter.
* *
The real crisis of the world may be con-
nected Avith astrology and assisted by a mil-
lennial conference of stars, but the astrono-
mer can’t connect up Avith them in his
grouping of weeks and months around the
acrobatics of the moon and sun. They come
noiselessly; they go without blast of trum-
pet ; and they may or may not be of import-
ance in the sum of things. Lincoln’s
proclamation which flung down the challenge
to the nigger-exploiting States was a large
and far-reaching noise; but the real crisis
which made it inevitable that South Caro-
lina should grip New Hampshire by the
throat was probably the almost-unnoticed
execution of John Brown. The Battle of
Hastings made a wild uproar in England,
but the fact that Robert the Devil fell in
love with a washerwoman was the real cause
of the Norman Conquest. The murder of
an Archduke in Bosnia and the subsequent
shriek of the German as he inserted his claws
in Belgium had something to do with the
present war; but an impoverished Prussian
family or. two with-notions of fighting out
an inheritance for some utterly useless
younger sons was probably responsible for a
lot more. In fact, Longfellow was pulling
his own leg when lie deprecated the tendency
to declare that “things are not what they
seem |; for lie was old enough in rhyme and
experience to know well that the out-
side look of the world is as tricky and decep-
tive. as the froth on ginger-beer and as little
to be trusted as the bartow-man who sells
Lb bananas for sixpence. If the world could
catch a real crisis, nail it down with dog-
spikes or something and make a lot of good
resolutions there and then, it might be worth
while ; but the trouble about all the alleged
'turning-points is that the more you look at
them the gentler curves they seem to assume.
Ancient Rome got a fresh start when
Brennus had retired with his plunder, and
every historian still points to that event as a
crisis in the history of man. But stand off
and look at the distant happening in the light
of other occurrences only a little less ancient.
What did Brennus and the whole Gallic in-
vasion amount to by the time Nero had
kissed the Vestal Virgins and lighted up
his pleasure-grounds with a few rows of
burning Christians? What did Rome matter,
anyhow, or Christopher Columbus or the
Athanasian Creed or the Bill of Rights or
the fall of Constantinople? Somebody else
was always doing something else at the time
that other people were doing a lot of different
things; and the general result was that the
world swept round the curve and didn’t turn
at any sharp angle from the path of its
alleged progress. It is, of course, a damnable
and dangerous doctrine that “nothing mat-
ters”; but it is a damnably difficult thing to
prove that anything in particular ever did
matter. Yet the great and abiding hope of
mankind is that something might matter and
could be made to matter; and in that hope
men reach out after the future with a
fistful of resolutions and an ecstatic look in
their eyes. It is that look which causes them
to be classed as men and grouped into sep-
arate pens from the unimaginative but
entirely well-meaning sheep and cows who
take the solar curve with a steady simplicity
of beef and mutton and never know that the
New Year has arrived.
* * * *
The charge was once solemnly laid
against the Early Christians that they had
■turned the world upside down. It seems to
have been just about the best thing that
eyer was said of them, though subsequent
events didn’t entirely bear out the charge.
To get a real heave on and turn the whole
concern entirely ovfer looks like an under-
taking that would be' in every way profit-
able and to jbe desired. That Australia fahes
the New Year wrong side up appears to
admit of no doubt. There are elements in
the make-up of this country and its people
that are.worth swelling epics and morning
hymns and evening prayers and more solid
cash than the whole world has to its
credit; and there are things foisted upon
us and tangled up with our lives that are
so base and futile and every way lamentable
that the wonder is how the national decency
we possess can exist under them. For the
unpleasant fact stands that the things we
ought to have no use for are on top of us
and that the real Australia —the Australia
that ought to be arising and shining and
awaking and getting a move on —is buried
underneath them. Clearly, the one burning
and desperate need of Australia is to be
turned upside down—to be set right end up.
The New Year, like every New Year that
went before it or like any other old time,
is full of opportunity; and it is not merely
up to somebody to do something but for
everybody to do a lot. If the new season
now supposed to open, as the skirts of a
more or less merry Christmas trail away,
can’t be fixed upon as the one special and
overshadowing crisis in our national his-
tory, at least it is evident to all who have
eyes to see that the times are critical
enough. Our machinery of government
weakens and goes to pieces ; our ideals are
mixed and uncertain ; the easy confidence in
our destiny and the future which marked
the years of piping peace is rudely shaken.
Small matters of self-interest are allowed
to outweigh the big national purposes which
we once saw forming; and the dangers
which beset us spring from smaller and
ever smaller causes. The war, which was
expected to weld us into one people united
in hope, sorrow and achievement, has only
tended to intensify our weaknesses. A riot
of mad finance passes for political effort;
a miserable clinging to office represents all
we have of statesmanship. We have left
undone the things that we ought to have
done and done a lot of things that must
be undone with loss and suffering; but
even now it cannot be fairly said that
there is no health in us. Australia is sound
enough at heart. What we want is revolu-
tion —not a revolution of blood and folly
with trimmings of plunder and tears, but
a complete upturning that will bring all
that is best to the top. The patience of the
Commonwealth is amazing; but it is not
to be counted to us for virtue. We want
the divine impatience that is- intolerant of
sham and fraud and firmly resolved to end
them. The situation is plain enough. If
we don’t end the shams and frauds, they
will end ns. The best New Year’s gift that
any Australian can offer to his country is an
honest and critical examination of its affairs.
Once that examination is made the rest
should come easy. Australia’s boot is big
enough once there is a firm resolve in the
brain behind it,
LABOR, THE I.W.W. AMD PALSIED
Parliaments.
Having passed for the period of the war and
six months afterwards a measure to sup-
press the 1.W.W., the Federal Government
can be relied upon to pose as the authors of
an Act by which the community will be
rescued from a sort of Red Terror. The
public can also be depended upon to discover
that for the uurposes the Ministry has in
view, or for any other serious purpose, this
Act is going to be as futile as a penn’orth
of peanuts. What it will succeed in doing
is to check in some measure a handful of
feather-headed individuals from the open
advocacy of crime—from yelling in public
places that the blood of the plutocrat should
be sprinkled over the landscape and the
abode of Fat reduced to ashes. What it
will not do is to interrupt even for a day
the spread of the particular doctrines and
industrial practices which the I.W.W. has
been in the habit of recommending. So far
as the criminal activities of individuals con-
nected with the I.W.W. are concerned, these
can be easily dealt with. But the Govern-
ment can by legislation of this kind no more
stop the adoption of the “go slow” policy or
the advocacy of the general strike than it can
change the direction of the wind. And it is
well within the bounds of truth to say that
the I.W.W. ideas on these subjects are enter-
tained more than favorably by a very large
number of Australian trades unionists. The
attempt that has been made to identify the
Labor movement throughout the Common-
wealth with the criminalities of the I.W.W.
is foolish. Murder and arson appeal to only a
handful of reckless individuals on about the
same level of intelligence as the inflamed citi-
zen who in times of industrial disturbance
can be heard recommending the execution of
trade-union secretaries. But the call to
“the class war,” to the universal slow-down
and the perpetration of industrial chaos is a
different proposition altogether. What are the
Australian Governments going to do about
that? Rant about “criminals” is in this
connection worthless. The spirit of revolt is
spreading fast. But the leading propagan-
dists have not been a few poor fools wno saw
blood and fire; they have been the political
tricksters and adventurers who for a score of
years have been leading the mass of the
peonle into a bog and have now nothing to
offer them except assurances that they want
to go on being the people’s leaders.
Labor has been fooled. Its platform is
ant-eaten from top to bottom. It started out
as an agency to secure legal, administrative
and economic reform. In a few directions it
succeeded —superficially, as a rule. But, as a
general thing, it has failed to do more than
create a mass of opportunity for fresh efforts
at reform —‘to make greater difficulties
for itself. Every Labor man knows it.
Every citiKen knows it. And ffs the
people did not know where the dopers
and juntas were steering, so many of them
are unable to see to-day the deeper pit
towards which they are being driven by the
sinister delusion that the way to salvation
is along the path of civic and industrial
anarchy. It is as useless to expect that
these conditions are even touched by upend-
ing the I.W.W. organisation as to imagine
that that upending will produce a period of
peace and goodwill and all the rest of it..
The truth about Australia is that as a
nation we are plunged in a welter of confused
policies, uncertain and timid, with the whole
superstructure of our legislation and the
entire fabric of our economic condition rest-
ing upon the perilous foundation of debt.
The party joss has been worshipped as if it
were divine. And now when the brute’s
hoofs cast a shadow over all our institu-
tions and the consequences of a prolonged
squalid scramble are being felt, and Parlia-
ments are mostly engaged in showing their
impotence, this paper at any rate is not sur-
prised that a fresh sort of folly should be
growing into a popular pastime. The
Bulletin long ago and many times ex-
plained what was coming. But it has no
sort of doubt that squashing the I.W.W.
represents little more as a measure of states-
manship than chasing a fly out of a butcher’s
shop. "It will no neither good nor harm ”
said Macdougall in the Senate, and that is
about the beginning and the end of it.
* * * *
Meanwhile, through Labor’s own inepti-
tude and the trifling of Parliaments, men
and women are being taught by the State
more than one of the lessons promulgated
by the I.W.W. It would be merely fatuous
for anyone to argue that during the past
couple of years Australian Governments
have not directly encouraged organised labor
to make war on the rest of the community
and incidentally upon itself. All through the
piece the attitude of the State has been that
of a bewildered onlooker miserably wringing
its hands, vainly trying to hold back the
rising tide by the desperate expedient of
handing out doles from a cashbox stuffed
with loan money—struggling by waste and
muddle to perpetuate a bogus prosperity
which was passed off as a substitute for the
outcome of those real nation-building reforms
that must yet come in Australia. The pro-
testations heard from many Labor platforms
repudiating the I.W.W. are genuine enough.
The Australian Labor movement has had no
place for the rabble which gravitated to an
organisation bringing to Australia the gospel
of violence engendered by deep social wrongs
and miseries in other countries. In its
genesis that movement was a repudiat'on of
nearly everything the I.W.W. stands for. It
still is. But it is impossible for Australians
not to observe that the identification of the
Labor movement with the hold-up and the
theory of industrial bash is becoming very
intimate. This is a position full of danger to
Labor as a political force ; and it is up to
Labor to realise that there are powerful
political influences at work which, rather
than ddter trades-unionists from abandon-
ing policies of sanity, would encourage
them to rush to destruction. If Labor
insists on giving the population addi-
tional daily evidence that in the indus-
trial arena it wants to stand for
frightfulnesss, and in the political field for
borrowing and loafing and bloating, then
there is going to be another earthquake and
quite a lot of the national crockery will be
broken. But the theories which thus threaten
the Labor party (and incidentally the C om-
mon wealth ) are not going to evaporate just
because the I.W.W. is ceasing to collect sub-
scriptions and hold seances for the study of
spontaneous combustion. It is the causes
which provoke men to listen to these theories
that have to be grappled with.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Repatriation by Talk.
Once again The Bulletin asks whether
there is anyone in the Commonwealth able
to explain h'ow much longef it will be before
this repatriation business consists of any-
thing bnt words. A fund has been created,
a committee appointed, a scheme put for-
ward for getting more funds, frequent con-
ferences held, a vast amount of printed
matter issued, and it all boils down to —
nothing ! Everyone appears to be in deadly
earnest and giving the matter close atten-
tion ; but anyone who wants to see the
machinery for the repatriation of the Aus-
tralian armies finds only a gust of wind.
While a number of people are talking about
the subject thousands of men are coming
back from the Front. Do these men come
within the ambit of any repatriation scheme
or do they not? If they do, why can they
not expeditiously know all about it? All
they can get is vague prattle about mil-
lions. The Federal Government was talk-
ing a few months ago about £10,000,000.
Now Hughes says this will probably be
inadequate. .Toe Cook’s view is that
£30,000,000 will be insufficient. But exactly
what they are all aiming at is concealed in
gloom. This is not good enough. A soldier
coming from the wars wants to know (1)

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
To the Limbo of the Past,
Where the years beyond recall
Lounge in stupor, came at last
This the bloodiest year of alb
Neko’s year from off his throne
Scuffled down to give him room ;
Tears the Goths had made their own
Veiled their brows in midnight doom.
Hun- and Spaniard-ridden years
Mouthing maledictions stood.
For his cloak was drenched with tears,
And his feet were trailing blood.
In his hands he held a screed
Telling of his ruthless sway :
Writ in red for Time to read
Ran the record of each day.
Here were sinking ships set down,
Here was told of greed and lust,
Here of ravaged farm and town
Wrecked and tumbled in the dust.
There it spoke of grim assault,
Winter-frost and famine-blight;
There of Heaven’s azure vault
Raining death throughout the night.
Here it told of blasted hope,
Ruined hearths and mercy fled ;
There of grassy hill and -slope
Pavemented with pallid dead.
Never yet, since Time began,
Had the monstrous choul of Fate
So be-mocked and humbled man,
So enthroned the fiend of Hate.
All the war-years, reading, cheered,
Circling in a scowling ring;
And with cries and gestures weird
Crowned the red newcomer king.
Down the limbo of the Past,
Through the long, grey ages mute.
Rang the news that man at last
Had out-bruted beast and brute.
Then arose the gracious years,
They that filled the golden age,
And their eyes were filled with tears.
And theii- heai-ts with noble rage.
“Shame,” they shouted, “deathless shame,
Shall be h i the ages down,
Whom exultantly ye name
King by right of red renown !
■“Trailing blood and drenched with tears,
Ye have named him front and first;
Reddest of the red war-years,
He shall stand a thing accursed !
“Nurselings in the dark shall tell
Of the woes his coming brought;
Men shall link his name with Hell,
■ Shuddering at the wrongs he wrought!”
Then the throned newcomer rose,
Anger dead, and in his eyes
Such a light as sometimes glows
After storm in sunset skies.
“Here and there,” he said, “behold”—
And his voice grew woman-soft —
“Certain lines are writ in gold,”
And he held his scroll aloft.
Then they thronged about his throne,
And they read with bated breath
Epic lines, wherein was shown
Liberty defying Death.
Never yet recording page
Showed such noble sacrifice ;
And they saw, and all their rage
Thinned and thawed away like ice.
There they read of heroes brave
Yielding fireside love and ease,
Life itself, that Man should have
Sweet redemption and release.
All the Golden Years grew glad,
Ranging in a glowing ring •
And, their hearts no longer sad,
Owned the great newcomer king.
Down the Limbo of the Past.
Lit by weird ana ancient lights,
Rang the news that man at last
Had attained to godly heights.

The Wasteful Use of Roosters’ Tails.
The accusation made against itself by the
N. S. Wales Government the other day of
an intention to lay violent hands on the
office of State Governor fell' as flat as the
beer that was spilled yesterday. No “Work-
ing Man” wrote to the Daily Telegraph
protesting against the severance of the bonds,
and not a single “Liberal Elector” bom-
barded the Herald with demands to know
whether the I.W.W. was in office. Nobody,
in short, troubled, because nobody believed
that the Ministry was in earnest over this
business. Yet the subject is one the com-
munity has a right to expect the numerous
Parliaments of Australia to approach with
sincerity. The country is _ simply infested
with Cocked Hats. There is no exctfse for
this. They are not wanted. They do nothing
that could not be done by some undecorated
person as a side-line. They are not taken
seriously, even by themselves. They live In
idleness. The yarn about their being con-
stitutional buffers or something is mostly
myth. They contribute nothing to the
national Wealth Or to the public Safety, And
they are horribly expensive. Heaveh knows
what they cost altogether, but the direct
mofietary charge for salaries ftnd repairs,
etc., ambuhts tb £71,000, of Which about
£lO,OOO is for etc. if the value of the
premises and offices occupied by these people
were taken into account and their free rail-
way transport brought into the sum, the
cost would probablv run, out at £BO,OOO, and
that is £l6OO a week, which is a lot of money
for Cocked Hats. When provided by
5,000,000 people it looks like evidence of
lunacy. No country on earth with a popula-
tion such as this ever paid anything within
coo-ee of what Australia gets rid of every
year for the support of Governors, Parlia-
ments, Speakers, Ushers of the Black Rod,
Lord Mayors and similar institutions. No
country could afford it. With a horribly
expensive war to pay for, Australia certainly
cannot.
The Alien Mob as a Pillar of War.
The ease with which the large paper army
of Roumania was pushed aside by the Ger-
man's seems to indicate that mere numerical
strength is not an important - factor even
in modern war. Roumanian soldiers,
hardened, by the shifts and chances of the
retreat, may come back and find their
punch, or the fighting Russian at their side
may stiffen them into something stronger
and more determined; but the fact that
their initial successes were so soon followed
by disaster, and that they could not even
maintain themselves in the strong places of
their own mountains, seems to indicate that
armies are what they prove themselves to
be and nothing more. Austrian armies,
first and last, appear to be poor business.
Backed by Germans, and with Germans to
take the brhnt of every attack, they may
just hold their oWh; but alone and unaided
they were no sort of match foi’ either Rus-
sians or Servians and, on equal terms, the
Italians can walk over them. The deduc-
tions to be made from such cases as those
of Austria and Roumania heap scorn on the
idea that one nation can arm another of
vast numerical strength but little resolution
to fight in its wars of aggression. Even
England, after centuries of rule in India,
could not arm the millions of that big, hot
dependency to fight in her quarrel. Certain
races of India can no doubt be .armed in
their thousands to do good service any-
where ; but not the teeming brown person of
that land as a whole. Where there is no
stomach for fight it would take a vast amount
of training and organisation to produce even
second-rate soldiers. Looking at the legions
in the field to day, the success of those that
have fought well can be traced directly to
one of two causes. Either they are in the
controversy with a keen desire to gain some
personal advantage, or there is a fine sense
of nationalism in the ranks. The common
soldier of most of the Austrian Empire has
nothing to gain by winning battles and noth-
ing serious to lose by defeat. Roumania’s
peasantry appear to be as yet unawakened.
On the other hand, Germany, with all its
crimes, is united and looks at things nation-
ally ; Russia, with her religion and patriot-
ism blended, moves and acts like one great
armed crusading church; and Bulgaria,
grown into a nation through the pains and
fears of the years of her Turkish bondage,
seems a very solid little proposition from
the military standpoint. More than that,
the Bulgar, like the Turk, has brigandage
in his blood and is as eager to gain some
soil in Macedonia as the Prussian was to
inherit the earth before the German Chan-
cellor flew his olive-tailed kite of loving
kindness. England and France are, of course,
urged mostly by a keen patriotism in which
self-preservation in the best sense counts a
lot. To talk, however, as some in London
did a few' months ago, of raising a force of
two million African niggers to settle the
German, is obviously absurd, just as it is
absurd to imagine somebody arming fifty
millions or so of unwilling Chinamen to con-
quer Australia or the United States or man-
kind. The dearest sort of soldier is the
one w r ho looks cheapest.
Red Tape at the Wicket.
The best excuse that Hughes could make
last week for levying income tax on a basis
which could qot be criticised because nobody
in the Federal Parliament understood it, was
that the method of calculation was “scientific,
mathematical, infinitesimal and equitable.”
He might just as well have said that it was
poetical, practical, incomprehensible and
simple. As the thing stands a citizen who
wants to know what his income tax is going
to be is faced with a big necessity for retir-
ing into a remote cellar with his arithmetic
and elaborate diagrams and a supply of
curves, squares, rhomboids and isosceles
triangles, and there in a vast silence proceed
to wrestle with multitudinous divisions and
multiplications and subtractions, keeping
throughout an eye on the decimal point,
because if he gets that in the wrong place
the whole thing will become a joke. This
paper has often wondered how the Roman
tradesman kept his books, but beyond sus-
pecting that a paddock was indispensable for
multiplying CCXIX by XXXVIII, has given
the subject no as a bad job. It has now
arrived at the conclusion that, by the time
the citizens of Australia have finished mak-
ing preliminary investigations into their lia-
bility for income tax, the yell to know why
paper is not manufactured on the premises
will be loud in the land. The whole business
has been absurdly complicated, and, so far as
The Bulletin is able to see, the only pos-
sible justification for the confused figuring
is that a host of clerks will have something
to do. The theory of graduation, which is
at the bottom of the system devised for cal-
culating the tax, is sound enough. In prac-
tice it is being carried to a point that makes
it merely a fetich of Red Tape. And the
consequence to the public is that not 1 per
cent, of the people or even a fractional num-
ber of members of Parliament could make
any rational explanation of the system fol-
lowed.
Ireland and the National Crisis.
From an Australian politician in khaki in
England:—
The attitude of Ireland is giving' good Liberal
opinion in the United Kingdom serious concern,
though it is difficult yet to say to what extent it is
going to affect Nationalist aspirations. That injury
has been, and is being, done by the insanity of the
extreme faction is" beyond doubt. The rebellion was
bad enough in all conscience. The heart-wrenching
cry it brought from John Redmond at a dinner
tendered to the Queensland Premier at Westminster
was sufficient evidence of that. Its evil results might,
however, have been lived down; for the best ele-
ments of Irish Nationalism repudiated it.' But now
the hemlock Is on the board again. Sir William
Robertson, Chief of the Imperial General Staff, is
demanding more men for the Front, and the question
of extending conscription to Ireland again conics
up. The problem of the great exempt area of Ireland
is serious, and its difficulties admittedly are great.
But in it lies Ireland’s opportunity. There is an
elemental injustice in the Irish people not bearing
their full share of the common burden —those who
have voluntarily taken it up have discharged their
obligations gloriously—and John Redmond’s pro-
nouncement at Waterford on October 6 did not dissi-
pate this contention. Home Rule, he declared in
effect, was safe if Ireland remained sane. But is
she remaining sane? I have long been a supporter
of Home Rule, have subscribed to its funds, have
spoken for Ireland on the platform, have Irish blood
in my veins. But lam doubtful of Ireland’s sanity
in her present attitude —doubtful and disappointed.
By taking up at the present juncture her full share
of the present heavy burden, legitimate Irish aspira-
tions might have been consolidated, friendships
strengthened, and the evil results of the -rebellion
largely scotched. In repudiating her obligations
in respect of national service she is not only robbing
herself of the full fruits of final victory, but is
alienating a fine body of Liberal support in the
United Kingdom that she can ill afford to lose.
I had opportunity the other day of talking with
men who have lent a steady support to Liberalism
and Home Rule since the days of - the advent of
CamPbEll-Bannehman. Frankly they were disap-
pointed, and they recognised that a substantial sec-
tion of English, Scotch and Welsh Liberalism will ■
withdraw its support from the Home Rule cause if
Ireland proves recreant to her obvious duty now.
The estimate of males in Ireland available for mili-
tary service varies from 570,000 to 600,000, and of
this total 105,000 have been contributed since
mobilisation: Ulster, 54,000; Leinster, 12,000 ; Con-
naught, 4000 ; Munster, 16,000; and Dublin, 19,000.
Figured on a population basis the percentages work
out thus: Ulster, 31.8; Leinster (including Dublin),
17.7; Munster, 11.7 ; Connaught, 4.9. It looks as if,
with tragic perverseness, Ireland is going to create
in the current crisis another Calvary for her dearest
aspiration.
William O’Brien, casting about with a vigorous
shillelagh, says: “The disaster is mainly due to
Mr. Redmond’s own failure to secure the Home Rule
settlement by consent, which he might easily have
had as the accompaniment of a united and victorious
recruiting campaign; to the miserable self-contradic-
tions in the attitude of Mr. Dillon and himself in
the first weeks after the rising; and above all, to
their attempts to thrust down the throat of the
country partition-proposals more hateful to all un-
bought Irish sentiment than Castlereagh’s Act of
Union, which, at least, left us a country.” If the
intricacies of the Irish situation continue to tangle
themselves so enthusisastically it will bring to
naught the wit, cunning and statecraft of the chosen ■
leaders, and it will need some power far better fitted,
both by temperament and inclination, for the con-
tinued management of the campaign for Home Rule,
and the unravelling of its complexities, than Liberal-
ism in the United Kingdom. And where, in British
polities to-day, is Home Rule likely to find a better
friend? Liberal adherence to Home Rule has not
in all cases been an uncomplaining devotion; it has
not always been beyond the impertinence of praise or
criticism; but it has been the most vital agency
in advancing the ideals of Nationalist Ireland to
within an ace of realisation.
“Witera” ; This is apropos the remark
made by Defence Minister Pearce some
time ago that “all things being equal, re-
turned soldiers should have preference” in
respect of jobs that might be going in the
Civil Service. After being discharged in
Australia, medically unfit, owing to getting
a bullet in the base of my spine, I travelled
to England with the intention of trying for
something in connection with the Forces —
something that would keep me near to where
things were happening. Being so obviously
a cripple, I found that the doors were shut
when applying at the Anzac Administrative
Headquarters, Horseferry-road; so I wan-
dered around to interview the High Commis-
sioner. After a wait of four weeks I was
given a job in the Pensions Department,
where I found six others employed. With
the exception of one, all were of military
age; and although doing the same work as
myself, i.e., examining pension applications,
all were paid more than I, the one returned
soldier among them. After grafting away
for a couple of months I pointed out that it
was scarcely just that I should be paid so
much less than the others. The answer was:
“The others have been employed by the
Military Authorities as clerks, and came
here from Horseferry-road when the
Egyptian Staff arrived.” I endeavored to
make a point of the fact that I, too, had
been employed by the Military Authorities,
from August, 1914, until February, 1916,
and had been smashed in the service; but
of course that didn’t matter. It was and
still is plainly a disqualification to have
been in the Army. As I am not a near
connection of Tyson’s, I had to dry up
my wail and wait. Next came word of the
Referendum on conscription, and though I
could do no'thing else than vote “Yes,” I
could not help thinking there was a. screw
loose somewhere. Australian money is being
paid out in hundreds here every week to a
large body of young exempted slackers.
There are scores of permanently disabled
Australians who are competent and willing
1 1 do the work in all of the Australian
Departments on this side, and their chance
of doing it Is —Buckley’s.
The poisonous plan adopted for the '‘settle-
ment” of the recent coal strike is having
immediate, effect. All over the country,
agreement or no agreement, workmen are
coming at their employers with demands for
higher wages or fewer hours. The formula
is the same in almost every case: “We. do
not want to injure you. If your costs in-
crease pass it on !” But it all comes in the
end to one man who can’t pass it on—the
producer who has to depend on the world’s
market for his living. The wheat farmer
in Australia has to compete with the wheat-
farmer in Russia; wherefore he can’t get a
farthing a bushel more than the competitive
price even though his own costs have been
forced up sixpence. All over Australia
farmers'are going out of wheat-cultivation;
and the movement will continue, in spite of
all the efforts to put returned soldiers on
the land. Hughes and Judge Edmunds sent
five thousand men back to the mines. At
the same time they drove another nail into
the coffin of five thousand men on the land.
Another beautiful result of our tangled
political system has just been exhibited in
Tasmania. A vessel trading along the coast,
where the- State law applies, ran on the
rocks in broad daylight in calm weather;
and a shipper of sheep, who had been re-
fused permission to get his cargo off the
vessel, claimed damages for its total loss.
Said his Honor Ewing :
I am not so sure that you have a claim for neg-
ligence. If the company had been carrying goods
to the, mainland and issued such a bill of lading it
would have been fined £lOO. Unfortunately the
legislation of Tasmania does not see fit to protect
shippers, and through wilful negligence on the part
of those concerned the company could drown every-
body on board and destroy all cargo, and there is
no remedy. There is a clause here that the com-
pany is not liable for any claim for negligence on
the part of the master. I do not see how, in the
face of this, you can sustain your contention.
Counsel for the gplaintiff arose hurriedly.
“I have gone through the bill of lading
very carefully, and must say that it is
one of the most shocking documents which
has ever come under my notice.” So,
because the Commonwealth law does not
apply, the shipper was not only nonsuited,
but had to pay all costs. That ought to.
mean one more vote for unification, anyway.

Manchester has decided to plant potatoes
in its public parks. As the spuds can’t go
into the ground until next March or April
and won’t be ripe until the late summer of
1917, this can hardly .be taken to signify
any current hunger. What the move, coupled
with some others which have been fore-
shadowed, does appear to mean is that Eng-
land is at last taking as few chances as pos-
sible. If al‘l goes well at the Front, the
potatoes will have a market value, and if all
goes wrong and the German develops new
attacks under sea, there will still be some-
thing to eat. The potato has been filling a
large place in the present war. Germany
appears to live mostly on the skins and
make synthetic rubber or some other explo-
sive out of the pulp ; and references to the
use of the tuber in England have grown
more frequent. It was the spud that for two
and a half centuries enabled Ireland to keep
her landlord fat without completely starving
her own family; and now it looks as if the
humble vegetable might prove the saviour
of Britain. At any rate, the planting of
parks with potatoes makes Britain more self-
contained, which not only helps the war to
a finish but lightens the national problems
that will come with the great silence after
the cannonade. If England had enough
potatoes to go round, she might possibly find
enough chemicals and manufactured articles
also, and the ashleaf kidney growing on the
grave of Cobden might stand for the best
thing left by the war.
In 1915 Sydney pastrycooks got an Arbitra-
tion award fixing wages as low as £2 Bs. per
week for male assistants. Snorting at this
injustice, they went on strike; but the
absence of meat-pies and sundries did not
hit the public hard enough, and the Union
caved in, entering into an agreement to
accept the award and pledging itself not to
make any application for a variation during
its currency. The Union really had some-
thing to complain about, because the lowest
rate was 4s. 6d. under the accepted standard
living wage. £2 12s. 6d., which has since been
raised to £2 14s. This increase of the stan-
dard was lately made the excuse for an appli-
cation to the Court that muffin-men’s wages
should be raised to that pinnacle; but as
there was nothing to be feared from such a
small union, the Court implacably reminded
it of its agreement, and said firmly that its
cake was dough. This sort of thing may he
all very right, but it would look a lot better
if it was not practised merely on the
small union with a genuine grievance, but
was equally applied to the strong organisa-
tions. On the experience of the past three
years our Industrial law isn’t the protection
of the weak, as it ought to be. It is, instead,
a huge bully, which is very stern indeed to
the small concern but a mere sycophant to
the powerful. And one can’t say much
worse about the administration of any law.

“5.0.5.” : Of Victoria’s men for the Front
approximately one soldier in five goes through
the V.D. camp at Langwarrin before depart-
ure. These are figures which reflect small
credit on the authorities who might control
the virus-distribution. To take a single
fact, one lady belonging to Mrs. Warren’s
profession has been responsible so far for no
less than S 2 cases at the V.D. camp; and
she is still pursuing her soldier with una-
bated zeal. Particulars like these are care-
fully elicited from the patients by the
military authorities, and records kept; yet it
would almost seem to be a waste of time,
since the State, which controls the source of
infection, seems to be too deeply concerned
about other things to bother about doing this
one. Yet, allowing six weeks at least, under
present treatment, before a recruit is fit to
return to the ranks, it will be seen how enor-
mous is the State’s responsibility in the
matter of retarding reinforcements.
The Two Machines.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Randolph Bedford makes ail end-of-the-
year audit—
This is an attempt to regard the warring political
parties dispassionately and as if the writer had no
politics; to look at parties as a geologist might
look at rocks for their differentiation. ,
The Liberal machine, provided with ample money
and almost destitute of enthusiasm, is like a steam-
less engine—a machine without drawing force. It
believes in abstracts, but without emotion; and in
concrete matters it begins well but fails to last the
distance.
Years ago as a Labor candidate for the House of
Representatives I found the power behind the Labor
machine —the thousands of nameless devotees who
worked for nothing, paid but by their own enthusi-
asm ; believing- in the ideals of Labor as a Turk
believes in Islam or an Early Christian believed in
the imminence of the Second Advent. It was the
spirit that made the Crusades, discovered continents
that were before but the wild surmises of a maniac ;
the spirit that made Revolutionary France, menaced
by the coalesced kings of Europe and the dynasties
of the woi'ld, repel musketeers with pikes, and march
to victory without boots, bloody-footed in their frail
coverings of straw. It was the soul in the machine
that made the machine; that, despite all the little
tyrannies of Jacks-in-office and the crudities of many
a leader, still keeps it in working order and inde-
pendent of any individual who dies or recants or
chooses to fall helpless by tine way.
On my. committee were all sorts —clerks, timber-
rtien, slaughtermen, grocers, small tradesmen of
thriving businesses. They had that talent for organ-
ising the best in every man which now we reluc-
tantly seek for in time of war. The man who could
paint signs bought his own paint and calico as often
as not and produced streamers of “Vote for Labor” at
his own cost; a tinsmith cut a stencil with a similar
legend, and after his own work of the day stayed up
half the night making the street-asphalt vocal with
his own hopes. Women workers left the children of
many families with one neighbor, cut the electorate
into sections of 50 houses, and worked each her
section to a standstill. And there was an old man
who could do nothing but ring a bell to advertise
meetings, and did so ring it that it became a habit
not easily suspended. I was defeated; but the man
who came three years later polled 17,000 votes at a
personal expenditure of £13. And he did it without
a newspaper to help ; all the journalism was on the
other side. Labor won because of efficient organisa-
tion, motored by a spirit that money cannot buy.
What man can imagine the Labor party making
tlie grossly ridiculous error of , the Liberals who
coalesced with the wreckage of the Holmanites
merely to gain another year of dishonorable life?
The Liberals had their enemies separated ; they had
as big-gun ammunition for an election all the
muddling of the Holman Government — the crazy
finance ; the criminal lunacy of the Norton
Griffiths agreement ; the wild extravagance of the
Holman borrowings ; the day-labor system made im-
possible by the forcing of supervisors and gangers
inside the unions ; the imbecile purchases of wheat,
oak, butter and chaff by Mr. Hall, and the losses
on the re-sale thereof. Here was sufficient to damn
any party, and even the Liberals saw it, fought with
it, made charges which gave work to a Royal Com-
mission and a Supreme Court; stated daily at the
top of its voice its disgust and contempt for the
men and the methods ; thanked God devoutly that it
was not as other men; coalesced with a Progressive
party which was to bring back" honest finance, honor-
able economy and pure government to the State.
The Liberal offsider, the Progressive party, too,
stumped the country in a campaign for its jdeals,
which were all that the Holman Government’s prac-
tice was not. And then at the first breath of a
danger that was probably non-existent, the very men
who had denounced the shocking Holman Government
joined them to the end of making such queer bed-
fellows as Hall and Fitzpatrick, Holman and
Fuller, Graham and James —Liberalism’s ammuni-
tion fired off before the battle, leaving them for
action but mere empty abuse of the -official Labor
party which will not cause the Labor machine to lose
one revolution or slip a cog.
For Liberalism to think that abuse can win a vote
is as ridiculous as applying for divorce after the con-
donation of adultery. And Liberalism has condoned
all—-waste, ruinous expenditure on public works, the
farces of the wheat, oats, chaff and butter deals, the
JfoifToN GUifpiths scandal, and the departmental
slackness that made the petrol charges possible. Per-
haps tlie Liberals signed away their future in pity for
theit stricken opponents; On the French principle
that to understand all is to forgive all. In this
case the Liberals understood their desire for office so
well that they were ready to forgive everything.
There is nothing Machiavellian about the Labor
machine; it is helped so much by the mistakes of
Liberalism that Liberalism defeats itself. In this
case it would appear that Liberalism has by its own
stupidity again been outwitted, outfought and out-
sailed. Anew party will grow, but it will take 15
years to organise to efficiency, and its efficiency de-
pends mostly on enthusiasm and belief.

The Year of War Ends in Words.
It was mostly a war of language last week,
and when Woodrow Wilson joined in there
was more noise than if all the munitions in
Europe had expired in one loud, simultaneous
bang. And as Wilson had to start explain-
ing himself, and his explanations made
everybody concerned more irascible than he
was previously, war began to look a
fairly placid thing by comparison with
peace. Why there should be all this heat
about another Note from Wilson is not ex-
plained by the adjectives that have been
thrown at the document. After all, it merely
illustrated what was perfectly well known
already—that he is a well-meaning peda-
gogue "with about as much tact as a stranded
whale. Meanwhile, the first phase of the peace
negotiations has been ended by Lloyd
George. Stripped of what was inevitable,
his speech in reply to the German Chancel-
lor amounts to a repudiation of the pro-
posal to discuss terms of peace which have
not been formulated, and an invitation to
the German to come down to bedrock and
say exactly what he wants to talk about.
How badly the German wants to knock off is
a mystery, but we can be certain that in this
conflict the discussion of peace will not be
postponed by the German or anyone else
until the moment of the last gasp. The
acceptance of definite terms insisted on by
the other side may be, but there is yet. no
evidence that either party is ready to swal-
low any leeks. Instead, the only olive
branches that are hanging round look like
clubs. And it is very likely that a smash
from one of them would so far do the Hun
more good than having a yarn with him
about knocking off. But of the deeper cur-
rents that are at work in this business, the
outside world knows little more than it did
about those which plunged the world into all
this trouble.
According to Millen,' talking in the
Senate, a worker at the Lithgow Small Arms
factory was recently twice assaulted by
fellow-employees because he would not “go
slow.” The offenders were each time fined
at the local police court, but they were still
retained at the factory. In reply Pearce
said that if the manager had dismissed them
the Government would have supported him.
Probably ; but the inference is that as they
weren’t dismissed none of the parties, ex-
cept the man who was assaulted, regard the
go-slow policy as of enough consequence to
make a fuss about.
Quiet little Brighton, where the long
Adelaide sea-beach merges into cliff, has had
two mysterious drowning cases in quick
succession. First, J. H. Luxmoore’s body
was found in the sea; he had lost his wife
•just before, and is supposed not to have
cared to survive her. The latest case is
that of John Boneh, sharebroker, whose
clothes were found on the beach one hot
day last week, and his body in the water
next morning. Aged 60, Itoach was one of
the senior members of the Stock Exchange
of Adelaide and a noted after-dinner
speaker.
Col. Price Weir gets the new post of
Public Service Commissioner for S. Aus.
He has come back after two years of war,
having commanded the 10th in Gallipoli,
and worked his way on as far as Pozierts.
The new Act begins with the new year, and
the new post has control of promotions in
the Civil Service, very much on Common-
wealth lines. In fact. S. Aus. claims to
have taken advantage of later experience to
improve slightly on the Commonwealth sys-
tem. But the Commissioner gets only £BOO
a year. Weir is 50, and has been in the
Land and Survey Department of his State
since he was 14.
Peace.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
‘Teace, peace,” in dulcet tones he cries,
Who launched the bolts of wrath.
And was it peace the Belgian knew
Who dared to cross his path?
Were slaughtered babes and ravished maids,
And honest burghers’ gore
The symbols of the peace he wants
That leaves him as before—
To build his ships and cast his guns
And set his plans of hate
Until his mad ambition dooms
More millions to their fate?
By ruined town and trampled field,
By blood of millions slain,
We swear no more the world shall feel
That blood-stained hand again!
Fight on, O Hohenzollern, fight,
You and your cursed tribe,
Till caught and caged like beast of prey,
You pay for those who died ;
The men you’ve slain, the homes you’ve
wrecked,
The lands you’ve soaked with gore—
To you the only peace we’ll grant
Is peace for evermore !
N.S.W.

“The Bunyip”-—the all-Australian panto.
-—duly aros'e at the Opera House, Sydney,
and looked its fairy godfathers, the Fullers,
squarely in the eye. The “hook” doesn’t
get the help it needs from the principals,
and the principals have fair ground for a
counter-claim against the book, which is
by Ella Airlie in collaboration with Nat
Phillips. As Phillips is also the producer
and Miss Airlie the musical directress of the
mostly-feminine orchestra, it is fair to
assume that the script got considerate treat-
ment when being cut to requirements. There
is a novelty in a female dame, but it doesn’t
work out well in this instance. The conso-
lation about the usual male “dame” is that
no woman could ever be like him. There
is an' agreeable" mellowness in the bonnet-
string humor of the best specialists in this
curious line that no born woman can hope
to equal—certainly no actress with a shrill
scolding voice. Daisy Merritt adds to that
handicap madly exaggerated costunjes that
amaze more than they amuse. Pearl Ladd,
as Fairy Princess Wattle Blossom, tempo-
rarily turned into a froglike Bunyip by the
evil enchantment of the Chief Gnome —a
Will-o’-the-Wisp-like character —has a good
deal of gracious wand-waving to do, and
round her surge various young women in
tights, very stagey station people, _ a pig-
tailed Chinaman who sings that he is going
back to his “honey” and a comic Jew all
the way from New York. Then there is a
pretty female swaggie in brown velveteen
breeches, and a savage, clay-daubed abori-
ginal who wails about his “Nulla-Nulla,”
seems greatly troubled about his color-color,
and makes some regretful reference in rag-
time to “another lovesick black.” The
spirit of Yankee ragtime seems to have

The most Australian thing in the show is

In the second act there is an expensive bush-

is well contrived.

With luck “The Cinema Star” shouldn’t'
wane at Svdney Majesty’s until the Raster
Cow and the J.C.W. Principal Boy arrive
together. It is well mounted, rarely runs
to talk, and the chorus people have so many
clothes to wear that they are kept busy
changing all the evening. The plot could be
imagined by any small boy and the dialogue
written by a junior flapper; but there is
the gratifying feeling that no expense has
been spared and that everyone concerned is
working overtime for the fun of the thing.
The second act flowers twice —first in
a quaint and striking black-and-white
Pierrette number, and secondly in a__ Hono-
lulu duet and chorus. There are 2o items
on the songandance list, and though none
of them are as remembersome as half a
dozen on the “Sp Long, Letty” programme
they go with a swing—all bar one. Minnie
Love with “A Broken Doll” has the record
slow sob-song, and with the help of a
chorus wrings the last tear out of a sac-

charine factory-made melody. The most
interesting item is the duet-dance episode
“Stop Thief” in the third act. That is by

* * * *
Florence Young, as Louise (“The Film
Princess”), chirps as clearly and confidently
as ever and looks as bright as her own dia-
monds. Some 18-carat frocks help the well-
established illusion that she is only making
her debut. Ethel Cadman wears her dresses
without distinction and sings correctly in
the disinterested manner of the concert
vocalist. Nellie Payne smiles and looks
decorative, and Mrs. George Lauri is admir-
able in a humorous flower-seller study in
the last act. Phil Smith, as the elderly
moralist who is decoyed into a sensational
film, is most successful when he is being
thrown about like a straw man by Holland,
the stuttering and infuriated stage manager.
Clyde Cook as the porter is a good dancing
comedian who can do amusing comedy falls;
his droopv, ’ drinky moustache makes the
third of that'brand seen on comedians in
Sydney within the last few months. Reg
Roberts is again a languid, singing, musical-
comedy lover threatened with middle-age.

“Peg o’ the Curls” is back once more at
Sydney Palace. One weakness of the cast
has been partially improved by A. L.
Pearce, who plays Christian Brent rather
better than his predecessor —which was
easy. Beatrice Yaldwin, as icy and mono-
syllabic as ever, is now a rather plumper
and more pleasing icicle. Cecil Brooking s
impersonation of the delightfully-idiotic bro-
ther is just the same bit of clever character-
isation ; and Gerald Henson makes an excel-
lent hero. The two thunderstorms are still
in good working order, though J. Hartley
Manners is either a poor dramatist to re-
quire two thunderstorms for one play or he
has got a political pull with the meteorologi-
cal department, and can have as many as he
likes. Peg’s infectious Irish laugh and her
quotations from her father will be on the
market till further notice.

When The Bulletin met “Just a Girl”
at Melbourne King’s last June, it diagnosed
it as a humorous bit of Family ’Erald
literature. The show, with none of its
brands altered, is now at Sydney Royal
demonstrating to the country holiday-makers
how any bush girl can get into the best
English society if she has only £2,000,000 in
her stocking besides a good climbing leg.
Esmeralda is the name of the girl with the
loaded hosiery, and when she goes to London
a lord who is much more presentable than
his cheques falls in love with her golden calf.
She flies back to Westralia on her wedding
morning when she finds out that she is not
loved for herself alone. But she generously
leaves behind her a marriage settlement of
£1,000,000, and all is over until the next
act, when the busted lord tells her that he
has sold all his estates in order to pay back
the £1,000,000 —or words to that effect.
Bert Bailey’s co. of honest Selectors do
their best with the quaintly absurd thing,
and the pintpot of melody is filled to the
brim.

tcrion on Friday night, when The Land ol
Promise” will be blotted off the map.
House of Glass” goes up on Saturday,
seems a bad time to build it with the Hud
in charge of Belgium and exports forbidden;
Sydney Tivoli, where "The Passing
Show” passes, has acquired Belle Oliver a
contralto who is worth listening to. (Jh
praise is louder than it looks in these days
when almost every performer puts up a bluff
that he or she can sing. A system f licens
ing will have to be adopted if the fraud
grows more popular.) Otherwise Sydney
James and his Strollers are offering an in-
teresting programme, and some of the
recruits ore worth their limelight.
The new Williamson annual loses no time
in stating a reason why it is called Ihe
House that Jack Built.” A brigade of pe-
cocious nippers and joyous young girleons
sit on forms confronting a flat and flinty
domicile and sing of the various animals and
persons associated with Jacks House m nai
serv rhyme. Meanwhile pictures of those
fabulous objects, drawn by Hop's hnaci-
studv 11., drop into view on flap-doors and the
Prologue ends in a further burst of melody.
As a key to subsequent proceedings it hardly
counts, though it serves to introduce an
alleged Cow with a Crumpled Horn, and the
youngsters mostly reappear m various out-
fits in a series of revue numbers, lhe snow
has a picturesque rivulet of poetic senti-
mentalism running through a meadow ol
revue. A Princess in disguise (Gretchen
Yates) comes along and engages herself as
lady-help on a farm, which is in a hopeless
state of insolvency until the Fairy Queen
takes the principal boy farm-hand (Hilda
Quiver) under her protection. During We
first act the she-boy hero is thwarted by a
malignant Prince Kegent lady and a Demon
who is clothed in motley and billed as the
Lord of Misrule. Under their direction the
Demon of Despair (Charles Albert) dan■•e»
attendance on Fairy Sunbeam (Maggie
Dickinson) and things are mixed with sad-
ness. Then the gallant she-boy discovers a
magic casket in the woodland haunt of the
ballet and becomes informally betrothed to
the royal dairymaid who takes over the cares
of government from the euchred Regent. The
rest is revue and frank irrelevancy. Gener-
ally speaking the Williamsonian annual is a
success in both of its moods. Compared with
last year’s production it was brilliantly
entertaining-even at the first performance,
and gave promise of growing more humorous
as the weeks roll on to Easter.
* * * *
Some of the scenic effects by Coleman
notably the Tulip Farm and the following
backgrounds for the ballet—arequite charm-
ing. Also the dresses are lavishy laid on,
and the girls and kiddies inside them reflect
credit on the land that gave them birth. Aiiy
young members of the chorus who have a
solo verse to sing—and none of the prin-
cipals save Amy Murphy as Fairy Queen
are entirely unsupported in their warble—‘do
it very well. This half-bred panto, is per-
haps stronger in massed talent than any of
its predecessors. Moreover, it has a full
allowance of genuine comedy and very little
of mere knockabout business. W. Fullbrook,
who plays a policeman and various other
people,, is a distinctly clever character actor.
In the item “Building a Chicken House he
makes a convincing old idiot. Harry Kox-
bury, as Sammy, is droll in a different style;
Langford, another newcomer, has at least an
elongated figure to recommend him; and
Stigant, the Dame of two years ago, conies
to light again as good as new or better.
Too many of the annual “dames” are cast
in the pessimistic mould, but Stigant’s cari-
cature of woman at her worst is. a light-
hearted extravagance. Miss Guiver, the
Principal Boy, has every qualification except
an incisive tone, and that is not badly missed
at a time when none of the latest panto, dit-
ties have much snap in them. Her mellow
voice doesn’t carry well enough to be called
fetching. Neither is Miss Yates, as the
principal girl, a very forcible songstress. Her
strong points are a slim and graceful, figure,
a dimpled smile with other facial fittings in
sw r eet accord, and a sense of humor. There
is a long time ahead for passing remarks on
“The House that Jack Built.” This notice
has already emphasised the fact that tne
result is something more than satisfactory,
and better than good enough.
The Christmas production at. Melbourne
Princess’s is “Richard III.” and its message
of war on earth, likewise illwill toward men.
is received with warm approval. There are
various reasons why Allan Wilkie’s picture
of Dick should be. the most popular in his
series of classic identities. -One of them
is that the character strikes everybody on
the same mental spot. It has the modern
Hun quality of thoroughness. The public
loves the good, earnest, pushful villain; he
excites open execration for his sins,
together with secret admiration- for his
moral audacity. Next to the Mephisto of
