“Faust” the famous Duke of Gloucester is
the most majestic evil-doer in poetic litera-
ture. Pie is a King of Crime; he wears a
jewelled crown of iniquity and a shining
breastplate of frightfulness, and Ins frown
spells sudden death. Wilkie produces the
version of “Richard III.” that Colley Cibbei
adapted- from Shakspeare and furnished
with a few ideas of his own. Starting with
a bit from the tail end of “Henry A 1.,
Cibber’s version makes the name-part easier
for the actor than even Shakspeare s did. it
rubs Richard’s wickedness in, so to -speak.
Which really means that this play is a trifle
more melodramatic than the Bard s undoc-
tored work; therefore managers naturally
prefer it. G. C. Miln of yore, the brainy but
barnstormy ex-parson from America, played
the Cibberised Gloucester, on the ground
that it shows him as an actual assassin, and
that when an audience is out for blood the
want should be supplied. The name-part and
the spectacular effects represent a full two-
thirds of the entertainment, whichever ver-
sion is produced ; so it may be said at once
that Wilkie’s cute and artistic staging of the
play is everything that was expected, and
more besides.
* * * *
The actor for his part is mostly as admir-
able as his make-up. He has one or two
tired moments when he seems to be repeat-
ing his lines from memory instead of utter-
ing them from a creative brain; but at his
best he is convincing, strong, magnetic. The
impersonation has a bite in it. It was born
“THE PASSING SHOW,” AT
SYDNEY TIVOLI.
Ballet of old gentlemen games, Desmond,
Le Brun and Graystone.
with teeth. Pictorially, Wilkie is quite as
effective as the oft-talked-of Barry Sullivan.
Personally he gives a more refined treatment
of the subject. He presents a happy medium
between the courtly, nervous Richard of
Henry Irving and the rough, declamatory
yulgarian of Sullivan, as this writer remem-
bers them. Like Irving, he is specially artis--
tic in his scene with Lady Anne, and his
manner of kissing the widow’s finger-tips
over the coffin of the late King Henry is a
fine climax to an amazing mash. With the
unobtrusive assistance of the lady he real-
ises the bare possibility of such a wooing.
Another fine piece of acting is his way of
turning dog on Buckingham; which suggests
that the natural brutality of the man has
overmastered the hypocrite in him. In the
first flush of monarchical power he is suffer-
ing from swelled head. Vivian Edwards
would be a likeable Buckingham if he put
more ducal dignity into his deportment and
speech; and Miss Hunter Watts as Anne
would do well to talk in subdued accents all
the time. Hers is a weak and worn voice
that needs tender usage. Fred Kehoe, on
the other hand, should put more hysterical
force into his railing at Richard. His bloodless
performance of Henry VI. is notable only
for its waste of opportunities. Miss Lorna
Forbes scores a valuable success as the tear-
ful Elizabeth, and Miss Leigh is passable as
the old Duchess. Whilst both the young
Princes speak their lines feelingly, one of
them, Dorothy Seacombe, is conspicuously
clever. All the same, their infantile distress
has no boyish character in it. They are just
girls dressed as brothers. Walter Hunt
makes a brisk, outspoken Richmond and a
respectable combatant in the last scene. Cos
grove is a slow, colorless Lord Stanley ; and
Dore Patey, Varna, Landor, Plinge and
Bellbridge speak up when required.
At Melbourne King’s the artless simplicity
of “The White Slave Traffic” is appealing to
all earnest followers of droll bellowdrama.
The Black Prince, who prowls around Manly
looking for victims,-, is still coming to a
violent end at the hands of Jim the Detec-
tive, whilst the other rescuer disposes of the
man with ice in his veins. Miss Kate
Howarde’s home-made play is often as good
as a pantomime in one sense of the word,
so the Christmas season should at least
carry it into a third week. Many a less
absurd piece of goods has been more tire-
some to the present writer.
“Damaged Goods” is expected to run until
January 5 at Melbourne Royal. Apparently
the city’s thirst for knowledge cannot be
satisfied before that date. Meantime the
audiences continue to have a large sprinkling
of lads and flappers who mostly listen to Dr.
Scanlan’s discourse with approving solemnity.
Some, however, venture a giggle here and
there, as if Brieux were guilty of attempting
a bit of comic relief.
A burlesque entitled “On Tour” was the
main suit of the St. Kilda (Vic.) Dandies
when The Bulletin last looked in on
their show. The piece, which pretended to
depict the trials of travelling theatricals,
brought out the finer side of J. Brennan’s
humor, and gave Messrs. Agnew, Campbell
and de Robeck a better opportunity to throw
themselves about. Marshall Lawrence, Ford
Waltham and the ladies made noises,
musical or the reverse as the situation de-
manded, whilst the band under the command
of maestro Speirs supplied the usual unob-
trusive support. In the first part the cus-
tomary concert was indulged in, and it only
needed a finale of tea and trifle to make this
bit of the entertainment feel like a polite
social evening at Geelong.
“Where Are My Children?” at -Melbourne
Auditorium, continues to enlighten tne igno-
rant on the source of some fashionable doc-
tors’ incomes. Dr. Malfit, the wittily-named
medico in the picture, evidently has a con-
siderable practice, and collects a heap of
dallahs from the creme de la creme of Noo
Yark sassiety. Apparently, howevpr, his
long record of successes makes him care-
less, for when he attends to a comparatively
humble patient he makes a mess of things
and celebrates the tragedy with 15 years’
hard labor. It is a terrible finish for the
eminent Malfit, and suggests once more that
even Daniel might have met with a nasty
accident if he had made a habit of having
breakfast in the lions’ den.
The headliner on Hoyt’s Christmas pro-
gramme in Melbourne is “The Lure of
Heart’s Desire,” a Metro film founded on
“The Spell of the Yukon.” written by the
Canadian poet Robert W. Service. Edmund
Breeze is the handsome apology for the out-
back tornado who is supposed to figure as
hero in this production. Another of Hoyt's
Christmas offerings is “The Forbidden Ad-
venture,” starring the much-advertised
Louise Glaum. Louise is known far and
wide as a vampire specialist, but she is also
a daring and original frocker who dresses
like the season after next.
The Follies continue their mild and aim-
less riot at Melbourne Tivoli. The well-
dressed show has been improved in tone
since this paper last saw it, but several
items are still wanting in finish, and much
of the business is too foolish for words. The
greatest shortcomings are found among the
comedians who do the old familiar fallabout
stunts, but, with the exception of Weems,
seem incapable of higher flights of humor.
Cannot scores best as a serious singer; as a
comic prattler he can be rarely forgiven.
His masterpiece of drollery is a long and
confused statement in stage Scotch, which
is one of the most trying features of a
rather dull programme.

aaued, joins in the daily “Important” adver-

Died, aged 70, Dr. Dunne, first R.C.
Bishop of Wilcannia. But he had scarcely
unpacked his mitre and his cope before the
rabbits ate the bottom out of Wilcannia, and
then he moved on to Broken Hill, where the
cathedral has camped for nearly 30 years.
Dr. Dunne, who had previously been admin-
istrator of the Goulburn diocese, was a
kindly old gentleman: he had broad sympa-
thies. and to the end of his days regretted
that he saw a joke only with great difficulty.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Toe Twiddler”: “Glen H.” (B. 7/12/’l6)
is the usual supercilious theatre critic. I
am no theorist or idealist, but merely one
with practical experience. In vaudeville it
is the piffle that interests. The direct act
(acrobatics especially) belongs to the circus
tent in the. estimation of most patrons; but
combine it with patter or piffle and they’ll
applaud it as a great vaudeville turn. In
the early ’9o’s, under Rickards’s manage-
ment, my brother and I were billed in “a
startling bicycle aerial trapeze act.” It
must have been a • startler, for at the end
of the first week newspaper critics, whilst
lauding the turns, implored the City Coun-
cil to compel us to use a net for the protec-
tion of ourselves and the stallites below,
who, at each performance, huddled to either
side. We were compelled to order a net,
and had to postpone the act until it was
made—at a cost to us of £35. Whilst wait-
ing we induced Rickards to let us
introduce our No. 2 turn under another
name. We thus became knockabout danc-
ing comedians. I had been an expert
from childhood, and my brother rapidly
learnt the tricks. We knew that it
would do no good showing direct as
dancers, so we clothed it with the piffle that
“Glen H.” objects to. It “went” and paid so
well that we cancelled our trapeze contract
and never used that act again. I’ve seen
hundreds of turns since,, and all have to
resort to the same trick. It’s a pity, I know,
but it’s the pay that talks loudest and the
disapproval of all the “Glen H.’s” won’t
reform the business.
“Dry Shave” : How the mob does flock to
the film with the suggestive title! ' The Un-
born”—ruled out in Sydney—is drawing six
full houses a day in Brisbane. “The Un-
born” is mostly seduction, abortion and
murder. Sobs are called for, but giggles
from flappers are all that happen. The
movie showmen who haven’t got it are on
tenterhooks lest Brother Stiggins should
wake up and demand a censor who has the
use of his eyes.

“0.K.”: Seeing that the late Marshall
Hall was a notoriously pro-German influ-
ence in Melbourne in days when he shouted
the philosophy of Nietzsche over restaurant
tables and scoffed at every product of
Britain, from its music to its muffins, com-
mon-sense suggests that the Conservatorium
teachers who are following in his tracks
should go a little out of their way to seem
as non-Germanised as possible. There are
other schools of music than the Teutonic, but
the followers of Marshall Hall are reluctant
to admit it. When Fritz Hart, director of
the Albert-street Conservatorium, wanted to
show off his star pupils in an unfamiliar
opera, he selected “Iphigenia in Aulis,” by
the late lamented Gliick, of Germany. Why
go to Germany for an obsolete old thing
when he might have revived, say, a charming
French work like “The Hermit’s Bell”?
“H. 0.”: It is always very beautiful to
note the forgiveness extended in melodrama
to a sinner who repenteth. In “The White
Slave Traffic,” now been carried on at Mel-
bourne King’s, the nice young man in the
last act marries a professional procuress who
only a few weeks previously had decoyed his
sister to the seraglio of a nigger potentate.
Of course the lady had repented of her evil
ways, but the young man’s good-nature seems
a trifle overdone.
“Private Boxer”: I did not assume, as
“Stargazer” (B. 7/12/’IG) appears to
imagine, that those who have adapted Aus-
tralian stories for the stage are pastmasters
in stage-craft. My suggestion was—and is
—that the original authors could make a
better job of it if they paid more attention
to technique. But the very word is anathema
to the despondent “Stargazer” and his bat-
racker “R. 8.” The former sniffs and men-
tions “cant”; the latter’s opinion is that
“stage technique should be classed with the
exploded superstitions.” Yet both seemingly
agree that the position of the Australian
playwright is hopeless. I prefer to think
that the native writer is clever enough to
overcome obstacles if he will but take more
time and trouble over his work.
“Ibsenshaw”: The British dramatists’
knowledge of Australia is not so extensive
as peculiar. A “psychical melodrama” in
one act by Zangwill was recently topping
the bill at the London Palladium. Title:
“The Moment Before.” A grey-haired duch-
ess noted for good deeds is stricken with
death while conversing with a bishop. In
the moment before dissolution the black epi-
sode in the lady’s past is lived over again
and shown on the stage. The scene is a
“gold-seeker’s” hut in North Queensland.
The boss’s name is plain John. John’s
missus is looking after the house, John being
away “seeking,” assisted by Ooralee, an abo.
Presently Harold, a stockman of the Never
Never Land, turns up and proceeds to make
passionate love to Mrs. John. The scene
is interrupted by John, who has rushed back
home to tell his missus that he has “struck
it rich.” The two men arrange to fight a
revolver duel, and while it is in progress the
lady takes a hand and shoots her John
dead. Her lover thereupon shoots the wit-
ness Ooralee. and the pair fly to England,
where Harold inherits a dukedom. Zangwill
says he will write some more Of these triie-
to-life one-acters if the first is successful. It
sounds almost like a blackmailing threat.

The dispute as to whether the pleasantry
known as “crucifixion” or “spreadeagling”
is still known to the British Army was
cleared up last week. The subject somehow
got into the House of Commons just as
The Bulletin’s “Old Soldier” and various
newer ones were telling each other that
they didn’t know what they were talking
about. every General, it was
explained, had reported against the abolition
of this method of. converting a man to
rectitude by tricing him up to a gun-waggon
wheel in the shape of a letter X. But you
shouldn’t call it “crucifixion,” it was added.
Call it “No. 1 field punishment” and it
won’t smell as rank. But call it what you
will, if it is like every other sort of cor-
poral punishment it is bad because it is a
class punishment. There are rich rogues
and blackguards; but if ever a rich rogue
and blackguard was flogged by the Law
The Bulletin hasn’t heard of it.

IF FOOD DISAGREES DRINK HOT
WATER.
When food lies like lead in the stomach,
and you have that uncomfortable, distended
feeling, it is because of insufficient blood
supply to the stomach, combined with acid
and food fermentation. In such cases try
the plan now followed in many hospitals
and advised by eminent specialists of taking
half a teaspoonful of bisurated. magnesia in
half a glass of water as hot as you can
comfortably drink it. The hot water draws
the blood to the stomach, and the bisurated
magnesia, as any physician or chemist can
tell you, instantly neutralises the acid and
stops the food fermentation. Try this
simple plan and you will be astonished at
the immediate feeling of relief and com-
fort that always follows the restoration
of the normal process of digestion. But be
sure you ask the chemist very distinctly for
bisurated magnesia, thus avoiding confusion
with the sulphates, oxides and citrates or
bismuth and magnesia mixtures which are
often unsuitable. Soldiers at the front and
travellers who are frequently obliged to take
hasty meals poorly prepared should always
take two or three five-grain tablets of
bisurated magnesia after meals to prevent
fermentation and neutralise the acid. — Advt.

CUPIN: “Hang the war! Another shaft
wasted! Every second chap I hit is
some fellow just off to'the Front.”
Billjim was a bit “squiffy” and when his
blanket roll fell over his head as he stooped
to pick up his hat, he got wild. “Aw.
the ——- blankets,” he said; “this is the
last war I’ll go to!”
Nothing can disturb Staff Headquarters.
It knows its job, and doesn’t allow a small
thing like a battle to interfere. Hence, while
an Australian Light Horse lot were chasing
Abdul over the desert, and were so buried
in transport and other troubles that all their
papers and records had been left far behind
at Base, these commands fell in upon them:
i.
Please state how many chaplains .you have in your
Brigade, stating Denominations and Seniority.
n.

“Chevron”: .Of the many stories of
Trooper Tommy Anzac, whom I love, I think
this best illustrates his humorous irreverence,
his willingness to help a man out of a hole,
and his instinctive impulse to be a sport. A
fine, fruity cleric, rising to his peroration
like a Murray cod to a grasshopper, observed
to his congregation of soldiers. “And what
did Moses say?” There was no reply from
his left- front, to which the question was
first addressed. To his direct front he
trumpeted once more, “And what did Moses
say?” Again no reply testified no business.
A' third time came the rhetorical query.
Dead silence. Then a taciturn Australian
galoot upheaved on the right front. “Oh,
well,” he drawled, while a slow grin spread
over his leathern dial, “I’ll be the blanky
mug. Wot did ’e say?”

“Billiam” : It was at Gallipoli and I was
gathering up the wounded when I came
upon a badly-injured captain singing “Lead,
Kindly Light.” It brought back to me in a
rush my early religious education, and a
hymn I used to hear. I tell you you can
feel pretty serious on a battlefield. To the
next wounded man I came to, I said, as I
was about to pick him up, “Come along, old
chap, Jesus knows all about our troubles.”
He looked at me, groaned, and answered:
“Well, He must have been workin’ overtime
lately.”

“R.N.L.”: The * Australian soldier in
France is not making much progress with
the language of the country. “Bon,” “no
bon” and “compree” make up the average
vocabulary. Being in urgent need of a
haircut I walked from the trenches one day
to the nearest town and discovered a barber
still plying his trade, although the Huns had
Commenced their “morning hate,” and the
civilians who “were seeing it through” were
fleeing wildly in all directions. I induced
Henri Gustave to make a start, and he was
getting along in a nervous fashion when a
Billjim ambled in and demanded coffee.
Henri looked desolate as I interpreted in
bad French. He waved hands and shrugged
shoulders and said “Non, non /” so I asked
Billjim' why he had come to, this particular
joint. “Well,” he said, “the bloke has a
sign up outside.” We trooped out to inves-
tigate, and Billjim solemnly pointed to a
board oh which was inscribed “Coiffeur.”
“Chevron”: It will be balm to the trucu-
lent soul of the writer of the Plain English
par. (B. 14/12/’l6) about Defence Minis-
ter Pearce’s confessed ignorance of the circs,
in which Australian soldiers travel in the
nigger compartments of Egyptian trains, to
know that Pearce is not going to be rude
about it. If it was expected that this inept
protector of the rights of Billjim was going
to make harsh remarks, that hope is per-
manently casualtied. Far from complaining,
the Minister praises and thanks them that
do despitefully use us. Haying learned that
the Egyptian railways carried free the gifts
of the Australian Comforts Fund, whatever
it did to the Australian soldier, he exudes
melted butter as follows: —
The distribution of gifts. .. .has been greatly facili-
tated by the liberal and courteous assistance
afforded by your department.... I desire to convey
to you [the secretary of the Egyptian State Rail-
ways] and to the responsible officers of your depart-
ment the thanks of the Commonwealth Government
for your helpful and public-spirited action in the
interests of the Australian soldiers.
No one will object to credit going where it
is due; but there is precious little due
to any administration which carries Bill-
jim’s “comforts” free but refuses to sell
Billjim himself a ticket to ride in anything
but an insanitary third- or nigger-class dog-
box.

“Tiaki” : In the S.M.’s Court, Wellington
(M.L.), recently the Yankee skipper of
the Florence Luckenbaek haled one of his
hands before the beak to see if he couldn’t
get some legal hazing dealt out. It
appears that the man, a quarter-master, had
allowed the stearing-gear to break while he
was at the wheel, and was disrated to the
fo’castle. From there he was spokesman for
the seamen, who wanted something eatable,
and, in answer, he was put in irons. At
court he was charged with disobeying lawful
commands, but a lawyer proved “no juris-
diction,” the alleged offence having been
committed outside the three-mile limit.
When the skipper found that there was no
gaoling to be done and the court was
cleared, he bawled to an officer, “Bring the
cuffs down, Mac, and I’ll have the
alongside in •no time. I’ll drag the
aboard by the neck. You’re my
mahn now, O’Brien. I’ll hev you in
irons an’- thur yew’ll stop till I get yew ta
New Yurk, you ” I never saw a man
look so much the picture of despair as did
that poor son of the sea. I rounded up the
orderly and several clerks, and we explained
that dragging a handcuffed man through
the streets was not looked upon with favor
in M.L. Then the lawyer appeared and
took the sailor to the station to get his
belongings. The rest of us made a tar-
paulin muster and quietly bolted O’Brien
aboard an upcountry train while a Yankee
skipper waited in vain for his “meat.” If
it had been put aboard we didn’t like its
chance of reaching Noo Yark alive.

“Castor” : Coming down the east coast a few
days ago, the collier Junee ran into a violent
thunderstorm, and immediately after one peal
of thunder something very large and mighty
hot lobbed_ into the sea about 40 yards dis-
tant with a roar like a six-inch gun hurling
destruction at the Ilunemy. The heat of
the missile must have been terrific, for a
cloud of steam (not spray) rose quite 30ft.
The second cook ventured the opinion that
it was a thunderbolt, and so far no one has
contradicted him.
“Yamba”: “Timi Piripi” (B. 14/12/’l6)
says “John Dunn has of course been modern-
ised into ‘John.’ ” I don't, agree with Timi.
I track our present word “John” —as indicat-
ing a bobby, peeler or a cop—in quite a
different direction. The old and fairly well-
known thieves’ rhyming-slang, of Cockney
London, is responsible. For instance, in
that lingo, “cop,” meaning a policeman, be
comes “John Hop.” A process of attrition,
due to sheer laziness, eliminates the “Hop”
part, and the policeman becomes just a
“John.” Many instances of similar muta-
tion may be found, e.g., the slang “On my
THE REASON.
SHE: “Why did Charlie fall out with you
after his wedding?”
HE: “It was I who advised him to get
married.”
own,” which became “On my. Pat Malone.”
is now rendered “On me Pat.” Similarly,
“Old Cheese and Kisses,” which indicated a
Cockney’s missus, became “Old Dutch
Cheese,” and finally, “Old Dutch,” concern-
ing whom Albert Chevalier has had a lot to
say. The Cockney who announced that he
had “nixey in his sky” was merely intimat-
ing that his sky-rocket, otherwise his pocket,
was empty.
ON DOGS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The British Board of Trade is considering how to
deal with useless, superfluous dogs because of the
waste of food in maintaining them.— Cable.
There may be a plague of hunger on the land
(Bring out your dogs!) ;
There are corn ships sinking to the slime and sand
(Bring out your dogs!);
All the pampered curs of Mayfair and Park Lane,
The Pekingese that wears a golden chain,
\nd the Pomeranian, and the Borzoi strain —-
Bring out your dogs!
The footman no more takes them for a walk ;
Bring out your dogs!
They shall hear no more the lazy ladies talk ;
Bring out your dogs!
No more card-case, nor a quilted coat of silk,
Nor lace handkerchiefs to wipe his mouth of milk ;
Nor the perfume sprayed upon his carcase whilk —
(Bring out your dogs!)—
“Whilk,” as I said, is doomed (“whilk” meaning
“which”)
(Bring out your l dogs!)—-
The flat-faced Japs, the pug, the Russian bitch;
Bring out your dogs !
No more the stately footman to Hyde Park
Shall lead out Fido when the night is dark ;
Nor hear that fatted cur attempt to bark.
Bring out your dogs!
No more debauched on beef-tea and champagne;
Bring out your dogs!
No more silk-coated from the winter’s rain ;
Bring out your dogs!
Dachshunds, no more beneath a London sky,
Looking like six dogs long, half a dog high,
Know Freetrade masters who love Germany.
Bring out your dogs!
At last the children come into their own—
Bring out your dogs!
Perverted love for curs that gnaw the bone.
Bring out your dogs !
Now dogs .must go ; the little London kids,
riendless and hungry, may make bolder bids,
Grateful that war them of their rival rids.
Bring out your dogs!
No more shall countesses achieve the mumps
(Bring out your dogs!)
By kissing Fido, Ponto, Tige or Dumps;
Bring out your dogs !
And the Dogs’ Cemetgry shall be lone,
Since duchesses shall raise no new headstone;
For defunct Fido hear Belgravia moan.
(Bring out your dogs !)
N'.S.W.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.

“Tiaki”: I think Annie Latham is the
most casual lady that Maoriland has met. In
1908 she married one llollier, a gun-barrel
grinder, at Birmingham (Eng.). After a
few years she tired of him and walked Fred-
erick Bradley to church. llollier presently
interviewed the new husband and explained
the position. It staggered Bradley. He
thought a. stretch of gaol might he hanging
out for him for bigamy, so, in his ignorance,
he paid a 10-pound note for the woman and
for her original owner’s silence. Then he lit
out with her for M.L. After a spell she jot
tired some more, and took one Templeton to
the altar. But the habit of change was upon
her, and she soon left him for another —one
Lyle. Templeton thereupon strode forth in
search of divorce, only to ascertain that
Bradley was ahead of him. Lyle now defies
Templeton to make him a co-re., arguing
that Annie is not his (Templeton’s) wife at
all. lie claims that if llollier is dead, Brad-
ley is the only one who could cite him as a
co-re. Bradley meanwhile has secured annul-
ment, and the only placid narty in the
general turbulence is the cause of it all.

“Bad Australian” from Buenos Aires :
Away back in the bad old times when I put in some
of the. best days of my life in a protracted but
steadily-losing fight against the “weird melancholy,”
Brer rabbit and a mortgage, I regarded the gum-
tree and its suckers as curses to be burned or blasted
off my bit of landscape with the least possible delay.
But I find that the poor benighted brown
denizen of J 3. America regards it as one of God's
best gifts to man. He plants it and nurtures it
with a great care, and he carefully collects such
leaves as it sheds from time to time. These, slowly
consumed in the embers of his brazier, he regards as
an infallible preventive of all kinds of disease except
poverty, and he goes so far as to assert that the
eucalyptified incense tends to soothe even that
scourge. In Argentina he uses an infusion of its
leaves in his camomile tea and mixes them with his
mustard-plaster. He sews them into his somewhat
insanitary chest-protector, and he regards the gom
mixed with a little oil or grease as a sure dividend-
payer in cases of sores or abrasions, and a sovereign
remedy for most stomach troubles. lie is only a
poor ignorant half-breed mostly, but his faith in the
eucalypt seems to be borne out by results. And
when his tree or . shrub strikes adversity and goes
out of business, he steps around to the nearest herb-
alist and is “soaked” at the rate of 4d. per pound
for a fresh supply of Iris universal panacea.
WHAT THE DEVIL MADE IT FOR.
BILLJIM: “And to think if it ’ain't been for
this war we might never have met!
Seems to me the bloomin' thing just
had to happen.”
Henry Holder, of Broken Hill, committed
suicide shortly after his wife’s body had been
found in a shaft at Stephens ureek. At
the inquest the wife of a soldier on active
service said she met Holder at a picnic,
when he represented himself as a single man.
She had gone for a drive with him, and at
other times she had been in his company in
the parks. “If she had known he was mar-
ried she would not have gone with him.”
Thank Heaven, there is at least one right-
thinking person in the community!

“Carfax” : The refusal of Justice Hodges
to grant leave to a woman to proceed upon
a writ issued against W. M. Hughes, claim-
ing £lOOO on a Melbourne property deal,
because defendant was “engaged upon
important public business” reminds me.
Over in Maoriland members of the Legisla-
ture cannot be sued for debt while Parlia-
ment is in session. The law was arranged
that way in the long ago to suit a big man
who owed cash to everybody. But it was
mighty convenient to others later on, includ-
ing one chronically impecunious Parliamen-
tarian who has since paid the last great
debt of all. When Parliament was in
recess his movements were wondrously
erratic; his whereabouts at any given
moment as uncertain as the weather. The
measures he took to throw the summons-
server off the trail while he negotiated a
journey to the barber’s or a pub or some-
where brought confusion to the constituents
who wanted to interview him about amend-
ing clause 4a, But when Parliament met,
lo ! that monumental form emerged into the
full glare of publicity, emancipated and
unafraid of the threatening butcher and the
emissary from a stricken baker. Decorated
with a silk hat it stalked majestically abroad,
while baffled creditors furiously gnashed any
teeth they happened to have. On the
last day of the session the air of independ-
ence and self-reliance fled. He began to look
furtively round corners and contemplate
short cuts. The flight from security on the
last night was generally a masterpiece of
strategy. He was a splendid ower and
used to declare that the law which every
year gave him a few months’ spell from sur-
prise parties was the finest piece < f demo-
cratic legislation ever placed upon the
statute book of any country on earth.
“F. Seedy”: When I read that General
Maxwell had been relieved of the Irish com-
mand, I knew that the British -Government
had no use for a man of tact. Maxwell is
a U tact. So is his wife. When they were
in Egypt Mrs. Max. told the Australian
officers that she and her husband would like
to come more into touch with the rank and
file generally. The r. and f. having no objec-
tion, the Maxwells ordered a spread in Cairo
for 8000 men in a large hall. When we
boys arrived we found trestle tables arrayed,
a go-as-you-please concert in full swing, and
the host and hostess “mixing” for all they
were worth. Then came the spread. Each
man had placed in front of him two most
appetising slices of bread and ’ jam and a
beautiful large mug of tea. And lest we
might swallow the mugs and thus do an
injury to ourselves, they were fastened to
the table.
“Carfax”: The gaoling ,of 40 stewards
who struck on the Canberra at Brisbane,
the sailing of the steamer without them, and
the great success with which the passengers
made their own beds, acquired food and
carried their handbags ashore may be tin l
dawn of a new day. Who desires 40
stewards? What most people who go on
hoard steamers want is to be discharged at
the other end, and they would be more
satisfied if they were left alone in the meari-
time. The system
ought to he altered
from top to bottom.
When a citizen pur-
chaseg a steamer
ticket inter - State,
anyway—he should be
handed a metal disc
entitling him to take
possession of a bunk.
If he wanted a sar-
dine for dinner he
could have one of his
own. That’s how
things are done on a
train. The business
could be. simplified by
the shipping com-
panies down to a
point that would cut
the price of transport
in halves. And the
price prevailing sim-
ply shrieks for treat-
ment with edged tools.
There are so many
stewards on steamers
now that by the time
a passenger has dis-.
covered one who will
get him a cup of tea
the voyage is over,
and all the others
have surrounded him
demanding to know
where his overcoat is
in order that they
may earn the price of six coats by lifting,it
on to the wharf and losing it in the wrong
cab.

“Billy T.” meets his Christmas drunk: —
Collins-street was choked with Christmas buyers,
when a motor-car came humming along, with a short,
podgy, brown-clad man hanging on behind, strain-
ing his brief legs to keep up. Noticing the crowd’s
excited interest the chauffeur pulled up at Swanston-
street. The man in brown leaned on the back of
the car. “There,” he said, “I won’t push the damn
thing a foot farther!”

“Do you like it?”

“Die Mihi” r Well done, Donald Charleson,
of Mooroorook (Vic.), “who disclaimed any
connection with Eureka”! As a perfervid
Australian, knowing my country’s history, I
think we ought to try to forget the Eureka
rebellion, which was a sordid and squalid
resistance to authority from pure love of
defiance of Government. Admitting that
Bentley the publican may have killed Scobie
the digger with a shovel for insolently de-
mantling drink at midnight, and even grant-
ing that Fletcher, Mclntyre and Weatherly
were therefore entitled to burn down
Bentley’s house, did that justify the insur-
gent diggers in arming against the authori-
ties? Gold by the law of Britain was of
old a “royal” metal; even upon sold land
it Wift claimable by the State. llow much
more, then, on the State’s own unalienated
territory! The gold did not belong to the
diggers. It was the property of the State
then as now. The State asked £1 10s. a
month, or £lB a year, for a license to pick
this gold up in slugs on the surface and
down to 12ft. by digging. The gold was
accessible and available to all, in immense
quantities, and surely 80s. a month was not
much to pay for the privilege of gathering
it in chunks and hunks. Besides, it was
a legitimate fee to form part of the Govern-
ment revenue, which was spent in protecting
the diggers and providing police and soldiers
to keep law and order. Contemporary infor-
mation indicates that the. diggers exaggerated
their grievance and were misled by agitators
of the type of Vern, the German commander-
in-chief at Eureka, and Peter Lalor, the
man who brought his grievance agin’ the
Government from distressful Ireland.
Hotham, the Governor, had proved himself a
remarkably brave and able naval commander,
and he commenced his career in Victoria by
a public profession of the. most ultra-demo-
cratic principles. When he reached Mel-
bourne lie found the Treasury empty and
that the public officials had been peculating
to the extent of several hundred thousand
pounds. Therefore revenue was needed. And,
on the whole, it was a pretty mean thing of
the diggers to refuse to. contribute when they
were on such a good wicket. So the Eureka
stockade riot stands on the same plane as
the refusal of the Boston Yankees to pay
taxes to the Home Government to be spent
in protecting them by police in their towns
and by soldiers on their frontiers. The far-
seeing American colonists saw a chance of
getting a lot of valuable land for nothing
by rebelling, and tliev rebelled. The diggers
saw a chance of getting a lot of gold for
nothiand they rebelled, too.
“Minute Gun”: The
piling up of the
packet Belmore on the
Macleay bar, N.S.W.
North Coast, recalls
another catastrophe.
A schooner beating up
to the entrance missed
stays and sagged in-
shore. At nightfall
her anchors carried
away and the heavy
. seas drove the frail
craft to her doom.
She crashed on the
sand with a jolt that
shook the very teeth
of her _ white-lipped
crew. Dimly the shore
could be seen under,
the lee, but nb assist-
ance was forthcoming,
so all hands took to
the rigging and clung
on for dear life. Some
prayed; all shouted
hoarsely . for help.
Heavy rain set in, and
it was icy cold, but
with the spirit of their
race these brave Brit-
ish seamen (mostly
called Johansen)
clung on. Just as the
first faint flush of
dawn spread the be-
numbed mate cried,
“What the ’ell’s that?
Joost listen.” Then a
gruff voice was heard
saying, “Why don’t
you Blokes come
ashore ?” The schooner
had been driven right
across the Spit, and
the beach was within easy wading distance.
It was a great procession.

“Y.Z.” : Re William Grant’s escapade in
walking out of Melbourne Gaol, the authori-
ties should by this time be impressed with the
futility of spiked-topped walls as a means of
preventing the man inside from getting out.
In at least half a dozen instances I can recall
the spikes have assisted prisoners. They are
a standing invitation to any criminal who
can get anything in the shape of a rope to
try his luck. Not very long ago one boy of
half a dozen who were playing cricket near
Pentridge went over the supposed impassable
stockade and recovered the ball.

PERTONAL

JOYFUL JACOB STUMM, M.H.R. (Q.).
The unfortunate Chidley, who has been
confined in Callan Park Asylum for some
weeks past, died unexpectedly last week. While
walking along the verandah he suddenly
threw up his hands and fell, death being
instantaneous. Probably the cause was an
undiscovered injury sustained when he threw
himself down a flight of stone stairs at the
reception house. The cult he fought to
establish is not to be allowed to die.
Chidley had many enthusiastic followers, and
one of them, at least, will put on the gar-
ments of a simple life and endeavor to
preach the glad doctrine of nudity. That
one is Shirlow, his chief supporter, who, by
the way, has a remarkable resemblance to
the founder. Shirlow also intends to
publish a book of Chidley memoirs.
Hughes’s new team ought to be called
the Sweet William Cabinet. Hughes,
Webster, Archibald, Bamford, Spence and
Laird Smith all bear the same name as the
male goat and the head of the Hohenzollern
tribe.
BILLJIM’S LITTLE JOKE.
When Victoria’s first gold (or thereabouts)
was discovered at Anderson’s Creek in 1851,
the then very-youthful Argus sent John de
Boos to write something about it. John saw
enough to make him throw up reporting and
go digging. In 1853 he thought he saw
something still better: pitching his tent at
Euroa he started a store to keep the Ovens
River diggers going. He has passed on. but
his widow still lives at Euroa and had her
99th birthday the other day.
Of the I.W.W. men who were sentenced
in Sydney lately to long terms of imprison-
ment Teen is married and has two children,
one of them only three months old. Glynn,
who was once a sergeant of police, but re-
cently editor of Direct Action, the I.W.W.
paper, and Larkin —a brother of “Jim,” of
Dublin" strike reputation—are also married,
and each has one child. Most of the men
were unskilled laborers, the majority of them
wharfies; but Larkin had worked as a sea-
man and docker, Grant as a ship painter
and docker, Besant as .an engineer, King
as printer and publisher of Direct Action,
and Hamilton as a miner. Fagin came from
Russia by way of the U.S.A., and brought
with him the latest stock of revolutionary
ideas. With a hard experience of Russia
and the industrial conditions of America,
he was the most bitter of all.
“Vulpes” concerning another White
Hope:—
Donald Mackinnon, who has taken on the job of
Chief Recruiter, made his early reputation as junior
to Higgins in the famous Federal Bank case. Higgins
in that dreary drama of Melbourne land boom spoke
for over 70 days from notes devilled by his plodding
assistant, and when the trial ended no eulogy was
more deserved than the compliment old Holroyd paid
to the mole-like industry and inexhaustible patience
that had built up such a wonderful fabric of figure
and fact. If he had continued in active practice
Mackinnon, with his talent for taking pains, might
have raised himself to High Court eminence. But he
lacked spur, and the Federal Bank affair was almost
his last appearance on the legal stage. In politics
he has been a pathetic disappointment. Not without
ideas and the gift of expression, he could never give
action to his carefully-nurtured thought. ' I remember
going to hear him speak at Prahran Town Hall during
a crisis in Cabbage Garden politics. He was then
the coming man. His reception was so enthusiastic
that Donald was flabbergasted by its fervor. Yet
before his two hours of philosophic platitudes had
ended every pub in Chapel-street was being over-
worked. AVhen Syme was fighting Tom Bent he
sent for Mackinnon to see if he had the makings of a
leader. Shrewd, keen-eyed David had the measure of
the eager candidate for the Premiership in five
minutes, and the job, too big for him, went to
Murray and Watt.
John Henry Cann, who, having to be
provided for when the coalition was formed
in N. S. Wales, has been pitchforked into
a Railway Gommissionership at £l2OO a
year, hasn’t had a day’s training for the
job. In the North of England he was a
coalminer, and, coming to Australia, fol-
lowed that calling in N. S. Wales until he
went to Broken Hill and got a place under-
ground there. He happened to be in office
in the -Miners’ Assoc, when the IS9I election
arrived, and was given the seat. He has
held it ever since, not because he has ever
done anything worth mentioning for the
miners, but because he is a pleasant chap
who knows how to avoid making enemies.
No man in the House is more suave; yet,
curiously, no member of the crude old Labor
party has taken less pains to educate him-
self, and his composition and his spelling
are still weird and wonderful.
Dead recently, Edward Pearson Ramsay,
LL.D., formerly curator of the Australian
Museum, Sydney, where he succeeded Gerard
Krefft. Ramsay was son of Dr. David Ram-
say, an old colonist, his mother being a
daughter of Simeon Lord, of Macquarie
Place, one of Sydney’s earliest and wealthiest
merchants. The elder Ramsay died in 1866,
the wife in 1889. Both' lie in a vault in a
private cemetery adjoining the Presbyterian
Church of St. David, Haberfield, the founda-
tion-stone of which was laid by the Duke of
Edinburgh. The suburb now known as
Haberfield Avas originally called Dob-
ro.vd. Away back in the dawn of last
cehtury Ensign Nicholas Bayley obtained a
grant of 480 acres, the Haberfield of modern
days. This passed to Simeon Lord, who
gavn it as a dowry to his daughter, Sarah
Ann, on her marriage in 1825 to Dr. Ramsay,
the father of the man who has just had a
funeral.
Cann’s removal to the N.S.W. Railway
office as a Commissioner leaves McGowen
the one member of the N. S. Wales Labor
party who has stuck to it throughout its
life. But there are several others of those
“ninety-oners” who have dropped out to
either come in again or join the Opposition
crowd: “Jupp” Gardiner, Joe Cook, J. B.
Nicholson, Arthur Rae (temporarily out
altogether), Edden, George Black and J. D.
Fitzgerald. Fitzgerald and McGowen were
members of the first committee of manage-
ment, which ran the party before it had
found its leader.
Lance Stirling, who has been President
of the S. Aus. Leg. Council for 15 years, has
not missed a sitting since he has held the
chair.
The Luke who happens to be Mayor of
Wellington is one of the few men in Maori-
land who are always spoken of in the
Yankee fashion: he is referred to by his
first Christian name and the initial of the
second. This precaution separates John P.
from about 125 other Lukes, cousin Jacks
to a man, and especially from his brother,
Charles M., a former Mayor and the pro-
prietor of a foundry and copious whiskers.
John P. is also in the foundry, but keeps
his foliage fairly well harvested. _ He has
been a strenuous battler in public affairs
for many years,
and at one time
was a Ward Lib-
eral in the House
of Reps. Being an
earnest Baptist
John P. is also
inclined to a
gentle, unostentati-
ous wowserism. He
was one of the first
engineers employed
in the Government
railway workshops
at Petone, is about
60 and has the
.honor of being hus-
band of a lady who
toils as hard for the
lads in khaki as
any of her sex this
side the river
Jordan.
J. P. LUKE,
Mayor of Welling-
ton ( M.L.).
Henare has been holding the largest tangi
that ever rippled along the Wanganui as a
send-off to Wikahi Tairapanga, of the
Ngatipamona tribe, the last of the big
chiefs on the river. Wikahi fought through
all the early Maori disturbances, and served
under Major Kemp in the historic brushes
at Moutoa and Waitotara. He also fought
with the pakeha against. Te Kooti, the
notorious rebel, whom many thought was
destined to rid Maoriland of the white man.
The old chief was 78 when he faded out of
the picture.

gust when a witness described how be
struck complainant with a lemonade bottle.
“Now, I ask you,” cut in defendant indig-
nantly, “what’d I be doin’ with a lemonade
bottle ?”

J. D. Andrews, captain of the P. and O. liner
Morea, who died the other day, was the son
of an Anglican canon in Adelaide. Andrew’s
was dodging his way to Australia in his ship
w’hen a weak heart put in a claim, and
he was buried at sea, off Gibraltar. He was
only 53.
Clifford Evershed, of the A.1.F., killed in
France, was the second son of Dr. Montague
F. Evershed, who has been Government
medical-officer at Bega (N.S.W.) for the
past 40 vears. Young Evershed, who was 84,
was in the Commercial Bank at Albury when
he enlisted last year. He was an old boy of
the King’s School, Parramatta, where he
learnt enough cricket and football to make
him one of the best all-round athletes in the
southern districts of the Ma State.
Captain W. E. Giblin, of the Army Medi-
cal Corps, who has won the Military Cross
as regimental surgeon of the Loyal North
Lancashires in France, is the 30-year-old son
of now-deceased N. F. Giblin, who managed
the Sydney office of the Bank of N. Z., and
was subsequently one of the Ma State’s
official assignees in bankruptcy. Young
Giblin was schooled at the C.E.G.S., North
Sydney, and took his medical degree at
Sydney Uni. a few years ago.
The first marriage celebrated by an R.C.
priest in Australasia was on April 30, 1803,
when Father Dixon, one of the Irish exiled
priests, united Henry Simpson, a shipwright,
to Catherine Rourke, of The Rocks, widow’.
“But the boys will be wanting something
to eat when they get to Gallipoli,” said Le
Joune, regimental cook of the 4th Light
Horse, to the pleading of certain officers
that he should remain with them in Egypt.
So he went away w'ith the horsemen that
were horsemen no longer, and was one of
that army of ready-made infantry which
won fame. Le Jeune, wdio was one of the
first to volunteer in Melbourne, was a chef
whose skill had been recognised at Parlia-
ment House. Under him the officers’ mess
of the 4th Light Horse became the Paris
House of Broadmeadow’S Camp, and in
Egypt those who had to stay behind could
hardly bear to let him go. But he jumped
from the frying-pan into the firing-line—or
as close to it as the field-kitchen could
come, and in Gallipoli a tent-fly would cover
both. On good days and bad days he was
as a tower of strength and an enduring
hash-mosque to the lads in the trenches —
until, on August 7, 1915, he was sitting
with five comrades, drinking tea, a Turk-
ish shell burst among them. Four were
wounded and two killed, Le Jeune being
struck in the legs and back. So ended his
service with the boys who “would be want-
ing something to eat.”
“W’EN ARE YER GOIN’ AWAY?”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I ’adn’t ten minutes enlisted,
W’en I bumps an old pot that I know,
An’ ’e collars me ’and,
An’ ’e ses: “Lad, it’s grand
F’r ter see all you young fellers go!
I’m too old f’r th’ fi’tin’,” ’e yabbers,
“Or I’d off to the trenches t’dav!”
Then ’e sighed, shook ’is head,
But bucked up an’ sed:
“An’ w’en are yer goin’ away?”
“W’en are yer goin’ away?”
It’s all that th’ civvies can say 4
W’ile they’re warmin’ their feet,
They’ll all smooge an’ repeat:
“Ow, w’en are yer goin’ away ?”
I went inter camp on the Monday,
An’ me name wasn’t dry on the book,
An’ me noo dungarees
Was still chafin’ me knees,'
W’en a pass on the Friday I took;
An’ I goes inter town in a moter—
I’d a feelin’ I wants to go gay—
An’ I meets an old cobber
’Oo screws at me clobber
With, “W’en are yer goin’ away ?”
“W’en are yer goin’ away?”
I knoo wot the blighter ’d say !
Me just ’nockulated,
An’ not vaccernated,
An’, “W’en are yer goin’ away?”
An' all through th’ weeks uv me trainin’,
Me musketry, drillin’, an’ that,
If I’d mooch up th’ street,
Ev’rv bloke that I’d meet
Would ’and me th’ same silly chat;
I’d buy ’em a pot or a shandy
(An’ they’d always allow me to pay),
An’ they'd sling me: “’Ere’s ’owl
Give ’im nothin’, the cow —
An’ w’en are yer goin’ away?”
“W’en are yer goin’ away?”
They’d kid to yer day aft&r day !
Fr'm th’ time I enlisted
The blokes still persisted
With, “W’en are yer goin’ away?”
Well, I lef’ all the cocktails be’ind me,
An’ 1 goes to me place in th’ scrap.
An’ I’m doin’ me bit,
W’en ole Fritz scores a ’it,
An’ I’m blanky near wiped off th’ map ;
An’ I’m back ’ere again convalescent,
An’ I waltz down the street th’ first day,
W’en up ’ops a bloke,
An’ th’ first words ’e spoke
Was: “W’en are yer goin’ away?”
Oh! “W’en are yer goin’ away?”
It’s all the tame maggies can say!
Though you’re chockful of lead,
An’ you’re damn nearly dead,
It’s, “W’en are yer goin’ away?”
QUEENSLAND.
NORMAN CAMPBELL.

It takes a lot of pushing to get an insur-
ance office on the move; but when it does
start it takes a lot to stop it. It is so diffi-
cult to show respectable figures for the*first
few years, especially if you are troubled with
a conscience; afterwards it is not exactly
difficult perhaps to show bad figures, but
with favorable conditions you can then easily
show attractive ones if you exercise reason-
able care and know something about organ-
isation. Yet, throwing in the undoubted
fact that Australia has been enjoying un-
usual prosperity (that it is largely of the
bogus variety which proceeds from whole-
sale borrowing doesn’t matter), this office’s
achievement is something it has good reason
to be proud of. And all the better reason
because, if we rule out the no-medical-exam-
ination policies, the society in getting busi-
ness has done nothing to which the most
respectable old insurance man can object.
As to the policies that are issued without
examination, the society seems as weii
pleased with them as with any. After 12
years’ experience the actuary reported that
“the ratio of actual to expected claims under
proposals so accepted has throughout not
exceeded the ratio of the total business.”
The immediate effect on the other side has
been to greatly reduce the proportion of
medical exes, to total outgoings. Here are
the figures :■ —-
Proportion of Medical Exes, to New Premiums.

It looks as though careful inquiry as to the
history and habits of a “case” can very
satisfactorily displace the ordinary medical
examination of which so many people have
a curious dread. It is quite likely that the
provision for doing without this examina-
tion brings an appreciable amount of
thoroughly good business to the office.
Though the National Mutual has always
been a strenuous business-getter, it has man-
aged to show a fine reduction in its expense
rate :—-

Of course this has been easier than it looks.
In the first place the interest earnings, which
now constitute very nearly a third of the
total income, have grown with the accumula-
tion of funds; and interest doesn’t cost
much to collect. But apart from that the
results are all right. Assuming that through-
out the old or renewal business cost 7 p.c. of
the premiums and the single-premium
(annuity) business cost 3 p.c., then the new
business has been got with the following-
outlay : —-

There are offices which show a lower figure
than 75.8 p.c.; but it is a good one, never-
theless. And it would probably be better
had the National Mutual not missed the
chance some years ago of establishing an
Industrial branch to feed the Ordinary busi-
ness. How much better this expense rate
will grow depends very largely upon circum-
stances beyond the society’s control. Dur-
ing the past 10 years Australia has spent
something like £130.000,000 of borrowed
money, apai-t from what the war has been
costing the Commonwealth; and a small
community like ours can’t do that without
feeling that it can well afford to take a
few pounds more of life-insurance. Unless
most of the prophets are all wrong there will
not be any £180,000,000 of money for the
States to borrow in the next 30 years. On
the contrary, there will be a huge interest
bill to be paid out of taxation ; and that is
going to have.a serious effect, not only on
the available amount of new business, but
on renewals as well. Incidentally, bonuses
will be affected bv the large payments which
this in common with other offices must, make
to meet claims in respect of soldiers’ lives.
While the total income for the past year
was only 19 p.c. greater than for 1913, the
claims were no less than 49 p.c. greater.
On the other hand, while the war has
largely increased the claims, it has slightly
improved the interest earnings

The total funds as at Sept. 30 were
£10,127,573, plus an investment fluctuation
reserve of £76,724; and ss. p.c. improvement
on that lot means about £25,500 a year—an
improvement which is going to last when the
death claims through the war have become
infinitesimal. Still, that is not going to
be anything like a complete set-off, because
with the extra earnings there will be
extra taxation. In 1914 the society paid
£18,024 in taxes. Last year the total was
and we have not reached the maxi-
mum yet by any means. Some relief will
no doubt be got through the investment of
most of the new money in Commonwealth
loans which are practically free from taxes.
But that relief only throws a larger propor-
tionate burden upon the other investments.

The movement, generally, has been towards
greater security for the policy-holders. The
investment in mortgages is, however, still
very heavy; and how they are going to pan
out depends mostly upon the skill and care
with which the money has been placed. 8o
far as probing here and there enables one to
say, the loans have beeli made always or
almost always on properties which are
assuredly productive. They may shrink in
capital value; but in that connection an
insurance office such as this is excellently
placed inasmuch as it can afford to wait for
the recovery which on past Australian
experience is inevitable.

This paper doesn’t know how the Inter-
colonial did it in the past year. All the light
the directors throw upon the matter is this:
“The conservative policy of the co. in past
years is now standing it in good stead, and,
though sales are not as active as formerly,
the income from other sources has been main-
tained.” The co., though it has been man-
aged with quite unusual skill, couldn’t have
done so remarkably well if ciremnstaffees had
not favored it. Increasing taxation on land
values made the holders of suburban estates
anxious to sell at reasonable prices. The
graduated tax made the impost on a big pad-
dock a serious matter; and the price was
affected in consequence. But the small allot-
ment, held by a man who had no other land
to speak of, escaped taxation altogether; and
so its price was not affected. Thus the
graduated taxation played into the hands of
the subdivisionist. And, of course, the bor-
rowing boom, which has for some years past
created artificial values, played still more.
Hence we find that while the average profits
of this co. for the three years to 1906 were
under £BOOO,. for the past three years they
have averaged nearly £32,000.
The co., while making these glittering-
profits, adopted a policy which, as it very
rightly says, is calculated to help it now.
Instead of paying out large cash bonuses it
kept the bulk of the money in the business
by issuing bonus shares. Therefore it has
never had to do much borrowing. To-day it
owes only £38.995, while its assets consist
of freeholds £56,141, borrowers’ and pur-
chasers’ balances (after deducting suspense
accounts) £225.629, cash and Commonwealth
war loan £18,503, and a few sundries, mak-
ing up a total of £300,756. The freeholds
include fine city office property; so either
the co. has precious few subdivision remnants
on hand, or they have been written down to
next to nothing. Probably some of these
severely-written-down remnants are being got
rid of, and that is where part of the profit
is now coming from. But the profit from
rhe old uncompleted business must tail off;
and the prospect of much new business is
sick. The reserves are, however, so substan-
tial that, assuming that there will be no
losses of principal to speak of (which seems
a very fair assumption), the co. need only
earn 5 p.c. net on its funds to show an 8 p.c.
dividend on its capital.

*Goodwill ( £9500) and other “paper” not counted

It used to be a proprietary co.; and pref.
shares were issued to get in some capital.
In those days part of the goodwill was
represented by ordinaries —£6000 worth.
The pref. shares stopped at 8 p.c. Then
the ordinaries agreed to let all rank alike
for dividend in return for 1200 more £1
shares. That is the £l2OO which appears
. high up in the table. It was at the same
time stipulated that the ordinary or as it
was then called deferred capital should
cease to be “deferred” when £6OOO stood
to thq credit of the reserve fund; and as
this amount has arrived, all shares now
rank alike in all respects.
The co. started in Melbourne, but pre-
sently found that it had better put up a
plant and keep a stock in Sydney as well.
Hence considerable changes in the assets: —
1910, 1914. 1915. 1916.
Premises .. £4,604 £11,184 £11,252 £11,645
Plant.. .. 10,244 14,193 13,498 13,369
Stock.. .. 6,833 11,166 10,756 20,381
Book debts . . 6,029 8,056 7,694 9,151
Let’s put it this way. In the six years tfie
co. has increased its solid assets (that is,
assets apart from goodwill) by £25,844; and
of that £8270 is represented by new capital
and £10,657 by new debt, making a total
of £19,127. That leaves £6917 to represent
the improvement in the co.’s position in
six years—profit, that is to say, made and
not divided. But that proposition depends
upon the valuation of the stock, machinery
and other assets. If they have been taken
at cost (or under where they couldn’t be
realised at cost) the position is as stated.
On that record the little co. looks very good.
It will have its set-backs, no doubt; and
probably one isn’t far off. Its sales depend
largely oh the volume of the general retail
business of the country; and there is going
to be a serious drop there before long.

This co. has a fine bunkering connection and
is the most prosperous of the N. S. Wales
South Coast coal propositions. Besides
having the coal and a very large area to
draw it. from, it has the right people behind
it. No doubt because of these things itffiad
. a great vogue in the share market. People
saw that very, sweet share bonuses had been
distributed, and, as in the case of Sydney
Ferries and Sydney Daily Telegraph, some
of them expected that sort of thing to go
on for ever. What these people didn’t
notice was that exes, were increasing tre-
mendously, and that with every bonus addi-
tion to the capital the usual dividend called
foi; more money. Had the old 12| p.c.
been paid in the last couple of years,
there would have been practically nothing
for formal reserves. These reserves, by the
way, include insurance funds.
Still, in spite of much higher costs, Bel-
lambi continues a very profitable concern.
True, what the actual profits of a coal-
mining co. are it is very hard to say. Most
of such cos. put by little or nothing to P lo '
vide for return of capital represented by
their wasting assets, and usually the divi-
dend isn’t a true dividend at all. In this
case, though, there is a nice bit over and
above the dividend. Look at this :?=-

In the six years since 1910 the liquid assets
have increased by £86,202 and the liabilities
by £19,305, making the growth of liquid
assets £66,897. In addition a lot of money
—this paper can’t even guess how much*— *
has been spent on plant, etc. For the past
two years there has been a net improvement
of very nearly £35,000, although the sale of
one of the co.’s colliers may be responsible
for some of that. After making every reason-
able deduction it is doubtful whether any
other Australian coal co. can show better
figures. The net liquid assets now repre-
sent just upon half the capital (£400.000) ;
they consist almost entirely of high-class
public securities, mostly Commonwealth and
State bonds.

This co., besides carrying on its ordinary
business, has been a sort of old bone mer-
chant. As derelicts of the Melbourne land
boom have got tired of winding themselves
up, this co. has bought what remained of
them and in some cases issued capital to
do it with. Hence the rapid growth of
figures in this table :
1900. 1914. 1915. 1910.
Permanent capital £72,741. £115,046 1115,046 £115,046
Investing capital 28,003 10,344 1,989 1.548
Deposits, etc. . . 74,334 -435,809 380,546 346,207
Advances, etc. . . 89,345 542,427 493,585 458,523
Foreclosed proper-
ties . . . . 49,935 1,358 1,684 863
Other freeholds . . 44,478 103,666 97,190 .103,923
The co. has strong people behind it, including
the National Mutual Life office, which is the
biggest shareholder and is well represented
on the Board ; so it has no difficulty in get-
ting what money it wants. It is still open to
do ordinary building society business, of
course, and no doubt its connection with the
National Mutual is a big help to it in that
connection ; but the disposal of the old bones
has been a very important part of its opera-
tions so far. The heavy State borrowing of
the last few years has largely increased
property values, and the co. has seized the
opportunity to get rid of practically all its
old foreclosures. Meantime, while no doubt
it has been making some new advances, it has
in two years got in nearly £100,000: and that
ought to put it in an excellent position to
meet the slump in values which will probably
come presently. The big trouble that struck
the Melbourne building societies in the
’nineties was that they had so many new
advances, on which very few repayments had
been made. It won’t be so this time.
As to the future, there doesn’t appear to be
any prospect of big building-society profits,
because State banks, etc., keep rates of inter-
est down on advances for house-purchase pur-
poses. But the co. has a big reserve; and,
assuming that this won’t be badly cut into to
make losses of principal good, it ought to
make a reasonable dividend of 7 or 8 p.c.
easy. Capital and reserves combined have
to earn only 4A p.c. to yield enough to pay
an 8 p.c. div. ; and considering the size of
the business the management charges are
extremely modest, which, of course, is another
great help in poor times.

Water Boards, Ardlethan Mines, graziers, etc., for
precipitating impurities and clarifying water in
reservoirs, dams, etc. One pound clarifies 2000/3000
gallons of the most turbid water within three hours.
It is exceedingly cheap, and harmless for either

Australia. Water is a valuable asset in summer

clear and crystalline ? Sole Agents: Wm. Adams & Co.,

From June to September the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yards
of road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. per cub.
yard, and the cost of the spalls per cub. yard aver-
aged 2s. 4d., making a total cost of 4s. lOd. per cub.
yard, broken and spread. Their last contract under
"the handbreaking system amounted to 6s. 3d. per
cub. yard stacked on the roadside, and spreading Is.;
total, 7s. 3d., as against 4s. lOd. — a saving of 2s. id.
per cub. yard. This result was obtained by using a
Bigelow 16in. x 9in. Patent Stonecrusher, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these breakers.— Advt. [2

Babcock and Wilcox Water-tube Boiler, so univer-

sidered, is due, no doubt, to the splitting up of the
water into 4in. tubes, which permit of rapid circula-

lin. plates used in the Shell Boiler being done away
with. — Advt.

Let’s see how a Mont de Piete feels after
over two years of war
0ct.,’13. 0ct.,’16.
Liabilities (Deposits, etc.). £19,349 £20,635
Advances and stock . . 33,107 35,889
Other property . . . . 6,020 7,238
The gross interest earnings and profit on
sales have gone up from £10,793 to £12,163
—that is, from 32 p.c.. to 34 p.c. per annum
calculated on the value of advances and stock
alone —but interest paid and exes, have
gone from £7747 to £9073, which means a
rise from 23 to 25 p.c., calculated also on
the value of the advances and stock alone.
So it doesn’t look as though uncle —this sort
of uncle, anyway—is making very much out
of the war. It is costing him 2 p.c. more,
calculated on his outstanding loans and
pledges, to run his business; and on the
same basis he is getting 2 p.c. more. But
as his turnover is a bit better, he can, while
cutting the same crust for himself, put a
rather bigger slice away for the rats.
N. S. Wales Mines Department has been
inquiring into a recent report that an old
bore in the Grafton district showed indica-
tions of mineral oil. The bore was put
down to 3698 ft. under Government autho-
rity, and abandoned 14 years ago. An
inspector recently visited it and took
samples of gas, water and sediment from it.
“These,” says the Under-Sec., “were ex-
haustively tested by the Departmental anal-
yst, who reports absolutely no trace of oil
in them. The gas was found on analysis to
consist of methane, or marsh gas, which,
while it forms the principal constituent of
natural gas, is also freely discharged from
coal and coaly strata where no oil exists.
Apropos methane, Government Geologist
Game says :—-
It mig-ht be pointed out that whilst natural gas
and oil are intimately associated in oil-fields, there
are innumerable occurrences of natural gas without
such an association. The gas discharged from coal
and coaly strata is formed of a considerable propor-
tion of the same hydrocarbon-methane or marsh gas
—constituting the greater proportion of natural gas.
It is, moreover, a common product of decomposition
of vegetation buried under mud and water —especi-
ally in stagnant pools.
This emphatic declaration with regard to
methane is of importance because it has
been represented that the alleged presence
of this gas at Robe (S.A.) is a sure indica-
tion that there is mineral oil in the neigh-
borhood.

“W.A.M.” : Some .time ago when I wrote
to The Bulletin and complained that
because a dead friend had taken £lOO of in-
scribed Stock, instead of bonds, in the War
Loan, his executor would have to pay £lB
legal expenses to obtain it, “Six-and-Eight”
suggested that someone had pulled my leg.
Although my lawyer’s anticipations were not
realised, the bill was fairly heavy. The
letters of administration have now been
granted by Mr. Justice Chubb, in Chambers,
and here are particulars of costs: —
Advertisements . . ..£22 6
Certificate of death .. .. 2 0
Our costs . . . . ..550
Brisbane agent’s costs . . 7 15 6
£l5 5 0
It is up to the Federal Treasurer to see that
in the next War Loan Act provision is
made, as in the Savings Banks, for small
amounts to be obtainable by an executor
making a statutory declaration.
* * * *
The Wild Cat sees one of its friends off:
Died on Christmas morn John W. Tilley, one
of the most thorough among the younger generation
of Australian mining engineers. After getting through
hig course at Sydney Uni. he had practical experi-
ence at Charters Towers, Mt. Morgan, Broken Hill
and Cobar, and he did also much mine-valuing-.
The Bulletin had the utmost confidence in his
judgment —his uprightness and honesty were unim-
peachable—and it was he who put and kept this
paper on the right track with regard to the Ardlethan
tin field and the Bullfinch group. Having specialised
in tin, Jack Tilley was sent by an English co. to
an Upper Nigerian tin proposition. There he con-
tracted malaria, and was in London recuperating
when the war broke out. Finding it impossible to
return to the mines, he later on came back to Aus-
tralia, where he put up a splendid fight against the
disease. But it beat him in the end. He was
married, and the son of William Tilley, of the
Sydney Stock Exchange.
* * * *
Sons of Gwalia’s efforts to find a new
mine in Westralia have so far been unsuc-
cessful. For months past a number of pros-
pectors have been fossicking on its behalf
in the vicinity of Mount Lucky, near Laver-
ton. but as results have fallen far short; of
local prophecies, preparations are being-
made to go elsewhere.
Lancefield (W.A.) is at last paying good
dividends. For years money was poured
into its plant and workings, and sharehold-
ers were assured at every annual meeting by
Govett, Hoover and others that everything
was going to be all right. But everything
continued to turn out all wrong, and after
several reconstructions the bailiff took pos-
session. One of the biggest creditors, a local
wood-fuel co., bought it, and finding that the
mining and treatment of refractory ore was
not in its line sold to a small syndicate of
mining men headed by Frank Moss and
George Ridgway. Profits were earned
almost from the jump, and now amount to a
few thousands a month.
* * * *
Westralian tributors are clamoring for a
law to protect them from owners. At pre-
sent as much as 40 p.c. of the gross yield
is being exacted, and where there are no
agreements the owners, principally cos., are
said to be always lying in wait for the
tributor to strike something good and then
push him out and work it themselves. In
many cases tribute parties have to fork out
Is. 6d. per week per man in advance for
insurance and 3s. per week per man to be
hauled up and down the shaft, and if they
get any gold at all the royalty is figured out
on its gross value irrespective of whether
wages have been made or not. Another
grievance put forward by the Kalgoorlie tri-
butors is that only an 85 per extraction is
paid fpr. Yet how many metallurgists would
keep their jobs if they could not show a
better gold recovery than that? A further
complaint is that only £4 per oz. is paid the
tributor for fine gold, and he wants to know
what better right has the co. to the balance
than he has.
After a 10 years’ struggle Ingliston Ex-
tended. on the Paddy’s Flat line of reef,
Meekatharra (W.A.), has, for a time at
least, given it up. Many thousands of
ounces of gold were extracted, but the co.
was never able to give shareholders any-
thing. Pending negotiations for a third re-
construction the mine has been let on
tribute.
* * * *
The mining crowd in Alec Peacock’s office
have again got nervy, or they wouldn’t
squeak as they do whenever a bit of a yarn
goes round about Edna Deeps. They lost
sleep over the story that prehistoric gnomes
had rolled slabs of granite into the shoot
of gold-stone. They worrited over the
report that the shaft didn’t hit a full face
of stone when it ought to. They had hardly
begun to dream that Nature had kicked the
shoot back south-west under the shaft when
a Mrs. Gamp gave them nightmare by say-
ing that water was creeping into the shaft.
So long as Bill Stuckey could cement it
out, that yarn needn't have caused anyone
to go and have two quick drinks; but it
did evoke another denial, notwithstanding a
solemn declaration that no more petty cash
was to be spent in contradicting bear yarns.
And now, at the very latest, some more
money has had to be taken out of the till
to find out from manager Stuckey if the
gossip that surveyor Macartney had re-
ported adversely to the mine was another
piece of bear exaggeration. No one seems
to have known that Macartney was in the
workings, but Stuckey tacked another lie
to the office counter by wiring a stout nega-
tive.
* * * *
From E. C. Dyason :—-
Re the proposed amalgamation of Bendigo Mines
and the par. (B. 14/12/’l6) questioning the right
of the promoters to one seat on the Arbitration
Board which is to give findings as to the interests of
the various cos. in the proposed scheme. My
recommendations are tentative only; yet I did not
make them without reasons. I had the choice of
suggesting:—
(1) An entirely independent body ; ,
(2) One consisting entirely of those interested; or
(3) A mixed Board consisting of interested and
disinterested parties.
I rejected No. 1 because such a Board, lacking
perhaps, in spite of evidence before it, a sufficient
knowledge of local intricacies, might fail to carry
weight with the. cos. to which its decisions must be
referred, and because of difficulties in getting a
suitable personnel. There is scarcely anyone with
knowledge who is not interested. No. 2 appeared to
me unsuitable because of a lack of sufficient disin-
terested weighting. I therefore suggested a mixed
Board with two members nominated by the Govern-
ment, one nominated by the local Chamber of Mines
to represent the owners, and one by the promoters
whose job it is to find the necessary capital and
who will have to see that prospective new share-
holders get a fair share of the profits. They also,
as projectors of the movement, have responsibilities
to the Federal Treasurer in regard to the respective
allotments. The last member suggested—a nominee
of the Bendigo Stock Exchange—-would be represen-
tative of both present and prospective shareholders.
The point isn’t really of great importance.
The big thing is how to constitute the Board
so that its recommendations will be most
likely to be accepted by the cos. The
Bulletin certainly shouldn’t regard
Dyason’s proposal as seriously damaging to
the scheme, even if it were 50 times more
offensive.
The financial proposals of the Bendigo
mining scheme have been published. The
co. at first proposed to register under the
limited liability Act, and this frightened
shareholders. It is since announced that
the co. will be registered as a no-liability
concern with a capital in 2,000,000 shares
of ss. One million shares fully paid to ss.
will be given to shareholders in the present
cos., their allocation to be settled by arbi-
trators. These shares will be freely sale-
able and transferable. Of this million the
promoters will get a good parcel in exchange
for the shares recently bought by them. The
second million shares will be divided by
issuing 400,000 at ss. to the public, reserv-
ing 400,000 and giving the promoters
200,000 fully paid. The promoters have
had the 400,000 shares to the public under-
written and also guaranteed up to ss. This
is something quite new in no liability co.
finance, for it means that if any shares
afre forfeited by the public the guarantors
must pay the calls so that the co. will not
suffer by having forfeited shares thrown
on its hands. Ultimately the 400,000 shares
will yield £lOO,OOO cash capital to start the
new concern going. The promoters take no
cash and have invested a good deal of their
own money in the purchase of forfeited
shares in existing cos. in order to obtain
control. How much it is not possible to
say. Bendigo shareholders through the
local Stock Exchange .express themselves
satisfied with the terms. But £lOO,OOO will
not go very far in modernising the mines;
it may be taken for granted the reserved
400,000 shares will be sold before the few
mines which the new proprietary proposes
to work are properly equipped.
* * * *
The Read and Rosebery Mining Co. has
got its special leases through the Speck’s
Parliament and has asked the State Govern-
ment to provide 25,000 hydro-electric horse-
power. The Government hopes to be able
to get it from the King and Franklin rivers
and is now trying to find out what the
scheme would cost. It is also negotiating
to buy out its co-mortgagee in the Zeehan
Smelters, the Deutsche Bank; but so far
its partner opens his mouth too wide. If
they come to an agreement the. State will
probably pass the smelters on to the Read-
Rosebery Co.
* * * *
From August, 1912, to the present time, a
little over four years, the Hampden has pro-
duced in round figures 27,000 tons of blister
copper. In 1912 reserves were computed at
24,880 tons dopper; in 1916 they contain
approximately 22,000 tons, or only 2880
tons less. But reserves now include a fairly
large quantity of concentrating ore, so the
trial run of 'the mill in March will be in-
teresting. The Hampden Co. is still a buyer
of stray copper leases. The Magnet and the
Una, acquired from the Leichhardt Co., are
the latest purchases. The Hampden was
very lucky in its early purchase of the
Duchess, and the Trikelano also looks like a
good bargain.
* * * *
Williams Fancy, Berringa (Vic.), earned
£6596 from its last six months’ work and
spent £7009 to get it. The nest-egg put by
when divs. were rolling in is paying differ-
ences just now. Stone runs a shade _ over
the sdwt. mark and would have paid if
Hansen hadn’t carried out heavy develop-
ment. His scheme is to prospect the old
Kangaroo reef from No. 4 level, and the
market is on the qui vive for a decent
strike on that line. The boss has had one
go from No. 7 level at the Surprise reef,
but only got poor spurs and veins. Still
he intends to use up more cash here by
pushing out north on the reef to see if the
shoots overhead live down in that direction.
On the Fancy lode he has to get away
from a break, but he wants something bet-
ter than the 3dwt. stone he has been driving
on. At Berringa every mine has petered out
below 1000 ft., so Hansen has more chance
of a strike in the higher formations than he
has at depth.
*** N _ *
The Junction gold-dredging show at
Bright (Vic.) has outlived most of its neigh-
bors. The astute William Quick collared
a big block of land long before the dredging
value of the Ovens Valley had become estab-
lished. Even now he has 3J years’ work in
sight, and with Tewksbury blown out of
competition by sludge boards, he expects to
be able to get hold of some more blocks and
rake in further profits. These profits don’t
run into big money, but with only 10:000
shares to pay on a profit of £lBO5 for the
last half-year with cash in hand enabled 3s.
per share to be pouched for the term. That
return came from 1.47 gr. ground: it took
296,000 cub. yds. to yield gold worth £3600.
Expenses aren’t covered nowadays by £6O
per week, for wear and tear on elderly plant
gobbles a lot of cash.
To date, Ruby Tin (Tas.) has put
£18,277 into the pockets of Andrew Sten-
house and his pals. But the sixpences don’t
come with the same speed as of old, though
lib. dirt still goes into the sluice-boxes. The
eastern side of the flat having been cleaned
out, the nozzle is now at work on the west-
ern ground, where it can go straight ahead
for several years. Broken ground has still
to be handled, but with the face ahead of a
big lode formation values ought to go above
the lib. margin. If so, New Ruby will pay
as of old, provided there’s the water-supply.
Perth Stock Exchange, which meets every
week-day but has not recorded more than
half a dozen sales within the recollection of
the oldest inhabitant, held its annual meet- 5
ing on December 7. A braw Scot, Donald
Carmichael, was re-elected president, and
the other half-dozen members present elected
themselves to the other positions.
* * * *
Old Wallaroo and Moonta (S.A.), which
for years stood at 30s. in the market, has
this year paid 15s. a share; and that brings
the total in the last 10 years to £3 ss. 6d.
But it is an uneven business, with no divi-
dends at all in 1910 and only Is. in 1911.
