A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney. December 26, 1916.
Mr Dear Artni, —
The almost-extinct garden party was re-
vived for the benefit of the State-school
teachers, fresh from their annual c-orroboree.
His Ex. came to town with Lady Edeline
and two or three daughters to give the glad
hand to the pedagogues. On a gorgeous
summer afternoon, even without a band to
cheer the spirits, a garden party is not alto-
gether futile. The company straggled out
on to the flowered lawns in front of Govern-
ment House, and when hard-pressed filled in
time by scattering compliments on Sydney’s
big blue pond.
The eldest daughter floated like a slim
grey cloud in misty pearl grey. The Sam
Griffith dame and the Charles Mackellar lady
were amongst the Chosen at Lady Edeline’s
tea-table. The Andy Stuart dame, in cool
summer drapery, and the Peter Board house-
hold were close to the official throng. Two
men who can keep on smiling—Cann,
M.L.A., the new amateur Railway Commis-
sioner, and Judge Backhouse—hobnobbed
with their pals on the tennis lawn. Justice
Street’s impressive presence decorated the
State tea-table. Dr. Cecil Purser —on the
Senate and therefore somewhere in the
Education orbit —brought his wife and pretty
daughter from Wahroonga. Premier Holman
came in later. Under the brim of a straw
hat he looked like the young man who might
have come to stir things up with a sonata
of his own composition, or a lightning-sketch
artist wlio’d forgotten his portfolio. Miss
Annie Parkes, daughter of the old political
lion, and Mrs. McGowen strolled together
amongst the scarlet salvia. General Finn,
on crutches, had to keep his damaged foot
up. Aide d’Apice and Secretary Budge
’passed round the cake.
Mrs. Black, the kindly missus of the lately-
retired Chief Secretary, came back the other
day, after a health-trip to Europe. The
usual clatter of tea-cups welcomed her at
the Australia, with Mrs. Holman to ask if
it was quite all right.
Do you remember, in the old happy-go-
lucky days, when naval officers had chiefly
rlance-engagements to think about, popular
Captain Dick Purefoy Fitzgerald? He had
to drop the Fitzgerald when the family
timber and shooting came his way. But
it’s the same breezy sailor who is now the
Admiral Purefoy recently appointed to the
British Legation at the Hague—the place
of kite-flying.
The handsome Joseph Ward lady (M.L.)
was amongst the confetti a few weeks ago
when a Christchurch girl, Marjorie Phillips,
walked away with Lieut. K. S. Williams.
The groom was shadowed by his mates of
the Canterbury infantry when he led his
girl out of Christ Church, near Hyde Park,
London. Her frock, by the way, of silk
brocade (same,as grandma liked in the long
ago), had the last moment’s modern touch —
one immense true-lover’s knot with a gar-
land of orange blossoms as a climax.
Governor Strickland had to say most of
the nice things at the opening of the Edith
Cavell rest-home for nurses. This is the
roomy mansion at Summer Hill given by the
W. E. Shaws. The Shaws, like Tom Dibbs
and his wife, have surrendered a home in
which they lived for 30 years—a harder thing
to part with than a new stucco villa. Sur-
geon-Colonel Stokes, P.M.0., totted up the
number of Australian army nurses who will,
after the war, be glad to put their feet up
and rest a bit at the Cavell home. Unless a
big percentage are snapped up by admiring
soldiers on the other side, the nest will have
about 300 on its books.
- Edith Gore Holdship, a relative of the lady
who used to be. Mrs. Willie Mitchell, was
married in England the other week. The
man was Arthur A. Brown —a Billjim, of
course.
From recent opera scenes and from Mrs.
Boult’s drama, “The Buckle o’ His Shoe,”
one gathers a few sartorial hints. Very
high leather boots were worn by the buck
of the 18th century on nearly every occasion
except his funeral. Boots that engulfed the
legs and wobbled like a blue-jacket’s bell
trousers —only the wobble was at the thighs.
Thus Monty Stephen, rather dry and legal
as the hero, the Earl of Chesney, was almost
hidden in his own top-boots while he defended
his home preserves against the bad Spanish
Admiral Gajiado. The latter was also booted
nearly to the waist.
Mrs. Boult, a slender streak of tireless
energy, writes plays while other women are
trying on new hats or wondering if the
washing’s coming home before Christmas.
In the latest effort —of course to help our
soldiers’ orphans when the stockings are
baited for Santa Claus —the Sydney woman
has produced a play that is playable. “The
Buckle o’ His Shoe,” which turns on the
siege of Gibraltar, only wants to be stroked
by a stage manager and played by a pro-
fessional cast to make interesting drama.
Queenie Boult took the heroine’s part in
the recent amateur performance at the
Theatre Royal. The vital spark of the
show was a clever youngster, Cecil Haines,
in the child’s part. The kiddy comes from
Maoriland. Colin Crane’s fine voice did its
best with some incidental songs written by
May Summerbelle. A crowded house made
a good cheque.
The new Baronet Leonard Lucas Tooth
has a new girl. Oh, no, not that sort! Later on
she will call him daddy. He is a ma'jor in the
British Army, and the younger brother of the
soldier who was killed at Ypres. The new
Tooth makes a great-aunt of Mrs. Cecil
Stephen, of Sydney, who is a sister of the
late Lucas.
Poor old Billjim nowadays has little
chance of being returned to Australia unless
he has his head knocked off. Even a patched-
up Billjim is so valuable, in the absence of
proper reinforcements, that, battered though
he may be, he is kept on the other side to
convalesce. Hence there is nothing for manv
of the Australian convalescent homes to do.
That large, luxurious nest, the Octagon, out
Darling Point way, lent by the Holts, is
therefore now sheltering its owners again.
Thev came back to their own hearthstone
simply because the Red Cross said : “Thank
you kindly, but we have no further use for
it.” Ardenbraught, handed over by Mrs.
Cecil Stephen, also rolls down its blinds and
gets out of business as a cobbler’s paradise
wliere worn Billjim could be half-soled and
wholly healed.
The teachers’ pow-wow over, and the in-
fants’ classes kissed good-bye, Miss M. M.
Simpson went off to Tasmania last week. If
you look at her pleasant face in this photo.
y OU will guess that sue carries the teacher’s
trade as easily as a pack of feathers. There
are school-marms who can smile at the end
of a hard year’s graft—and here is the Mark
Tapley of the Blackfriars Teachers’ College,
Some time ago this lover of children was
sent to Italy by the Department to inquire
into the Montessori method and see its fount,
Dr. Maria, pour her lessons-made-easy into
the infant minds. Miss Simpson has grafted
all that is best in the new system on to the
Froebel stock—the original Kindergarten
training which is a mid-Victorian relative of
the more modern scheme. And she is still
receptive. As principal of the Montessori
Practice School (Blackfriars) and lecturer
on education, she was one of the first to
grasp the merits of the Lilian Ellis 1915
phonetic system. So there’s reading without
tears in the Montessori child-garden,
What becomes of the V.a.D.’s? Thrown
out of work? Not they—bless ’em! They’re
trotting round with suit-cases and clean
aprons to help in Red Cross kitchens or keep
the home-fires burning in jam and pickle
depots.
Mrs. Bob Anderson Is to join her strenu-
ous Brigadier-General in London shortly,
He is the commercial man who went from
Sydney to give a hand with the business
affairs of our army on the other side, and
the authorities decorated him with the impos-
ing label to go on with. Months and months
ago Mrs. Anderson had got ready to go to
Egypt; then the blow fell—no ladies
admitted !
Even the usually dreamy girl art-students
noticed that there was a good deal of rain
falling during the early part of December,
So Dattilo Rubbo’s pupils conspired together
to give him a new umbrella. The great
surprise was preceded by lunch in the Rowe-
street studio. In the interval between soft
drinks, Lister Lister, on behalf of the dam-
sels present, handed over the gold-topped
brolly. For the first time on record words
failed the ebullient Rubbo.
They’re all doing it! Miss Fraser —daugh-
ter of School Inspector Donald —got up a
moonlight dance for the 34th Battalion the
other night at Sandringham. There were
more than fairies present, and £32 10s. fell
into the comforts’ fund. The girl’s uncle,
Malcolm Lamb, is O.C. of the 34th, so I
fancy he’ll send her a kiss by post for the
Christmas party.
As usual. Mrs. Harry Rickards sent out
1000 invitations for a Christmas dinner in
the Town Hall basement. The guests are
supposed to present one word of introduc-
tion—Poverty.
It’s quite 10 weeks ago since Frank
Bladen’s widow married Captain Harcourt
Douglas Brown in London, but I don’t
think the news is generally known here-
abouts.
The Soldiers’ Club, with Dr. Mary Booth
in charge, had a Christmas tree for lonely
Kangaroos. Mabel Grant Cooper was Mrs.
Santa for the occasion, and her lady helps
committed the usual piracy for weeks, raid-
ing every male relative for his last ounce of
baccy and a new pipe.
It is a poor mission that does no one any
good. A mountain village recently came
through much soul-searching, during the visit
of Parson Crotty, a John Storm orator who
explored the local conscience for about a
week and audited the debit and credit
account. When the hamlet settled down
again it called a meeting and presented its
own homely cleric with a new cassock. The
soul-searching had unearthed, amongst other
minor sins, the fact that there was a large
patch on the shabby old gown of the good
man who carried beef-tea and very gentle
admonition round from January to December.
Majorie Quinn, journalist Pat’s only
daughter, sends a line from N’York : —-
Eva Mylott’s concert last week in the iEolian Hall
was a big success. Needless to say, Australians were
there to swell the encores. Mrs. Hugh Ward and
Amy Castles; Frank Coffee (who bought his ticket at
the door and wandered in like a casual Australian) ;
Kathleen and Rita Narelle, daughters of songstress
Marie; and Hilda Aitken, who used to accompany
singers in her Sydney days—these were in the crowd.
Eva’s bronze hair was coiled in shining bands around
her head, and glinted in harmony with a mermaidy
sea-green velvet gown. “Gee,” said a ’Murkan lady,
“she’s a stunner!” Amy Castles is staying with sisters
Dolly and Eileen. With Percy Grainger’s golden
locks at the piano in the same city and Frances Alda
warbling in Caruso’s ear at the Metropolitian Opera
House, Australians are making themselves heard in
New York.
When the arguments are over as to who
owns which and what is to he done about it.
Miss Evans will take over the Domain Anzae
Buffet. She is the capable Mosman Y.A.D.
who mothered the first such crib for soldiers
in this city l —in the cave under the old Edu-
cation Building that was lately carted away
in brick and mortar remnants to the dust-
heap.
Titian-haired Mrs. Scot-Skirving, hand-
some wife of the Sydney surgeon, is still in
England. They lost a son in France early
in the year.
Young Fitzpatrick, son of the talkative
member who is a Minister at ’last, is back
from the Sand Heap, on sick-leave. He is
wearing the pale blue, and white badge of
that Light Horse lot which helped to aston-
ish the Turks in the desert.
Saw the other day the kind of mountain
home that a tired lawyer longs to retire
to sometimes —the big. brown bungalow just
built at Leura by Chief Justice Cullen. It
hangs on the green eyebrows of a lovely
gorge—the Valley of the Waters, away from
the track of the holiday tripper, with blue
hills and infinity for its front view. The
Cullens are passionate lovers of Australian
bushland —witness the carefully-preserved
native trees and flowers in their Mosman
garden. Before the blinds are up in the
mountain eyrie, tall waratahs, red-canped
sentinels, stand at attention in the grounds
that are still wild as the birds in the adja-
cent gully.
Over in Sydney for a few weeks to see her
husband in “The Land of Promise” is Mrs.
Arthur Styan. She used to be Clarice
Masters, a handsome Melbourne • girl who
had a taste of the footlights a few years
back.
Sister Elsa Kaye was married in London
this month to a Sydney man, Harold Jacobs.
Old soldier Wallack, who is probably
being retired from the Permanent Artillery,
is, so it’s said, taking up the simple life as
an extra A.D.C. to his Ex. If the old Artil-
leryman is driven to thus earn a crust as
a Gentleman Help at this time of day, it’s
no credit to the Defence Department.
Hitherto Permanent soldiers have not been
even turned out to grass when the chopper
fell. They have just been turned out —to
starve, unless some friend could offer them
a picking.
A man's view
Amazing - the way the feminine conventions change.
Not so long ago girls put on the warpaint seeretb
in their bedrooms; next they retired moaestlv to
corners to daub and powder ; now every one of them
carries a complete kit of nose- and lip-paint, and
fills in the conversational pauses bj' “making- up
publicly. I wish I had the courage to shave on the
morning boat! Some of them tittivatc so often
throughout the day that they are practically buried
alive in toilet rubbish by lights-out. Last night I
went home on a late ferry full of yawning citizens,
and one indefatigable mass of fashion who was within
15 minutes of her couch was still smearing it on in
front of the tiny mirror in her vanity ease. The
youth for whose benefit it was being done looked
or. dully. What’s the remedy for dyehards of this
sort ?
December has resounded with the wail of
fiddles and the vocalists’ search for Lost
Chords at Soldiers’ Entertainments. The
committee begs, borrows or merely pays
the exes. During the year that is almost
dust £620 has been handed over as out-of-
pocket exes, to the music-makers. Mrs.
George Earp arranges the items and steers
the committee as president.
Sydney contralto Eileen Boyd, widowed
by the war, meant to come hack this New
Year to her native State. But a rush of
concert engagements in England—where
they keep up the spirits of the wounded sol-
diers in weary days of convalescence —makes
her revoke.
Word comes that Mrs. Martin Donohoe,
who has been keeping as near her war cor-
respondent as the times allow, has gone to
Florence.
lion. sec. H. W. Edney, of the Aus-
tralian War Contingent, London, sends a
ream of information. Its hospitality card
invites Australian Billjim when he’s in
England on furlough to send his address
to the secretary, 59 Palace-street, London.
After doing that he can decide-which offer
of private British hospitality he’ll accept.
Another clever fledgling, Eadith Sullivan,
has had a good-bye concert in the Conserva-
torium. She’s the youthful pianist, a pupil
of Gordon Lavers, who won the Emmeline
Woolley scholarship, which gives the girleen
three years at the Royal Academy in Lon-
don.
Now that Mrs. Lyster Ormesby is away—
hubby is in khaki—her place as missus of
the Girls’ Guild Jumble Depot (Surry Hills)
is filled by sister Louie. Meanwhile Mrs.
Ormesby is hewing the Sunshine camp
scheme out of the rough. This means a cot
by the sea and a daily dip in the blue Pacific
for some of the children of mean streets
during the sweltering days of January.
It works -out at about ss. a week to keep
each youngster. They take no baggage. Ynys
just picks them up as they are—mostly in
two garments with lots of fresh air blowing
through. If you want to help the Sunshine
camp, ring up Edgecliff 1515, and say so.
One of Sydney’s persistent socks-for-sojer
girls, Constance Carson, is now in London.
After leaving Sydney she spent some months
in Pharaoh’s land helping Mrs. Willie Chis-
holm at Heliopolis.
George Castles, of the Bendigo aviary,
writes from Salisbury, where he drills to
meet the enemy and in his spare time sings
in adjacent villages. George is with the
A.S.C. “Beg Wykeham is quartermaster
and I am train storeman,” he says. “Reg
looks to the tucker and clothing; I give out
the shovels, brooms, soap, and so on. Later,
Reg will have charge of the rum —I’m going
to stick to* him like glue!”
Amongst the officers at Jervis Bay to take
part in the hand-shake with the G.-G. the
other morning was old acquaintance Lieut.-
Commander Seton. He used to be Flag-
Lieut. in the days of the progressive King-
Hall —first British Admiral on the Austra-
lian station to barrack for the Australian
navy as a necessity for our national life.
Go to buy the white buckskin shoes you
growled at paying 18s. 6d. for early in 1915.
and the bootman asks you 275. 6d.! By the
time it costs another 20 per cent, to shoe
us all, we’ll give it up and try home-made
sandals of brown paper.
Still they come! The 4th D.A.C. Com-
forts Fund hangs out a sign at 28 Moore-
street.

The Royal Bank of Queensland
Limited.
N otice is hereby given that an
Extraordinary General Meeting of
The Royal Bank of Queensland Limited will
be held at the registered office of;, the Com-
pany, 180 Queen Street, Brisbane, on
Tuesday, the 9th day of January, 1917, at
12 noon, when the subjoined Resolutions will
be proposed as Extraordinary Resolutions: —
1. That it is expedient to effect an amalgamation
of this Company with The Bank of North Queensland
Limited.
2. That, with a view to the amalgamation of this
Company with The Bank of North Queensland Limi-
ted, this Company be wound up voluntarily.
3. That Edward Denny Day and Edmund Harry
Matthews, both of Brisbane," be and' they are hereby
appointed liquidators for the purpose of such winding-
up, and that all the powers of the said liquidators
may be exercised by either of them.
4. For the purposes of facilitating the carrying
out of such amalgamation the Directors of this Com-
pany or a majority of them may and they are hereby
authorised if necessary to consent to the registration
of a new Company to be named “The Bank of
Queensland Limited,” with a Memorandum and
Articles of Association which have already been
prepared with the privity and approval of the Direc-
tors of this Company.
5. That the draft agreement expressed to be made
between this Company and the liquidators thereof
of the first part, The Bank of North Queensland
Limited and the liquidators thereof of the second
part, and The Bank of Queensland Limited of the
third part, submitted to this meeting be and the
same is hereby approved, and that the liquidators of
this Company as aforesaid be and they are hereby
authorised pursuant to Section 151 of “The Com-
panies Act 1863,” to enter into an agreement on
behalf of this Company in terms of the said draft
(which contains inter alia a provision for paying
under the circumstances mentioned therein the sums
and allowances for life specified therein to the pre-
sent General Manager of The Royal Bank of Queens-
land Limited and to the present General Manager of
The Bank of North Queensland Limited) with such
(if any) modifications as the said liquidators may
think expedient and to carry the same into effect.
Should the. Resolutions be passed by the
required majority they will be submitted for
confirmation its Special Resolutions to a
second Extraordinary Meeting, which will be
subsequently convened.
Dated the twenty-first day of December,
1916. By Order of the Board,
EDWD. D. DAY, General Manager,
ISO Queen Street, Brisbane.
MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, December 26, 1916.
• My Dear Yarrie, —
Alan, son of the Argus Spowers, is
amassing decorations at top speed. Firstly
he managed to pick up a Military Cross and
a few wounds, which sent him back home
for repairs. During his second smack at
the enemy in the young man
has earned the D.S.O.
The sole surviving daughter of the late
school-inspector Rix has been hard hit by
the war. She and her mother and younger
sister, all three being artists, were studying
painting abroad when the first guns sent
them hurrying back to England. As a re-
sult of the terrible journey the mother and
sister died within a few weeks of each other.
The grief-stricken sister, after a brief flash
of happiness as the bride of Major Nicholson,
D. 5.0., is now a widow. The Major, who
left a State-school teacher’s billet to take
on a fighting job, came from the Western
District.
Nigel Prichard, who had some nasty war-
experiences as a medical officer in Servia, and
has since been acting as resident-surgeon at
the Geelong Hospital, is once more on the
wing. This time he is making a flight to a
newly-bought practice at Pyramid Hill, on
the northern rim of the dry-farming belt,
where the mosquitoes come up from the
Murray country every night, and are very
thirsty when they get there.
Capt. Jack Locke, one of the Duntroon
collegiates who burned to get away with
the first Australian Expeditionary Force
and wasn’t allowed to, has fully made up
for lost time. Jack, who is Staff-Captain
of the 4th Infantry Brigade, achieved the
Military Cross some six months ago. Writ-
ing to his mother in Melbourne he guesses
that he got the Cross—which, by the way,
is a silver decoration —for leading a
night march against the Turks at Gallipoli
in August last year. Since that bauble was
bestowed on him he has been officially con-
gratulated on his doings in France. Official
congrats., it seems, are issued on type-,
written postcards. One of these, signed by
Major-General Cox, refers to “good work”
accomplished in two specified lots of opera-
tions “near Pozieres” during August, 1916.
Few of our kliaki-clads. who climb into
the trenches have brought back such a varied
assortment of first-hand experiences as Dr.
McAdam’s boy Cecil. Soon after clutcn-
ing his degree, Cecil joined a British army
corps with 99 other medical fledglings. He
was able to send along a column of Dublin
riot matter from the spot to the Argus,
which —in gratitude perhaps—later on pub-
lished a good deal about young Cecil’s lecture
on army hospital work. Next, there’s a
quarter-column in the Argus about Greeks
the same young man has known. The
articles are headlined : “By a British Army
Officer,” the name of the medico being in
smaller type. There’s nothing like hiding
your light under a bushel if you want to
escape notice.
From “Rosna,” in London: —
Don’t tell me millinery doesn’t count in war.
The turned-up hat of Tommy Kangaroo has been one
of the biggest assets of that engaging gentleman or
bloque, partly because in a world bespread with
khaki it immediately marked him as different from
every other warrior. Canada has acknowledged this
late in the day by inventing a distinctive headgear
for her army, too. It is a tam-o’-shanter with tails
behind and a stiff cock’s feather standing perkily up
in front. It gives the wearer a startled air, and
cannot touch the Australian hat. And neither can
Canada, poor thing, produce the grin that goes
under the hat of an Anzac ; and grins are nearly as
important as millinery.
That spry young veteran Reggie Bright,
whom the war marooned in England, writes
to say he is still going strong, but is disap-
pointed that no war work can be found for
eager gentlemen of 83.
Sam McCulloch’s American wife, with a
small son and his smaller sister in tow, has
been enjoying a spell of city life in a South
Yarra hash-palace. Her fellow-boarders are
struck dumb by the head coverings she
comes down to breakfast in. One little
extinguisher hides all her hair but a lock.
F. W. Wheatley, the science man from
the Naval College, trekked dver from Jervis
Bay to spend Christmas. After putting in
a fortnight seeing Melbourne he goes down
to the seaside to fit himself for next teaching
year.
Mrs. Arthur Woolcott, the Button-day
specialist, has received doctor’s orders to
drop the patriotic badge business and retire
into a private hospital for a rest. The poor
lady overdid it like all enthusiasts, and at
last started to hear collection-boxes in her
reams.
Mrs. Ben Nathan, of Ripponlea, has been
overcome by her efforts to rule a
domain, so the place will be shut up while
she takes a rest. For some time past the
portly dame has been fattening the patriotic
funds by charging people for the privilege
of viewing the lake, lawn, fernery and what
not which she and her intellectual hubby re-
gard as home. Apparently the good lady’s
pastime was too great a success.
Unable to return to W.A., owing to the lack
of steamers, the John Forrests have been
given shelter by the George Fairbairns at
Mt. Martha. Bigjohn and his lady, fol-
lowed by Miss North and a wake of luggage,
arrived at the Fairbairn seat last week.
Bigjohn, by the way, wants to sell his Perth
mansion and lay the foundations of another
a bit farther out.
Ballarat is to get its new imported bishop
early next month. The expensive shepherd
will be consecrated by Archbish. Clarke in
Melbourne on January 6 and inspected by
his flock on the following day. The
stranger’s name, I hope you haven’t for-
gotten, is Maxwell Homfray Maxwell-
Gumbleton. Gummy for short.
The handsome Roy Greene pair have
moved themselves and their most valued
chattels from South Yarra to the late Moles-
worth Greene’s Greystones property at
Bacchus Marsh. The fine old estate has
been left to this son, together with a pile of
dollars. Roy, who is probably the most-
carefully-groomed male in the city, onfce
decorated the local Garrison Artillery, but
faded back to civil life after the Govern-
ment had allowed him six months’ experi-
ence with British gunners in the Strand
somewhere in the latter ’Bo’s. Mrs. Roy is
a daughter of retired Justice Hartley Wil-
liams, who spends his pension in the Fog.
TOMMY COCHRANE, the Good Fairy of
the Repertory At Homes.
Tommy Cochrane is all in all at the Re-
pertory social junkets. Tommy can man-
age in his spare time to appear on the stage,
act as secretary for the more serious business
of the Reps., and turn up in the latest
smart-set duds and act as the beneficent fairy
at Rep. parties. At these last the old ladies
and the nearly-on-the-shelf dames can be sure
of attention from Tommy: he juggles extra
chairs from the air and dispenses smiles and
goodwill all round.
The garden fete at the Oreswicks’ Mal-
vern castle, Yarrien, was not stamped flat;
nevertheless a few hundred-odd of the charit-
ably inclined drifted in to give a financial
boost to the Berry-street Training Home
for Girls, which can hardly be described as
a really indispensable institution. It took
me some time to discover one person who
admits having obtained a domestic help from
it. The Como Armytage spinster was prime
mover in the Yarrien revel. The stormy
Stonington show 7 has rather scared the
people who wear fine chiffons at such func-
tions. Hundreds of pounds’ worth of. dresses
w r ere ruined by the weather that time, and
the experience shook .every best dresser’s
nerve. The doctors have _ gained many
guineas prescribing for chills caught by
some of the soaked unfortunates who that
day beat on the vice-regal doors. There was
no getting into the Stanley domicile to ruin
the carpets. The one creditable thing about
the fiasco was the large-hearted hospitality
of the Bowes Kellys; every door in their
place was thrown open to the cowering re-
fugees.
The Leslie Stuarts are moving into Logan
House, the Fred Fairbairns’ roomy castle
in the Toorak-road. The Fairbairns, after
building the white mansion and filling it
with costly furniture, suddenly decided to
become Anglo-Australians, and have been
passionately seeking a tenant who didn’t
w 7 ant to keep boarders as paying pets.
D. Y. Syme, jun., has dropped into the
w r ell-padded chair of the Steamship Owners’
Federation, vacated by W. T. Appleton.
The new young chairman came from the
managerial desk in the Melbourne Steam-
ship Co.’s Westralian office. Dad Syme,
by the way, looks a sad man since the loss
of the genial w 7 ife who made him brother-in-
law to George Reid.
Adelaide’s communique : —-
The new premises of the Returned Soldiers Asso-
ciation were formally announced by the Premier to
be habitable. The rooms are in Netter’s Buildings,
and the first' six months’ rent has been paid by the
proprietor.
The Trench Comforts Fund will benefit by a
f6te that was held in F. C. Howard's garden at
Mount Lofty on Saturday. It deserves it.
The Board of governors of the Art Gallery has
purchased from George Brookman a tapestry, “The
Adoration of the Magi,” designed by Burne-Jones
and worked by Morris and Co., the firm of which
the late William Morris was the head. The Diana
vase, said to be one of the best samples of art that
the late Seabridge Wilson handed to the Doulton
people, and loaned to the Gallery since 1909, has
now been given to it by Mr. Brookman.
Having grumbled thoroughly at the unceasing rain
and unjoyous winter, we are now doing it all over
again owing to a sudden onset of summer. At the
end of last week the thermometer clambered into the
nineties and mounted cheerfully till it touched
nearly 105 deg. on Tuesday. The only applause for
this performance came from the farmer and the
draper—the former looking to save his crops and
the latter anxious to sell some hitherto unwanted
summer clothing.
The Emergency Corps Committee of the League of
Loyal Women, with the help of the Regimental
Clubs, upended 14 Christmas trees at the Oval on
Tuesday for the pleasure of soldiers’ children and
their mothers. Over 2200 youngster* were stuffed
with good things and loaded with toys, which were
mostly home-made. Extraordinarily well-made they
were. It’s safe to prophesy that next year home-
made toys will be the thing in the way of Christmas
presents.
A Flower Day, arranged by the schools, added
more money to the Repatriation Fund on Wednes-
day. In the past fortnight teachers and scholars
have added almost £3OOO to the account.
Mareeba, the Red Cross Hospital at Woodville that
has just been closed, is to become a Babies’ Hospi-
tal, under State control, early in the year. The
Government intends to ask the help of the present.
Babies’ Hospital management committee in starting
its new work.
The Sturt statue was unveiled by the Governor on
Thursday. It stands at the north-western corner of
Victoria Square, close to the statues of Light and
Stuart. Over 10 years ago the committee of the
Colonel Light Statue Fund handed the balance of
its cash to the Mayor with a recommendation that
it be used as the nucleus of a monument to Sturt;
the Price Government agreed to give pound-for-
pound subsidy ; and the City Council presented the
site. The total cost was about £l6OO, of which a
little over £l4OO has come to hand. Adrian Jones
was the sculptor, and the explorer’s daughter says he
has achieved an excellent likeness.
There being no Savage Club rule which
prohibits, the marriage of the secretary,
“Bill” Tanish did it on Wednesday, his co-
adventurer being Mina Moore, our best-
known woman photographer. The wedding
was a quiet one, but the evening before the
Savages drank William’s health and pre-
sented him with furniture, silverware, advice
and encouragement.
Pretty Meryl Waxman, daughter of lawyer
Louis, said she would to young Dr. Lister
at Holy Trinity last week. Only a few
near relations were present, as the bride’s
father is a very sick man nowadays.
Hal Gye, questioned at the point of a pen,
has divulged particulars of his recent dash
into matrimony. They have been married
three months, and have been living quietly
at Ascot Yale in furtive enjoyment of their
lovely secret. Mrs. Gye was a Miss Alice
Gifford, and the artist embezzled her from a
Williamson pantomime co. Evidently she is
the pride of his life, for he speaks of her in
a low, intense manner which suggests that he
will soon be dropping caricature for lyric
poetry. ,
Miss H. Romanis, who pushes a secre-
tarial pen for the P’ran Red Cross, was mar-
ried to Corporal H. Grayndler last week.
The bride is the sister of the immaculate
personage who has town-clerked for some
years for the bargain suburb.
Mere words cannot paint barrister Whit-
ing’s expression at the present time. He
has a new grand-daughter, and daughter
Eileen is its mother. Eileen’s husband, Cap-
tain Geoffrey Walker, 31st Punjaubis, is too
far away to signify his approval, so grandpa
Whiting beams for the lot.
One of the recent golden weddings in Mel-
bourne was nothing of an advt. for early
marriages. Mr. and Mrs. George Buzzard,
of St. Kilda, who have run togther for half
a century, were 37 and 31 respectively when
they put the double harness on. George
Buzzard put in 50 years of active life in
Vic. as an insurance man in the service of
the Liverpool, London and Globe.
The late Laurie Abrahams, whose South
Yarra home was a Sunday-night rendezvous
for artists in his days of health, had been
bedridden for nearly four years before he
died. He took a trip to Europe in company
with Louis Hattenbach, the ’cellist, and re-
turned to the wharf at Port Melbourne on a
stretcher. It was bad enough for the good
old Australian Hebrew, but he might have
come to a worse end. Had he taken the
same trip 18 months later he would probably
have died in a German prison camp. One
of the Abrahams boys, by the way, lately
went off to join the Anzacs at the Front.
The industrious sculptress, Margaret Bas-
kerville, is preparing to drop a block of
marble on the coffin of Edith Cavell. The
stone is to bear a bronze head of the
deceased nurse attached tb a symbolic
arrangement which will try to reveal the
soul emerging bashfully from the body. At
time of writing, the soul is only in an
embyro state, but there is enough of it visible
to show that Margaret must have viewed the
object at close quarters.

“Ships that Pass in the Night,” lifting up

Of course it’s a spiteful man who writes
this
I was privileged to be in a city hairdressery the
other day when our most-talked-of tenor was having
his beautiful moustache curled. It was wonderfully
impressive. Singer and barber —each an artist
exchanged bows, as though about <to begin a
minuet, and after a few graceful gestures and some
well-chosen remarks in Italian —the: harshness of
his native Saxon would have spoilt everything—the
tenor sank into a chair and tossed tack his head.
When the operator seized the heated tongs a hush
fell upon the chatterers who waited their turn. The
silence lasted until the rite was completed, when
both artists heaved deep sighs. The tenor went to
the mirror and gazed ecstatically; he touched the
adornment reverently, as though doubting that so
beautiful a thing could be real. After languorously
rolling his expressive eyes at his own reflection he
sighed, and with a gesture of hopeless wonderment
reluctantly turned away. Capturing his halo, he
presented the barber with a speech of thanks in
Italian and a sweeping bow in 18th century French,
bent his head to the waiting patients, and —well,
then we resumed our discussion of the war.
Ruby Gray, the musical member of the
Collins-street clan, went to Honolulu this
week to stay with Melba. She won’t get
there in time for Christmas, but she’ll hear
Peggy Centre at her "return home” concert.
At Her Majesty’s on Saturday opera-
glasses were concentrated on the new prin-
cipal boy, Hilda Guiver. She is a mixture
of blonde and brunette and has enough good
looks to make the compromise attractive.
Her starring partner, Gretchen Yates, is of
more modest dimensions, but she has one
of those passionate heads of hair that set
one hunting for euphemisms to describe its
coloring. It would be untruthful to liken
her tresses to certain humble vegetables.
Still it is wonderful how she manages to
keep cool under such a conflagration. She
explains, by the way, that Gretchen is not
of Hun origin, but was merely adopted as a
suitable companion for Yates. Another
interesting lady in the panto, is Mrs. Ritchie,
who directs the break-neck biking troupe.
She brought the team from the Fog, and on
the voyage out a torpedo nearly dropped in
for breakfast. Mrs. Ritchie, like the others,
grabbed her lifebelt, and for the remainder
of the journey was practically married to
the thing.
Phil Finkelstein, the classic-featured Wil-
liamson press-agent, who has been laid up
for some time with nerve trouble, is back at
work helping to keep the pot boiling.
Queenscliff is threatened with an invasion
of war-time economy women. They promise to
begin operations in the bathing-suit depart-
ment, and say that no one will have cause
to complain that any material has been
wasted in the making of their gowns.
Length is to be abolished, and width con-
fined chiefly to stripes, some of which are
said to be loud enough to waken the dead.
But. as usual, the censor won’t let me give
you fuller details.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“1.V.D.” ; Things once seen never for-
gotten. A pair of wedge-tailed eagles, a party of
brolgas or a-small lot of pelicans, soaring in a clear
sky. A mob of galahs—about four or five thousand
strong—taking their afternoon flight, moving with
the precision of cavalry, obeying some law or leader,
and in their flight showing their rose and lavender
plumage against the low sun. A mob of emus
making in to water. A flock of ibis flying to their
roost on the edge of the timber across a plain, the
different formations of front, probably to meet air
currents, conceived and acted on instantly. The
incomparable dance of the brolgas. The antics of
the lyre-bird and his glorious notes of the most
perfect mimicry while doing his turn on the mound.
The strategic movements of wild ducks in hiding
themselves and their young. The mother duck,
gammoning winged, will often lead a new-chum a
mile away from her brood. The flight of budgeri-
gars (many thousands) at sunrise, or the same
flock feeding on button-grass. A flight of green
parrakeets when the eucalypt is in flower and full
of honey. A flock of pigeons when migrating.
You’ve got to go a good step out now to see this.
Pelicans fishing, silent, methodical, deadly. A
mother emu piloting her chicks through a bit of
enemy country, evading gohannas, carpet snakes,
eagles and hawks and dingoes, and (incidentally)
man. The swoop of a full-grown eagle on to a
plain turkey, lamb or wallaby, or his chase after a
duqk. I have seen the top of a wallaby’s head
lifted clean off and the brain exposed by one stroke
of the hind claw of this great bird. The swoop and
crash of a falcon into a flock of small birds, killing
perhaps four or five and retrieving most of them
before they hit the ground. The cruel little black
falcon hunting bats in the dark or late dusk.
Friend kookaburra handling a snake. The fish-hawk
dipping for a yellow-belly. The cuckoo laying her
egg (on the ground) and then putting it in, say, a

“Eddyson”: Godfrey had had words with
Embling, as a consequence of the latter’s persistence
in paying attention to Miss Olive Godfrey. He had
gone to Embling as one gentleman to another, and
had called Embling a crimson-dipped, misbegotten
son of an unmentionable person, averring that he
would bat the young fellow’s detestably cow-face in
with a blood-spattered spade if he did not desist
from thrusting his loathsome company on the young
lady in question. Embling had nierely shifted his
weight from one leg to the other and grumbled
inarticulately. “Mind,” said Godfrey, “if these ’ere
goin’s on goes on I’ll be sayin’ somethin’ you won’t
like.” Then when the Godfrey arose one morning
and discovered that Olive was absent from her bed
and missing from her home, leaving a note, which
read: “Full up of Bedrock, full up of cows, full up
of YOU. Off! —Your ever-loving daughter, Olive,”
the distracted father wasted no moments weighing-
matters, but dashed straight over the hill to
Embling’s selection. He found Embling dismember-
iug' a sheep he had recently killed. Godfrey weighed
15st., Embling something under 9st., and the latter,
with the former on top, had no show on earth.
Godfrey hammered the hapless young man into
insensibility. A subsequent search failed to bring
to light any vestige of the missing maiden. Next
day Embling was able to sit out and take the air; a
week later he was in a position to call upon Godfrey
with a view to settling the case out of court. It
was now known that Miss Godfrey had gone to Mel-
bourne with a railway ganger named Evans. Embling
gave a list of his physical injuries, he enlarged upon
his moral wrongs, and dilated on a shock to the
system. Then suddenly Godfrey fell into a 11117.
“Bring yer action !” he howled. “Show yer bruises,
’n’ breaks, ’n’ cuts, and let the Court see all the
trouble I’ve had for nothin’.”
**. < * *
“A. J. Grey” : Allow me to introduce the
champion teetotaller of Australia. He is a wedge-
tailed eagle who for the last four years has been
chained to a perch outside the Australian Hotel at
Murgon (Q.). When first captured water was* put for
him to drink, • but he never touched it, so the tin
was taken away and he has done without it ever
since. Year after year he has sat and watched the
thirsty visit the bar, but, rising superior to his
environment, he has defied the hottest and driest
weather to make him take a drink. It may be
his abstinence that makes his eye so clear and his
mien so unhappy, and this may also account for
his docility, for though his beak and talons are
fully developed and look highly dangerous, he has
never hurt anyone, and a child can stroke his head.
His food is raw meat, which evidently contains suffi-
cient moisture for his needs. But can anyone tell
me is that the case with all meat-eating birds? I
have wondered, too, where the gulls in mid-ocean
drink. Some say they get moisture from the clouds,
but with our friend as an example of how water
may be done without, is it not more probable that
they do not drink at all?

WAGGONING HYMN, 1917.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
On the water-waggon
Shun the driver’s seat —•
In the lonely boneyard
Soon or late we’ll meet —
Sit upon the tailboard,
Smiling as she goes,
A sly flask in your pocket
For to warm your ruddy nose.
Old Argent-street was dust and heat in golden eighty-
eight ;
And men were men, and money dross, and naught
could youth abate;
And, for that strength was such, at length they paid
excess a fee,
To so discount their youth that they might rest more
peacefully.
Whiskjr in the pannikin,
Golden as the sun ;
Smelting in the cannikin
Youth and years foredone.
Underneath its oily wave
Hid were old age and the grave.
Whisky in the pannikin,
Golden as the sun.
On the button-grass the big men pass in the biting,
endless rain,
To dig galena by Zeehan Creek and to spend it fast
again.
To dare the sea by Reminie and - the rocks of
Royenrine,
And to conquer rain with a heavier wet when the
West Coast winds blow keen.
Brandy in the Jack Shea,
Three-star in the can;
Leeches where the tracks lay
Waiting for a man ;
Wet sky, mourning as with crape—
’Needs the sunlight for the grape.
Brandy in the Jack Shea,
Three-star in the can.
The sunlight on the salt lakes makes mirage—the
devil’s sea—
And men swamped up to Hannan’s, broke, but
grinning cheerfully—•
Salt white as wool, condensers full, with drought the
* land accurst;
And he was slain who’d try in vain, to baulk dry
blowers’ thirst.
Shypoo in the billycan,
Old rye in the glass—
’ Dust and willy-willy can
None but wowsers pass.
Shall a ’Venturer of Chance
Find “in tea his Lost Romance?
Shypoo in the billycan,
Old rye in the glass.
By the rocks of lime, a-many a time, on the plains
of Chillagoe,
Where the bower-birds play by Cathedral Rock and
the scented gales do blow;
By the spinifex and the flies that vex from the
Leichhardt to the Wills,
Where a man has life (if he has no wife), there’s
a quick demand for stills.
Whisky in the pannikin,
Old rye in the glass;
Golden is the cannikin
As the sunbeams pass.
Lemonade and gingerbeer
Never made our country here.
Sparkling is the pannikin
As the sunbeams pass.
In Tuscan fields the thin'soil yields its sour and
feeble wine;
London its dockyard glass of port and Paris absinthe
fine;
And New York sells in brilliant hells its cocktails
complicate;
Nor boozer may by ’Frisco Bay be long without a
mate.
Vinegar in flagons,
Absinthe in a cup;
Both these carry dragons
To double sinners up ;
To the Queensland scrubs I come
For the sugar-cane and rum.
Vinegar in flagons
Doubles sinners up.
By Botany Bay as bright as day the moon comes
from the sea,
Half-masted in the Norfolk pines; and, on the bal-
cony,
Sit. Jack and me the while the sea lisps flatt’ry to the
sand,
And drink the health of Captain Cook, & tankard in
each hand;
Tanglefoot in tankard
Foaming to the brim ;
Captain Cook we drank hard,
And the mates of him—
Drank his health till ours was lost.
Damn the future and the cost —-
Captain Cook we drank hard .
And the mates of him.
A sickled reach by Aola beach up in the Solomons,
And a nigger to each white man carrying gin to
toast the Dons ;
Two nigger boys to bring the joys of the eocoanut
milk when green;
In the boat a case of the good square face to broach
where the palm-trees lean.
Squareface in the eocoanut
Swizzled with the milk—
Every nobbier broke a nut —
Mixture soft as silk.
Swizzled gin is just the thing
For the kidneys of a king.
Squareface in the eocoanut.
Mixture soft as silk.
On the water-waggon
Beating six o’clock !
All the talk of Gothenberg
Does the waggon mock.
Virtue’s road is dreary
And soon we will be dead —
Wine is for the weary —
Let the waggon plod ahead.
BOTANY BAY.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.

“Leo Lear”: Did you ever hear of a gate
getting fixed on a man's mind? Some years ago
riding into Morven station (now closer-settled) in
Western Vic. I overtook an old man walking on the road
and bound for the same place. We went in through
a gate which he opened and shut, and when half-way
across the paddock he stopped short and went through
the dumb show of lifting the wire coupling of another
gate, pushing it open, going through, and shutting
and fastening it behind him. I mentioned the
matter at the station, and was informed that a fence
and g'ate once crossed the track at the exact spot at
which the man stopped. He seemed rational enough
on other subjects, but never passed the locality
without opening and shutting the imaginary giate.
Seems to me some of our jockeys see similar obstacles
in the straight.
* * * *
'Hay”: One of the things which always
appeared unaccountable to me in my early days out
in the Sunset was the way in which wild cats had
distributed themselves many hundreds of miles from
any human habitation. The ordinary imported tabby
and tortoiseshell were to be met with even in almost
waterless tracts of country. Evidently the life suited
them, for they were always in good condition and
must have subsisted on birds, lizards and such small
game. Binghi viewed them with a kind of super-
stitious dread and would not even allow his dogs
to molest them if he could prevent it. He called
the cat “teen.va teeehera,” which means mystery, or
fog, or anything that creeps silently. Fog or mist
is rare in the Outback regions, and like the coming
of the soft-footed cat seemed to be too much of a
puzzle for Binghi’s comprehension. At any rate
he thought it best to treat pussy respectfully, and
when told that the white man made it comfortable
in his house and let it live with him Binghi was
scornfully incredulous. The manner in which so
many of our so-called tame cats have answered the
call of the wild makes me think that my sphinx-like
purring machine on the softest cushion is only
acting a part, and that in her heart of hearts she
would far rather be rearing a brood of scratching,
spitting wild devils in a hollow log at the back of
Beyond.
“Te; Babarantiko”: People are killed by
falling cocoanuts, ’spite of what “Te Pana” says (B.
16/11/T6). I know cases. Close shaves are innu-
merable. I once sat for an hour or so under a young-
tree some 20ft. high, loaded with bunches of three-
quarter-grown nuts, the stage at which they practi-
cally never fall unless disease is present. When I
arose and took a couple of steps to remove the
kinks from my back —-plop! a nut weighing, several
pounds fell in the exact spot where I had been sit-
ting, and it remained standing on end, being driven
into the soft ground. Another time, when gathering
nuts with the aid of a wheelbarrow, I passed under a
tree some 60ft. high, and a ripe nut fell in front of
me between the shafts of the barrow and bounced
out again, almost touching my nose. On another
occasion a nut fell from a full-grown tree
close behind me. Startled, I turned quickly, and
another nut dropped right before me so near that.l
felt the air of its passage on my face. When
brown relative walks the palm-overhung road his
eyes are always-aloft on the watch for trouble.

UNREASONABLE.
MRS. NOBBLER (2 a.m.): "This is indeed a nice hour to come home!"
MR. NOBBLER : “Have sensh, woman! D’ speck a man to stop out all night on the
misherable sum of four an' tuppence?"
“Karauria”: Why should “Perewiki”
grumble (B. 7/12/'l6) because the Maori uses what
he calls “Anglicised words”? Henare could no more
get along nowadays with his own lingo than Horace
could with his if he turned up on the Appian Way.
If the Maori wants the long beer, or the cigar, or
the cow, or the pair of pants, or the box of pills he
has to use one or other of those words because he
had none of his. own. If he wants to send a tele-
gram, or ride in a motor, or discuss the relative
advantages of sowing cocksfoot or alsike on certain
soil, or hold a debate on the influence of Christianity
or Judaism, he-is at once involved in subjects which
bankrupt the tongue that-was good enough for the
crew of the Arawa. The fact is that the Maori
is ceasing to speak real Maori because to do so
would be useless, and because he has necessarily
ceased to think like his forefathers. There is
nothing very wonderful about that. But the yarn
about a golden-haired waihine is a bit over the odds.
The only color that beats the black is red, and even
then the rusty carrot wins -through very seldom —
never on the first cross. And what does “Perewiki”
know about the “peculiar vices” of the original
article? Bah! He te tangata korero. 1 know no
man with the blood of the Ngapuhi or Waikatos in
Him whom even a Cockney strain has made vicious
enough to slander the women of the pakeha nor
shallow enough to make cheap ridicule of the Eng-
lishman because his language is chock full of Teuton
words. If I did, I would call him kuri.
“D.F.” : The kelp-beds mentioned (B.
30/11/’l6) by “N. or M.” have another use besides
sheltering the fish. A's fishermen know well,
they make a splendid harbor in rough weather.
When a sea gets up the boats run in
among the kelp, which breaks the force
of the waves and turns them into a mere swell. A
boat can ride safely in a big kelp-bed when it would
stand a very poor chance outside, and it can be
rough round this coast. When the kelp comes ashore,
as it does in millions of tons sometimes after a
storm, it’s a rattling good manure. In fact, it
used to be said of the people at Orford, small
farmers and fruitgrowers, that they lived on kelp
and seaweed.
“Chevron”: Close calls? Here's an early
start. While a small boy was sitting in a jinker
drawn up at the roadside in Prahran (Vic.) an elec-
tric tram, whose brakes had failed to act, crashed
into the back of a stationary motor-car. The car
was hurled 40 feet forward against the jinker. The
jinker in turn was flung against a verandah-post, and
the verandah-post snapping, the upper part fell
between the boy’s knees and pierced the floor of the
vehicle. The kid was . unhurt; but he doesn’t want
ever again to be the penultimate link in such a
chain of colliding circumstances.
“Two Seas” : Re jibbing horses. Often a
good hard-working moke gets a bad name from a
new owner simply because he to pull when
harnessed in an unaccustomed position. There is no
animal so obstinately conservative, and none with
such a marvellous memory.- He is in the Upper
House of the animal kingdom, so to speak, and hates
change. One instance out of many. A neighbor of
mine bought a horsb at a sale that on bis looks
was equal to shifting two tons on a level road. A
fortnight later the buyer brought him In me and
offered to swap him for a-pair of fowls. He told me
quite frankly that lie had spent a week trying to
make the animal shift an empty dray, and certainly
the obstinate moke looked as if someone had been
busy upon him. His rump was covered with the
rogue’s brand, put on with a spreader, and his hair
looked as if the owner's persuasive language had
fired the grass under him. After the deal was made
I tried the animal in a team, and he pulled like 40
devils. It didn’t matter to him if the leader wasn't
pulling an ounce. So long as there was anything in
the shape of a horse in front of him my singed
“jib” was quite content to do all the work. Ho had
always been used to working behind a mate, and
refused to work on his lonesome. By the way, can
any Abo. horse-master mention a sure cure for a
mare that has a habit of going up on her hind legs and
pawing the atmosphere whenever she feels annoyed?
I have tried most things short of dropping a bomb
on her. head, but the bill for new shafts still appears
regularly.
“Barcoo”: Next to using the lariat,
throwing and tying down a beast is the most intei
esting and spectacular performance on the Noi em
Queensland cattle camps. It becomes of giea ei
interest on account of the danger that attaches to
keeping the beast on the ground, although the
dinkum stockman, such as you’ll find in the Gu
country, has little difficulty in doing this. One
man who is right up to his business can manage
by himself, but the ordinary hand usually has an
assistant. One operator races his horse up
beside an animal, makes it put on pace, reaches
over to grasp the horny’s tail firmly in one hand,
and gives it a couple of sharp swings towards his
mount, which causes the proprietor to cross its
hind legs. Immediately this happens over goes
the show, both men are off their horses in a flash,
and the horny is kept on its back by one grasping
the horn and muzzle, holding the former down and
the latter up, while the other man passes the tail
round between the hind legs, -at the same time
grabbing the hind leg nearest him. The rest is easy.
Where scrub cattle have to be captured dogs trained
to the purpose are usually employed, the breed gener-
ally having a splash of bulldog blood. When the
Mickey is sighted the dogs set sail, one makes a
grab and hooks on to the flying beast’s lip, while
the other grabs the tail. The captive soon comes
down and the musterer does the. rest.
“O. Kow”: Has the Gippsland farmer
tried this waj' of clearing his land of bracken fern?
Cut and burn, and as soon as it starts to shoot
again put on as many sheep as you can (say, five to
the acre). I worked for Jack Webb, at Te Horo
(M.L.), and that was the way he cleared his land
of fern.' As the job knocked Sheol out of his own
sheep owing to the young fern scouring them he
bought a mob of old ewes at a bob a head for the
work. Ground with bracken Bft. high has been
cleared by this treatment. After the first time you
can crush fern every spring with your own stock.

“Askeaton” : Allow me to introduce “Glen-
dower” (B. 30/11/’l6) to the rest of the Kelly
family while he is touring North Queensland with
his banjo: Crosscut Kelly, Black Kelly, Kelly the

“Papilio”: Victoria’s recent record rains,
which have caused such a growth of grass that big
bushfires will be a feature of the summer, have
had the interim result of promoting the growth of
the caper plant. This in turn has led te a vast
increase in the clouds of caper butterflies which visit
the State annually. Coming on the wings of the
north wind, these insects, whose half-mourning cos-
tume of pearl-white with black trimmings seems to
indicate that Nature is showing her sympathy for
the long casualty-lists, have in many cases overshot
the mark, and millions, falling into the sea, have
been washed ashore on the coasts of Port Phillip

PUNDIN: "Burr! Yarns can't pass a pub."
“O’Phiim'i-t.v”: A Bulletin par. men-
tioned that Kelly, the first circumnavigator of Tas-
mania, said the natives were of gigantic stature.
Curious that Tasman made a similar statement in his
journal for 1643, the year of his visit to the island.
He inferred from steps cut so far apart in trees
that the natives must have very long legs. This Was
their method of climbing. A loop of grass rope
with three or four feet of slack was placed round
the trunk of a tree. The climber, generally a
woman, according to the letters of our oldest in-
habitants, got inside this, and gathered the slack
under her arms. A small hole was made in the
trunk about three and a half feet from the ground.
Lifting her leg the athletic lady placed her big toe
in this, and gradually worked the loop up the trunk,
alternately leaning her weight against the rope and
jerking it upwards, until her body was raised right
above the flirt step. Another hole was then cut, the
toe of the other foot inserted, and upward progress
continued in similar manner. In this way the
loftiest trees were scaled, the steps being never less
than three feet apart. While not in use the flint
for making the holes was balanced on the head.

“J.F.H.”: “The Best of Luck,” Drury
Lane’s latest spectacular melodrama, con-
tains a couple of thrills. Madge Titheradge
is the central figure of one of them. She has
to chase an express train on a motor-cycle,
and in doing so rides over a plank stretched
across what looks like an abyss of tremen-
dous depth. It is doubtless not quite so
dangerous as it looks, but it provides a
genuine thrill when viewed from the front,
all right. The management wanted 'a trick
cyclist to do this feat, but Madge insisted on
playing the whole of the. part herself.
“E.T.”: Australian-born composer Clut-
sam is responsible for half the score of
Robert Courtneidge’s new musical produc-
tion, which will soon be in the bills of Daly’s,
London. The other half is the work of that
Britisher, Bath, who was conducting grand
opera in Australia a few years ago. The
librettist is old-hand Captain Basil Hood;
the title, “Young England”; the central
figure, Drake.
By way of showing to the world that
Shakspeare belongs exclusively to them, the
Huns publish their theatrical statistics for
the past season, claiming 675 performances
of the Bard’s plays in 94 theatres. “The
Merchant of Venice” is top-scorer with 98
performances; “Hamlet” a good second with
97. Only two of the English historical plays
were presented—“ Henry the, Fourth” and
“Richard the Third.”
From London :—•
Elocutionist Alex. Watson, who starts in February
for another Australian tour, has just got back after
three months spent in entertaining the boys on the
Western Front. Altogether he gave 75 recitals, some
to the accompaniment of fire and shell. Big
crowds rolled up everywhere. Both comic and
serious selections were given; but Billjim preferred
the latter, and especially Masefield’s fine “Ever-
Jasting Mercy.”
“B.B.” t Mention of picture censors re-
minds me that Maoriland’s recently-
appointed in the first seven weeks of his
dreadful job examined 640,000 ft. of film.
Out of 260 pictures four were fired out, and
minor alterations were made in 35. The
taste of picture audiences can be gauged by
the fact that there was not a single “indus-
trial” film in the bunch and only four of
scenery, • And practically all Yank! I
wouldn’t be a film censor for a stall seat on
Judgment Day.
From London :—-
Old Melbourne man Oswald Stoll must be feeling
sorry he parted with £55,000 for the Opera House,
the big palace built and abandoned by New York
Jew Hammerstein. The Alhambra and the Coliseum
under Stoll’s direction are constantly crowded, but
no matter how he tries he cannot persuade the
public to patronise the other. He has tried the ex-
periment of transferring for a week the whole of a
highly-successful Coliseum programme to the L.0.H.,
but it didn’t work. The artists who performed to
crowded houses twice a day at the Col. faced dreary
wastes at the Op. By the way, Stoll has just had
trouble with his musicians. They demanded a big
rise in wages to meet increased cost of living. Stoll
said he couldn’t, so they struck, and now their places
in his half-dozen London Variety houses are occupied
by women, of whom no less than 2000 applied. The
drummer at the Coliseum .gets £7 10s. a week —
twice the screw of the first fiddler. He didn’t strike.
He was the only one at the Col. who didn’t.
Vachell, author of “Quinney’s,” continues
to work overtime. Recently two more dramas
dropped off the end of his pen, making half
a dozen for about a year’s work. One of
the new nieces, bearing the odd title of “Mr.
Jubilee Drax,” was expected at the London
Haymarket last month. The other, “Mrs.
Pomeroy’s Reputation,” is Violet Vanbrugh’s
principal excuse for a tour of the “provinces.’'
“Melb” : I don’t know what Allen Doone
chops out of Mack’s “Tom Moore,” written
by Sayre, an American, but certain it is
that, as acted by Mack in Australia, the
play could not be staged in London now.
George IV. appeared in it, as Prince of
Wales, with his wife, Mrs. Fitzherbert.
Directly George V. came to the throne, a
ukase went forth that there was td be no
mention of George IV. on the English stage.
There had been an enormous amount of
George matter thrust upon managers and
publishers. In particular the Americans
shot in three comedies which had long been
acted and approved in the United States.
One was “Tom Moore,” another “The Fourth
George,” and the third the well-constructed
“Beau Brummell,” by Fitch, I think. Mase-
field protested against the Chamberlain veto-
ing a play he submitted, with only an
oblique and highly complimentary reference
to George. “Never mind,” said the careful
official, “there is not to be any reference at
all.”
I DO NOT ASK FOR MISTLETOE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
All the delightful old customs associatwl w'tli
Christmas such as the kissing beneath the mist e
toe, seem to be forgotten in Australia. •.
—Complaint of a woman writer.
A splendid ev’ning gilded by the moon ;
A beat of music pulsing in the air ;
Life in a tender passion half a-swoon
Where waters of an endless longing croon ,
And you yourself the ecstasy to shaie,
While hours in spendthrift beauty come and go
I do not ask, dear heart, for mistletoe !
Or any other staging of our play
Will do, perchance. ’Twill not be drab, I know,
While love-lights in your eyes can make it gay,
And the soft wings of joyance brush away
All mundane thoughts, and bid all troubles go,
Taking the joys life offers as they flow ;
I do not ask, dear heart, for mistletoe.
To old delightful customs still I cling ;
They make life sweet; but when our high-keyed
hearts
Are tuned in Love’s sweet carnival to sing,
And all the dross of life' aside we fling,
We need no paltry cue to act our parts;
While lips to lips in happy passion go,
What is the need, dear heart, for mistletoe? •
N.S.W.
HAMER.
Business, Robbery, etc.
Australia has at least one sovereign that
hasn’t been borrowed. The unique coin was
dug up in a garden at Mudgee (N.S.W.)
lately and bears date 1782. It looks quite
young after its long rest —-and just as well,
for there’s plenty of work waiting for it.
When the Scaddan Government went fish-
ing off the Westralian coast it probably
reckoned on losing a sinker or two and a
couple of hooks; but Colebatcli (Colonial
Secretary) has discovered that it lost all
its tackle and its basket and then fell over-
board and was drowned. All the capital
invested (£6000) was provided without
Parliamentary authority by the issue of 4
p.c. Treasury bills, redeemable in two years
—and the time is up in six months. To
meet them there is a deficit of £7OOO, and
an Inspector of Fisheries who reports
gravely that “there is no prospect of im-
provement.” The little steamers that were
bought to bring the fish from Shark’s Bay
on ice were quite unsuitable, but the main
cause of the insolvency was that “fish can-
not be trusted to bite, within the regular
hours allotted to State employees.” A sort
of conspiracy, no doubt, to down the Labor
Government.
* ' * * *
“Webfoot”: Various Melbourne and sub-
urban Bungs have suffered by this lurk.
Shortly after five Q’clock, on Saturday for
choice, a shrewdie buys four bottles of ale of
a specified brand. A few minutes before 6
p.m., in the thick of the closing-time rush,
he hurries back with three bottles, explains
that the contents of the ‘fourth have been
drunk, and asks that the three be changed
for a trio of another brand. Bung accedes,
and later finds that the bottles for which the
new brand has been exchanged contain
water. Shrewdie has decanted them, re-
placed the capsules with skill, and trusted
to the rush to escape. Now that the gaff
has been blown the trick will lose its vogue,
but pubs in other places might keep their
eyes skinned.
Art is at a low ebb in Melbourne, and ser-
mons in stones are a drug in the market. A
Richmond constable found Horace Pearson
sitting on the pavement beside three pictures
upon which lay a meagre dole of pence. The
pictures, which were those of the usual pave-
ment artist, represented King George, Billy
Hughes, Andy Fisher and a fat mine-owner,
behind whom was a collier with a pick. Iu
reply to the Law, Horace said that he
couldn’t go to the war himself, so he had
drawn the pictures to induce others to go.
Apparently the Richmond lads showed no
disposition to exchange one form of fright-
fulness for the other, for on seeing Horace's
art they simply grinned and bore it. Being
doubtful whether a charge of being a recruit-
ing-sergeant without a license would lie,
John proceeded against Horace for having-
used the footpath for purposes of Art con-
trary to the local by-law, and it cost ss.
“Jobber” : Some hardcase Billjims hated to
spend real money when travelling through
Egypt. One of the best bluffers I met rushed
on the train, with a cobber who had a ticket,
at the last moment. When the collector
came through he produced a field-service post-
card, and the nigger examined it very closely.
When Bill thought his game was suspected
he grabbed the card and “roared the nigger
up,” threatening to report the matter to the
General. He dined on the train, on the
strength of an order on the G.O.C. for 30
“disasters.” Coming back an old typed leave-
pass put him right with the ticket-examiner,
but, unfortunately, he struck the same waiter
in the dining-car, and as he hadn’t any more
wastepaper with him he had to surrender.
Bill could cross Asia with a whisky label
and four cigarette cards.
*■/*■**
“Y.Z.”: People rarely think of picture-
buying as a good spec. Yet I know of one or
two private collections in Melbourne which
have trebled their value in a few years. I
saw a small lot of little Hilder pieces for the
first time the other day. The prices indi-
cated leaps and bounds from the modest
values the artist put on his stuff when alive ;
but even at the present rates one could buy
Hilders with the absolute certainty of the
lovely little paintings doubling and redoub-
ling their value in 10 years. Lindsay’s
water-color experiments with the human
figure soaked in sunshine are so individual
and so clever that —assuming Australia is to
be spared to the unfettered pursuit of her
art—they represent a better investment for a
rainy day than gold or precious stones. As
a mere man of business, I am thinking of
forming a syndicate to buy up all the N.L.
water-colors, and then commission a cheap
assassin to knock the artist admiringly on
the head. There is money in Australian art
tempered "with assassination.
* * * *
The fine distinction between a false state-
ment and a false promise was nicely drawn
in Sydney latelv. A man, using another
name, sent a telegram to himself, and on the
strength of the news it purported to contain
induced a Sydney publican to accept a post-
dated cheque for'£l2, and give him £3 4s. 6d.
change. When the cheque was presented on
the due date it was found to be an interest-
ing but valueless scrap of paper. Without
troubling the jury his Honor pointed out
that the intimation that the money would be
at the bank at a future date was not a false
statement, but a false promise, and there-
fore —being about something in the future —•
could not be a false pretence. Naturally the
publican was delighted. It was worth
£3 4s. 6d. of anybody’s money to see a Judge
do juggling tricks like that.
* * * *
“Nulla” : Glenbrook tunnel, on the West-
ern Line (N.S.W.), which has been scratched
off the railway map as a result of recent
deviations, hasn’t ceased to be a permanent
and reproductive work. A Japanese firm has
leased the hole in the Blue Mountains, and
will presently instal machinery for regulating
the temperature. Then the job of. mush-
room-culture will be started scientifically.
Writer has paid 4s. a pound for the fungus in
Sydney, and there should be money in the
business.
“O. K.” : Daylesford (Vic.), the once vol-
canic district which abounds in mineral
water and subterranean gases, is again dis-
cussing the question of the Hepburn' Spring
and the exploitation thereof by a much-mis-
trusted company. The spring that lost its
bearings some five years ago and was
thought to be gone forever is now a sud-
denly-returned wanderer, flowing through the
old original hidden waterway and gushing
from its rocky outlet at the rate of 300 gal-
lons an hour or more. Now, as heretofore,
the trouble is that the Hepburn Spring
doesn’t come directly into the Pavilion where
the public gets its free drinks, but is inter-
cepted by the precious co. which has a
bottling shanty at the back of the Pavilion,
and pays a paltry £BO a year rent to Dayles-
ford Town Council for the right of doing
what it pleases in the way of sinking wells
and laying traps to catch the natural gas
from the spring ere it reaches the public
outlets. Hepburn folk, and visitors who
flock to the Pavilion, get the water in fact
not by the grace of God, but by grace of a
mysterious band of exploiters. And the
Daylesford district naturally objects to get-
ting its gift from God after a gang of mostly-
politicians have sweated a dividend out of it.
“McNorth”: A Queensland regulation
says a selector may not take up a sea-
frontage exceeding in length two-thirds of
the depth of his lease. I said this was a
handicap to cocoa nut-growing '; whereat
“D.C.8.” scoffed (B. 19/10/T6), pointing
out that as the palms flourish a hundred
miles from the coast in Ceylon and other
places, proximity to the sea is not an im-
portant matter. I don’t deny that cocoa-
nuts will flourish inland. In fact, I believe
that the importance attached to the saline
air is much over-rated. But that’s not the
point. A feature of the littoral of Northern
Australia is a belt of fertile scrub-land
facing the ocean, with a heap of useless
country behind. This back country is gener-
ally. swampy and sour, and would certainly
not grow cocoanuts. That being so, it is
ok/ious that a regulation compelling the
would-be planter to take up a lot of land
utterly useless for his purpose is a serious
drag to an industry that people are just
beginning to inquire about. The pioneer
planters don’t want to select away inland
when suitable soil can be obtained near the
beach. With a wise land policy a great
future lies before the copra industry in the
Far North, I am sure. The trees bear
better, and often sooner, than in any other
part of the world. I am in the business, and
I know.
* * * *
“F.W.W.’V The time-payment system for
agricultural machinery ought to be objection-
able but it has its advantages. “Well, my
boy,” says old Harrow, “it’s this way. If I
pay cash, the bloomin’ agent don’t know me
again till he thinks it’s near wore out, and if
it goes wrong I can yell as hard as I like for
an expert without gettin’ him. But, if I
pay a deposit an’ the thing breaks or jibs, I
just go to the agent an’ tell him that if an
expert ain’t along quick an’ lively I’ll drag
his bloomin’ old rattletrap back into the
yard an’ get a new one somewhere else. If
you can talk like that, there’ll be an expert
going out every time with you. The bit
extra you pay for terms is a good insurance.”
# * * *
John Higgins, the Melbourne auditor who
holds the scales between the Government
and the Prahran city council, discovered this
item while making his annual tour of in-
spection : “Motor-vehicles for funeral,
£6 10s.” In the same stack of vouch-
ers was another interesting record:
“Addresses to ex-councillors, £21.” Both
impudent claims on the ratepayers’ purses
were promptly thrown out; so the
kindly Victorian councillor-soul who wants
to mourn with those that mourn, either by
going to the funeral in a motor-car or by
presenting an address over a glass of hiccup,
will in future have to be a kindly soul on a
smaller scale.

A POET’S FRIGHTFULNESS.
[For The Bulletin.]
Full often in the past the wolf
Had howled outside my door—
A thing that it is wont to do
To rhyming wights and others, Who
Both lowly are and poor.
Upon reprisals I resolved,
And got a notion cute.
Its visits now are rare indeed,
For when it comes I promptly read -
Some verses to the brute.
Victoria. HARRISON O.
THE VOICE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ever he passes my door;
Ever in pain he goes' by—
Passes and sobs, and no more.
Oh, but I shrink from that cry 1
Fainter and fainter it grows;
Wailing he goes on his way —-
Wails that now beat me like blows.
Ne’er at my door does he stay.
What are the words he repeats,
Sung like a dirge to the dead,
Echoing down the long streets,
Throbbing like pain through my head?
What is the boon that they crave—•
Always those words, and no more;
Hollow, like sounds from the grave;
Echoing in at my door?
Must he go on with his rote?
Plaintively moan till Death stops,
Choking the cry in his throat,
With a last gurgling: “Props ! Clothes
Props !”
N.S.W.

All traffic laws, rules and regulations are becoming
more rigid every day.
This is as it should be. Safety first.
All the more reason for having the best speedometer
on your car - the Stewart Speedometer.
Without one you unconsciously break the law,
because no motorist can possibly estimate the speed of his
car without a speedometer.
Unless you do drive at a speed within the law, you
are liable to the embarrassment of arrest, the humiliation
of a trial and the possibility of a heavy fine.
Do not smile. It might happen to you any day, as
it has to thousands of others.
This magnetic speedometer is especially built for
Ford and other small-type cars.
Price only £3/10/- complete.
Priced low on account of our great production.
