OF ALL
DEALERS.
rs
POLITICAL
POINTS
One of the first things done by the
“National” Government of N. S. Wales —
the Government which says that this is no
time for party warfare and that the win-
ning of the war must be put first—was to
decide to compete in the market against the
war loans. The other States have left
their borrowings in the hands of the Com-
monwealth. The “National” Government of
the Nark State refused to do anything of
the sort on the ground that if it goes on its
own it will probably be able to grab a mil-
lion or two which might otherwise be sub-
scribed for war purposes. There is “patri-
otism” for you !
Hughes talks rubbish when he rails at the
Senate for dislocating the fiscal arrange-
ments of the Government and causing the
country to lose £50,000 in revenue—“and
this in war time.” What the Senate did was
to squelch an artful device by which the
Government was putting practically the
whole weight of the increase in income-tax
on to the smaller incomes, increasing the tax
on the £4OO man by 100 per cent., while an
income of £2OOO would pay only 25 per cent,
more. In preventing this inequity the
Senate was as much within its rights as
Hughes was in employing the majority which
follows him in the Reps, to perpetrate it.
And if the Senate by preventing a Govern-
ment from looting the less-affluent' taxpayer
catises it to lose £50,000, the answer is that
the tragedy is of Ministers’ own making.
THE SILVER BULLET.
SMITH (talking about the war): “Oh, well,
you'll find it's money makes the mare to
go!"
ROBINSON (who has just scored a had loss) :
“Not when your last few bob are on her,
old boy."
George Reid solemnly drowned the last
hair of the dry-dog the other day in London.
The Freetraders, he admitted, had no longer
even the dry bones of an argument to feed
the beast on.
Three or four months ago the talk in a
Maorilander’s dug-out turned on the chances
of getting a bit of land in Australia “after-
wards” ; and a letter was sent to The
Bulletin asking whether Billjim of Maori-
land would come in level with Billjim of
Australia iii any allotment of Australian
land. The Bulletin -didn’t know ; neither
did anybody else at the time. But the
Repatriation Committee got to work to find
out what the various Cabinets thought about
it, and now desires to inform this paper
“that the Governments of Vic., Queensland
and Westralia are prepared to offer to dis-
charged soldiers of other dominions the privi-
leges which will be extended to their own
men in regard to land settlement.” Which
seems to mean that the Government of the
old nark State of N. S. Wales is not pre-
pared to do anything of the kind.
DURING THE RAINS.
FIRST COVE : “Floods do any damage to
your place?"
SECOND COVE: “ Dunno! Ain't found it
yet.”
Once more. Is the Repatriation Fund
which the politicians talk of every now
and again mere bait on their hook? If it
isn’t, how comes it that the Returned Sol-
diers’ Association of N. S. Wales finds it
necessary to make statements like this:—
It is hard to realise that there are hundreds of
returned soldiers, many of whom saw last Christmas
in the trenches, who this year will find it hard to
secure a Christmas dinner. Yet such is the case....
We can vouch for the fact that there are hundreds
of our members out of work through no fault of
their own who have neither money nor credit. These
are genuine cases.
If the Repatriation Fund is the mere bait
of the politician, what has the Australia
Day Fund got to say for itself? Until this
matter has been gone into and satisfactorily
disposed the recruiting-committee might
just as well shut its doors. There is going
to be no recruiting worth tuppence so long
as it . is. possible for a Returned Soldiers’
Association, under the hand of the Dean
of Sydney/ to declare that hundreds of its
members, out of work, out of money, out'of
credit, didn’t lfnow where to turn for a
Christmas dinner.
The intensely patriotic N. S. Wales Gov-
ernment regards it as a mere idle rumor that
wool is grown in Australia and blankets are
made here. Hence early visitors to the new
and elaborate accommodation-house at Jenolan
Caves were not surprised to find themselves
under blankets bearing the legend: “Wool
and cotton —made in Japan.” Incidentally
the Government is wondering what is to
become of the 1200 men who have just been
sacked from unfinished N. S. Wales railway
works because there is no more loan money
to pay them with.
It is now suggested that a Commission be
appointed to inquire into the Northern Terri-
tory. Certainly! In fact, whenever a man
is appointed to a Government job a Commis-
sion should be also appointed to start out on
his trail 10 minutes after he has got off the
mark. Two men, in short, for each job: one
to do It, and the other to tell why it hasn’t
been done.
J. H. Cann, Minister for Works in the
Holman Government before the Grab, and
a miner before that, fell out of the Leg.
Assembly last week into the job of drawing
£l2OO a year as a N.S.W. Railway Commis-
sioner. It is one of the worst scandals ever
perpetrated in Australia. Sydney Telegraph
sorrowfully remarks that the “experiment
has to justify itself by results.” There are
no results that can justify so flagrant an
outrage, upon every principle which should
govern public appointments.
Lloyd George to the House of Commons:
The cost of the war is increasing. We must be
careful of every pound.
Of course, lie means every pound that is left
after Australia has had' what it wants tor
building ornamental lakes at Canberra and
monkey-cages and merry-go-rounds at the new
Sydney Zoo.
Amongst the delegates to the Inter-State
Labor Conference which met the other day to
__ .. L.. tliA nlof-
make repairs to the plat-
form that’ the dingoes
had bitten there was
only one politician (Sen-
ator Jupp Gardiner)
from N. S. -Wales and
none from Victoria.
Queensland sent two
legislators amongst its
bunch, South Aus. and
Westralia three each,
and Tasmania five. In
the biggest States Labor
has revolted against the
domination of the poli-
tician. and now insists
on keeping him merely
as a machine to register
the views of the move-
ment in Parliament. But
a more direct and less
degrading method would
be to make a far wider
use of the initiative and
referendum, leaving the
politician—much reduced
in numbers not a
machine to register
views, but an expert to
settle details.
The leader of Vic-
toria’s new Economy
Party is the quaintly-
named Bowser, represen-
tative of Wangaratta.
Bowser’s ideas on re-
trenchment can be gauged
by his Parliamentary
statement that the coun-
try gets a fair return
from the money spent on
the Government Printing
Office—the institution which sends out tons
of never-read papers every year. Not that
that is all the fault of the Printing Office.
The twaddling inefficients who control the
Departments are almost wholly responsible
for that.
N.S.W. Billions Club, in anticipation of the
nation being someday plunged into peace, is
bombarding Hughes with exhortations “to
give the question of immigration his earnest
attention.” But there are at least two other
questions which come first. Talk about
immigration can be nothing but blither until
the conditions are such that when the immi-
grant arrives he can either get (1) a job or
(2) easy access to land at a price within
the reach of the kind of man who immigrates.
As Australia says it has to borrow cash in
order to give the father of the local kid a
job, while it is hopelessly fumbling with the
problem of settling a few thousands of its
own soldiers on the land, nothing is solved
by merely chattering about immigration.
Amongst other things lost sight of in the
war turmoil may be included the Inter-State
Commission. The entrance to its magnilo-
quently styled “Court” in Albert-street, East
Melbourne, is festooned with cobwebs. There
is the usual staff of clerks and messen-
gers on full salaries, but it is comatose, dor-
mant, doped. Mr. Commissioner Piddington
has spent much time writing to the papers
giving advice about peace terms; Mr. Com-
missioner Lockyer has melted into thin air ;
Mr. Commissioner Swinburne helps the
Federal Treasurer to discriminate between
industrial good things and mining wild
cats. Meantime the country spends many
thousands a year on itj and the best proof
that the money is utterly wasted is that,
after the Commission has been in existence
nearly four years, Australia now spends
more money on foreign goods than ever it
did.
At last week’s meeting of Melbourne
Working Men’s College it was asserted that
some of the boys at the Junior 'lechnica
School cannot afford to pay for their books.
The question is: Can they get them without
paying? Surely no aspiring work-boy is
allowed to miss any of the technical educa-
tion he is seeking for want of the price ot
school-books!
Cabled from Berlin last week that the
Socialist organ Y'orwaerts violently attacks
Premier Massey, of Maoriland, for prolong-
ing the war. Poor, inoffensive old Bill, who
never in his life said anything more truculent
than that the Empire would stand shoulder
to shoulder, and John Bull could have all
the cheese he wanted from the Taranaki
cockies at BM. a pound!
How curiously alike, in some respects,
present conditions are to those of 25 years
ago. Then, as now, the disappointed under-
dog, who had been previously told that there
was not going to be any under-dog, rebelled
against his political bosses. Then it was
the Labor party that was created to dis-
place the old Radicals'; to-day it is the
Labor party which, having been corrupted,
has to go through the fire. Further, there
was then a small crowd which could see no
hope in old methods, just as there is the
I.W.W. to-day. After all, it isn’t fair to
assume from the existence of the I.W.W.
that the working-man is getting more des-
perately wicked. Apparently, judging by
the semi-hysterical measures of men who
were Labor leaders till the other day, these
people think there never was such an out-
burst before. But there was. In 1892
this proclamation was pretty widely circu-
lated among the dockers and others of Lon-
don :—•
To carry out our aims brutal force is absolutely in-
dispensable. It is absolutely necessary to burn the
churches, palaces, convents, barracks and prisons and
to destroy without mercy all who are opposed to us.
Let us occupy ourselves with chemistry. Let us
manufacture bombs, dynamite and other high explo-
sives, which are more efficacious than guns, to bring
about, the destruction of our enemies and spare the
blood of our comrades. Call us criminals, vermin,
call us what you will, shower your hard names upon
us, but do not be surprised if your houses are shat-
tered with dynamite, and then when we strike at you-
without ruth and without mercy do not whine for
pity, for it will be useless.
Then the Labor party arrived to give the
under-dogs new hope; and the doctrine of
despair went to sleep for another 20 years
or so.

RESURRECTION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
In perfect order still they come,
With, play of fife and roll of drum,
Like irises their flags abound ;
Fresh leaping from the bloody ground,
The stern-eyed soldiers, cold and mute,
Come on, so young, so resolute.
And ever as the front ranks fade
New armies from the vapor made
Rise up behind, as shadows may,
Grow straight and solid for the fray.
Where innocents were slain in flight
Battalions ripen in the night.
The Kaiser turned. Again he saw
The victims of his bestial law,
The red blood of his sacrifice,
The thrice-stabbed sire, the trampled wife;
And whispei'ing, “Mein Gott,” he said,
“See how prolific are the dead!”
VICTORIA.
EDWARD DYSON.
THE GREAT GOD RENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
The garden paths around the city rise,
The asters bloom on many an ordered lawn
Beneath the cobalt blue of generous skies,
And warmed by sunbeams from the gates of dawn;
Wide orchards yield a fruitage rich and rare,
And fairest gifts by Nature’s skill are sent....
And haggard women gaunt and worn are there,
Wan sacrifices to the great god Rent.
The snow-winged yachts in conscious swiftness steer
Along the silver of the moving tide;
Plumed with grey smoke, the ferry-boats appear,
Crossing with tireless keels the waters wide ;
Fair are the white-bayed shores and coves as though
The spirit of beauty dwelt there, well content....
And here are lanes of hovels, drab and low,
The sordid altars of the great god Rent.
In all the .world there is no fairer place
Than this broad city by the harbor deep,
Where Nature clothes her in her rarest grace
And Beauty loves her festal days to keep!
Sure in the world there is no fairer place! ■
But here, aye even here, Hell finds a vent
And lowers and scowls on many a sullen face
Where helpless bond-slaves feed the great god Rent.
TASMANIA.

Why Look Old?
You need not use hair dye to conceal grey hair.
Grey hair adds years to anyone’s appear-
ance. The objections to dyeing the hair are
too numerous and too obvious to mention.
Few people are aware that a simple, old-
fashioned recipe, that can be made up at
home, will quickly turn grey hair back to a
natural color without injuring the hair in
any way. One has only to get two ounces
of concentrate of tammalite from the chemist
and mix it with three ounces of bay rum.
With a small sponge apply the lotion to the
hair for a few nights and the desired change
will have been neatly accomplished. This
simple formula has given great satisfac-
tion for generations to those in possession of
it. —Advt. [°

NOTIONS
Results of the New Year’s meeting at
Flemington next Monday are not unlikely to include
some striking reversals of the form at Caulfield on
Boxing Day. The whole lot of entrants for the Ilope-
toun Cup at the V.A.T.C. meeting are available for
the Bagot Handicap at the V.R.O. fixture. And most
of the starters for the Standish Handicap flutter will
have been seen out on Boxing Day. It is safer to
expect reversals rather than endorsements of Christ-
mas-holiday form, but there is no fixed rule on the
subject.
* * * *
It’s good to huve a few bob on a nag
when it wins as Balarang did the A.J.C. Highweight
Handicap on Saturday. He started favorite, and all
the afternoon was touted a good tiling provided he
condescended to leave the barrier smartly. It came
off right enough. Balarang- hopped out, and before
a furlong had gone was sailing along in front. The
further the others went the further Balarang left
them behind. He won pulling up by eight lengins,
having carved out the six furlongs in lmin. 12.[sec.
—good going with Bst. 111 b. in the saddle. This
was the nag’s first win since a Ilawkesbury meeting
nearly 12 months ago, and the manner in which
he galloped on Saturday suggested that he might
secure anything he happened to be turned loose after.
* * * *
The Robinson stable at Randwick seems
to always have a horse capable of winning a race.
Tony, winner of the A.J.C. Trial Handicap, owned and
trained by Robinson, was not fancied much and
started at 10 to 1. Incidentally he downed Sam
Hordern’s Sir Paddington by a head. Tony won
at Moorefield a few weeks back, so that by this
time he has partly made up for previous failures
when solidly backed. This particular race was a
sad sight for punters. Black Nutjla red-hot favorite,
was left standing at the post apd''beat only a couple
home. '** '
* « * *
A fortnight ago this page advised punters
not to forget that Wedding Day was as compact a.
little hit of horse as had been seen lately in these
parts. On Saturday at Randwick the lady appro-
priated A.J.O. Viliiers Stakes from a fair field.
After going a furlgng she was second last, and was
not sighted at the, bottom of the straight. But from
there on she commenced putting in tremendously long
strides and hit the front 50 yards from home. Had
Royal Laddie, who ended up secdlid, won, Ike would
have hurled his liat over the grandstand in delight,
for the Laddie’s noinina.l price vvas 100 to 1 and
no takers. The winder was at 15’s. Most of the
cash went on Carine, who got a bad passage and
finished fifth. 7
* * * *
After many days and many tries last
year’s three-year-old boom, Wallace Isinglass, came
ito light in the Grandstand Stakes on Saturday, and
3 to 1 was the best the crowd could get on the
animal on whom they had lost, money' consistently.
Wallace carried 9st. 51b. and had a rather ordinary
field to beat. He had to be ridden right but to get
ahead of- The Fortune Hunter, and that is an animal
which up to the present no one has won a fortune
with. The Stakes running brought Wallace Isinglass
into much demand for the Summer Clip, a race
in which the public frequently stumbles over its
feet during the rush to Israel. And this time Wal-
lace didn't win again. The Hunter arrived first.
* - * * *
Some of the horses had none of the luck
(ill the first day of the A.J.C. meeting. In the
Nursery Handicap the Fortafix filly Fortuna was
backed down to 2 to 1 in a field of 22, and the
public stood ready to sprint towards Ikey and collect.
But they didn’t do it. Rounding the turn Fortuna
came down and gave light-weight Dempsey a had
shaking. The filly was so much damaged that she
will never race again. In the Trial Handicap the
Denison-owned mare Australienne, winner of a race
at Warwick Farm a. week before, turned a seven
and had to be shot. Queerly enough both animals
suffered the same injury—a smash of the near knee.
It is an unusual kind of accident at any time; a
double on the same afternoon is uncanny.
* _ * •'* *
A casual inquiry as to the biggest weight
ever carried on a racecourse has. set the London sport-
ing papers digging up records. All recent figures
were soon overshadowed, and it becomes a question
of sheer cruelty to animals, perpetrated by 19th
century “sports.” Owners up, catch-weights, over a
course —sometimes with fences —was the usual form
of the outrage. For a time, Sir John Astley's 16st.
61b. seemed to hold top place. His horse, Drumhead,
was never any good again, says one writer. Quite
wrong, says another ; it did some good racing after
that, but broke down during another “match” with
16st. 101 b. up. And now someone has discovered
that, about 1860, an 18st. owner named Malcolmsoh
was taken on by Captain Macliell and another, who,
to make a match against his 18st., agreed both to
ride on one horse. They scaled together 22st. 51b.,
put an unhappy horse over a two-mile course under
that cruel burden, and won (of course) a “substantial
sum.”
* * * *
Has anyone heard a proprietary racing-
club or Ikey or anyone like that moaning because
daylight saving will root them out of bed an hour
earlier? Under this scheme it will be light in the
vicinity of midsummer until nearly nine o’clock o’
nights, and if that doesn’t result in the breeding
of mokes being given greater encouragement by
tacking a race or two on to present programmes —•
well it ought to. There would seem now to be
nothino- to prevent tlje pony crowd having .25 races
per day and running little evening scrambles from
5 to 8 30 —say a dozen events —in between times.

“Mujik”: It is the silliest thing in the
world to imagine that the punter is going to find the
winner any more frequently when he drops the elu-
sive quid on the tote that when he handed it to
Ike. The best he can look for is that when he does
discover the animal the cash return will be gieatei
than it would be if he made a successful bet with
Israel. When he locates a loser the quid is gone m
either case. Among Maoriland punters the most
agonising moments of life are when they drop coin
on Hardpull while the machine shows about 10 to 1,
and after about 10 minutes ecstasy discover that
the multitude is on the same lurk and the wretched
animal is being reduced to 6 to 4 on, or something-.
Then are they terrified lest the owner may repent of
his determination to have & cut; wherefore they
dash in to save the situation by backing Scooter,
and when Hardpull lands the bacon and they collect
a divvy of £1 ss. and balance the debit and
credit, their moaning is like the dirge of many
ravens.
* » * *
“Trot”; Yes, a book can keep on layin’
’em at tote odds till the cows come home, but only
on condition that he sees the machine. Otherwise
he must put a limit on the price or get blown sky-
high. In s.p. transactions depending on the tote
the backer’s object in life is to deter anyone from
putting a bean on his nag in the machine, for every
one that goes on reduces his prospective win from
the bookmaker by lessening the div. But Ike’s
. interests are in another direction altogether. He
wants to see as many quids as the machine will hold
dumped on to the animal he has a bet against.
Therefore when the owner of Blue Murder distributes
a couple of hundred among the obliging Ikes under
this system, he goes about miserably shaking his
head and remarking that his nag is suffering- from
blind staggers or something. Ike, however, circu-
lates the glad tidings far and wide that Blue Murder
is out for keeps, and the mqre ears which hearken
the- narrower the odds become and the safer the
bookie’s risk. Working inside 10 to 1 it is a nice
comfortable sort of business, like holding shares in
a brewery.
But conducting- operations on the full-dividend
scale is as dangerous as looking down a volcano.
The machine has a mean habit of occasionally
announcing that £ll7 is payable on something, and
• any prad that MaeShortodds lay 116 to 1
against would have to be acccgnpauie'd; by the sworn
declaration of a vet. that get out of a
walk; also it would have to he ridden by Ike’s
brother-in-law while Ike minded his purse. Now,
given a smart horse, a sufficiently lugubrious owner,
a remote meeting with the s.p. books a long- way
off and all the money which knew anything diverted
from the machine to Ike, a div. of £ll7 is an easy
thing to manufacture. But if Ike stood for ramps
like that he would discover before a week was over
that someone had torn the best end of half a million
from him and - That the Promised Land was b.eing
devoured by caterpillars.

“Nomad” : In 1878 I rode a liorse bred by
Halls, of Dartbrook, near Aberdeen (N.S.W.), from
Tamworth to Barraba and hack to Attunga station,
in 14 hours, including one hour’s rest at Barraba.
I left Tamworth at 6 a.m. and arrived at Attunga
at 8 p.m. -the same evening'. Total distance, 108
miles. Tijiie vehicle was purely grass-fed.

'Jacli Juuesse parted with the bantam
championship on Saturday, and the decoration is
now worn by Vince Blackburn, of Balmain (Syd-
ney). The Victorian wandered north to interview
Blackburn on the subject a fortnight ago, and at
the end of a Monday-night disturbance the Stadium
referee gave judgment for usthe defendant. But _it
was such a close thing: arid the biff so scientific
that the pair were matched again for the end of
last weisk. They provided one -. of the best battles
ever seen at the Stadium —fast, hard and clean.
The Sydney boy changed his tactics this time and
carried - war to the enemy trenches from the start.
Janesse met the assault with a pair of beautifully-
educated gloves and nimble feet. He is a rare good
boxer and a fine strategist, but Blackburn carried
t6o much ginger and punched his way to victor,'.
Compared with some of the displays given by
lumbering individuals of greater tonnage the en-
counter between this pair of game-cocks was the
champagne of stoush.

Mexican Benny Palmer started a revolu-
tion at Brisbane on Saturday with Ferdinand Quen-
dreaux as the opposition party. The Frenchman kept
something in reserve till the thirteenth clash ; then
he emerged with heavy artillery. Palmer kept plod-
ding away with straight lefts and, with 48sec. _to
go to knoeking-off time, collided with something-
leaden. That was the end of the revolution, and
mighty hard luck for Palmer, who had had the best
of every round.
’*-***
Darcy reached New York last week and it
is cabled that the vessel he travelled on was met
by six tugs in the vicinity of the Statue of Liberty,
and that six pug managers megaphoned their yells
about contracts. If Darcy didn’t forget to take
his grin with him he probably put it on when all
this noise smote the atmosphere. As it is stated
that he “went to Tex Rickard’s” headquarters there
’ seems reason to suppose that Tex had the loudest
yell. Anyhow, Rickard has been working up fights
for a long time and is probably able to offer most
money for a scrap.
* * * *
So when* Darcy fell into New York lie
was “reticent about his method of leaving Aus-
tralia.” In the name of Heaven, why ? Everyone in
the game knows how he got away, and what it cost ;
also, they can all tell the story of the honest indivi-
dual who pushed into the proceedings at the last
minute and earned a bit by threatening to give the
game away and yell for the Hops. How Darcy got
away over the back fence is of less importance than
why he chose that method of making a scoot.
* * * *
“J.J.C.” : Talking of walks, Jacky Ireland,
now town-crier of Gympie (Q.), strolled from
Cympie to Maryborough, about 67 miles, in one day,
and returned a day later. He did not consider the
matter of much moment. I met Malachi Ryan out-
side Camooweal en route for Pine Greek. He had
been for a jaunt to see his brother in Rockhampton,
and had done the round trip per hobnail express and
caught a lot of his tucker en route. Six hundred
miles or more, I should say. Next best was the
unknown Labor chap in Far West N. S. Wales who
some years ago walked from down near Menindie to
Bourke “to vote for Hughie Langwell.” Three hun-
dred miles or thereabouts that was.
* * * *
The hungry Australian bike-pushers are
still cleaning up the slate in Yankeeland, the more
recent success being in the American all-round cham-
pionship, in which R. J. McNamara (N.S.W.), Alf
Goullet (Vic.) and A. Grenda (Tas.) filled the places.
This wound up the Newark racing season. Con-
tinental and American riders scarcely picked up a
crust.
* * * *
The Bourke Cup, a race instituted by the
Tamar Rowing Club in ’B7, and carrying the Schools’
Championship of Tasmania, was won this year by
the Launceston State School No. 1 crew. Devon-
port High School was second. This was the first
year of the race in which no private schools com-
peted. The State High Schools (established by the
late. Premier Solomon) entered the race only four
years ago, and probably no one would have objected
to them rowing while they continued to tail the
other crews home. But the Launceston High School
crew had the cheek to win last year and add insult
to injury by going to Hobart and winning the
Clarke Challenge Cup. This is more than self-
respecting private schools could stand, and, as the
Tamar Rowing Club refused to bar the State schools,
Private Enterprise got the spike, instituted a race on
its own and hung out the imported title “Head of
the River” as a prize.
* * * *
Hooray! A beak at Wellington (M.L.)
has refused to fine anyone for playing golf on a
municipal reserve on Sunday. The view he took
was that the by-law framed by Bumble to restrain
the local citizen advancing to the tee with his kit
of ironmongery on the Sabbath morn was an unreas-
onable interference with the right of private judge-
ment. Meanwhile Stiggins is talking about taking
up a collection for an appeal to the Privy Council
and praying hard that the sinner may be bunkered
and the caddie vanish with the implements of
wrath.
.** * *
Cricket is dying rapidly in Westralia, but
a competition drags painfully onward and occa-
sionally'something happens. On the first two Satur-
days in December North Perth made 179, and dis-
missed Midland Swan for 74. In the follow-on
sundries made 11 of the 32 placed in the scorebook.
The machine-gunner of the attack was Hughes, who
took 6 for 28 and 5 for 10, clean-bowling all his
victims. Christian, who was prominent in Victorian
cricket 20 years ago, fathers North Perth. He
dashed up 76 (helped to put up 100 in 37 minutes
at one stage) and took 4 for 35 and 3 for 11. Le
Couteur, the Victorian Rhodes scholar, who did
things for Oxford and the Gents of England, bats
and bowls for the local University eleven.

The brightest fish story of the season
comes from down south. Three Port Melbourne
anglers had their lines out from a 20ft. yacht
three miles off Point Cook. One of the party was
in the act of hauling in something big, when a shark-
made a dash for it. Probably annoyed at missing
the fish the shark struck the yacht with such force
that its snout made a hole in both the outer and
inside lining of the boat and then swam awav
to nurse its bruised proboscis. The hole was
plugged with a bag and the craft was taken to
Point Cook and run ashore. If anybody doubts the
yarn there’s the hole in the boat to prove it.
* * * *
Blown across the desert, a scrap of paper
fluttered into the tent of Captain John Kendall, of the
Australian Army Veterinary Corps, attached to the
Anzac Mounted Division (Chauvel’s crowd) in Egypt.
On that paper was Defence-Minister Pearce’s praise
of Australian horses —and for once Pearce was record-
ing the opinions of. somebody who knew what he was
talking about. The come-b.v-chance text has inspired
Kendail to write home a fine sermon. “For sheer
grit and endurance,” he says, “and for. quick recovery
after long marches, the Australian horse is far
superior to the British or Canadian. I have to super-
vise 11,0'CK) horses and mules and about 4000 camels.
One of our good, tough,’ bony-looking old nags is
ecii.al to any two of tne others. We were at times
36 hours without water, and then had only one drink,
very salty, and another long stretch. After these
trips one would see the horses of a complete unit
looking like hunted devils —completely exhausted. Yet
softer a good drink and feed they not only recovered
but brought, the men home. Our men are not light
fellows, and I fully anticipated that 3 per cent, of
the horses would have to be destroyed, that 10 per
cent, would die, and that 30 per cent, would have to
be sent to hospital, but I could find only about
eight horses per battery and 20 per regiment which
required attention. With all the system and particu-
lar mating of mares with sires which prevail in Eng-
land, our draughts, light draughts and gun horses are
admired by everybody, and have held their condition
the best. Of course’, the thoroughbred is needed in
the troop-horse, but the stout, rough cob is the lad to
fatten up quicklv again. With very little choosing of
matqs we can produce in Australia any kind of army
horse in larger numbers and cheaper than most coun-
tries. Mules, after this war, must come to stay in
Australia, for we have plenty of mares which will
breed good mules : and the mule can lick the horse
out of sight for everything except gun work. In guns
he is not so easily handled in teams when turning.
But mules are the’best packs in the world.”
“Murkah”: “Hay’s’’ fence-smashing ’roo

“Bad Australian”: Another unusual
shooting experience. I was hunting for beef for a
crowd of “hoysenas,” as the poisoner called them, and
any of you who have lived for a solid month on
wallaby-tail stew ' with spuds and wonga, black
cockatoo and lyre-bird will understand the applica-
tion of the title. Presently I saw a couple of old
bulls and a mixed mob of 20 cows and young
cattle about half a mile away. They were as wild
as hawks, and after losing them twice I came across
them jogging down a long ridge about 600 yards
away. As they were making for the hop scrub and
brush on the creek bottom, and remembering the
state of mind of the “hoysenas,” I chanced the long
shot at a young cow. The only apparent effect
was an immediate stampede of the whole
outfit; but after going about 200 yards the cow-
stopped and began to turn slowly round and round,
and then sat down on her haunches, exactly as a
dog does, and started walking around herself with her
forelegs, using the butt of her tail as a pivot. When
I had administered the knock-out I found that my
first bullet had just chipped the top of the backbone
about six inches forward of the coupling, leaving the
spinal cord exposed. An inch higher, and it would
have been a miss.
On another occasion I was out with a double gun
—one barrel shot, the other ball-cartridge—when a
full-grown kangaroo hopped out of the bracken and
squatted on a ridge 80 or 90 yards off. I
snapped from the shoulder, and the Too leaped
sft. into the air, landed on his ear or thereabouts,
rebounded like a punchball, and set off as if he just
hated to be there at all. I picked up his tracks, and
found that he had been hit pretty severely. He left
gouts of blood on the dry fern, sticks and gum-leaves
at every jump. To my amazement I found several
small spots of brain or spinal matter mixed with the
gouts of blood. I followed the tracks until the blood-
spots cut out, and then went home to think it out.
That incident occurred about 20 years ago, and I
have tackled the problem at intervals since. Every
pal I have told the tale to has intimated that I am
a record liar. But the other day I caught a new-
chum English Johnnie, and after the tale had soaked
in he said: “Haw, jusso. But I weally see nothin
extwaovdinawy in that, y’ know, old chappie. Those
bally mawsupials of youahs cawwy theah young in
a pouch or something, don’t y’ know. It must have
been the bwain of the pwogeny which led y’ astway,
deah chappie. The mothah pwobably suffahed little
injury. What!” He had hit it in one.
I was out once with a new .450 sporting Martini,
and saw a fine “old man” on a ridge about 120
yards away. I dropped down and took a lean over a
big log, aimed and pulled. Nothing happened. Then
I suddenly remembered the safety-catch, which was
a new contraption to me. I lifted my eye off the
sights and tipped the safety-catch with my thumb.
I was using a very light pull, almost “hair-trigger,
and the jar of the sudden release of the safetv-catch
loosed her off as if I had pulled the trigger. Incredi-
ble as it would appear if anyone else was telling the
yarn, the ball struck the “old man” in the pelvis,
arid the subsequent etcetera interested him no more.
After that experience I cut the hair-trigger out for
good. _____
The Tasmanian House of Dodery treated
McDonald’s Referendum Bill as a lamb led,
in for slaughter: sixteen to one was the
vote. In fact, on calling for a division.
McDonald had to be his own teller. About the
only argument employed against the Bill was
that the first use the people would make of
the Referendum would be to abolish the
Upper House, and what would the Empire
do without its Dodery?

Miss Gladys Cooper Explains
Some Beauty Secrets.
The Popular London Star Favors Simple Methods.
I have been asked to give a few simple recipes that I know, eithei the
use or by observation, to be valuable to the toilet, and which are wi expensive
average woman. In these days of £lOO facial treatments and elaoo ' are
beautifying processes my suggestions may read like lessons in econo y, e ‘ vno te
not especiallv so intended. They are merely practical suggestions, m w already
is “effectiveness.” All the materials or ingredients which I mention aie either a a y
at hand in the home or may be readily procured from the chemist. E ortunately lao
suffer from the. ailments or troubles enumerated below, but some people wh
fortunate have told me their experiences, and with your permission I will se
remedies which they have found to be efficacious.
Home-made Hair Tonic.
My acquaintances say that a good stimulant
to the natural growth of the hair is boranium.
A very simple, effective and safe stimulating
lotion made up by mixing one ounce of bora-
nium with \ pint- of bay rum. This may be
applied to the hajr roots occasionally with the
finger-tips with good results.
Complexion Renewals.
Complexion experts advise me that a normal,
healthy complexidn is constantly renewing it-
self by dropping off tiny flakes of worn-out
tissue, thus revealing the fresh young skin
underneath. They say that when this process
is checked by age, expo-
sure or some oi many
causes, the complexion
becomes dull and ugly.
The rational treatment
recommended is to help
the skin perform the
natural functions of
“shedding” worn-out tis-
sue. For this purpose, I
am informed, there is
nothing so good as pure
mercolized wax, used for
a few nights, just as you
would use a face cream.
It is claimed that it pos-
sesses a special affinity
for the effete scarf-skin,
which it uuickly removes
by absorbing it. The face.
I am assured, will soon
look much younger and
preuier under this treat-
ment.
The Curling Iron.
Don’t use a hot iron to
curl your hair. Some of
my friends make the cun-
ningest sort of curls
wherever they want
them simply by damp-
ing the hair with liquid
siimerine before retiring
at night. When the hair is dry in the morn-
ing it will be softly curly just where you
want it to he. This method is perfectly harm-
less, even beneficial to the hair, and the curls
last a long time. The liquid is quite pleasant
and neither sticky nor greasy.
How to Shampoo.
Most women, I am informed, do not know
how to use stallax properly when shampooing
with it. Unless the hair is naturally very
oily, a stallax shampoo may sometimes leave
it rather dry. But I am told if you will apply
olive oil freely to your 1. nr and scalp just
before shampooing with stallax, the result is
most delightful. The hair will be left clean,
soft, bright and wavy, the olive oil having
properly balanced the action of this wonderful
hair cleanser..
Is Powder Necessary?
A great many women object to using pow-
der, for various reasons. The following
formula is a good one: Dissolve an ounce of

Photo Wrather <Ss Buy.
eleminite in four tablespoonfuls of water, or
witch-hazel, and use it as a face lotion,
smoothing the skin with the fingers until it
is drv. This method is perfectly harmless.
I am "told that a really beautiful, natural vel-
vety bloom results and remains for many hours
quite unaffected by the most trying conditions
out of doors or in the ballroom or theatre,
and that it gives a much more natural appear-

Grey Hair.
I have observed many attempts of many
people to conceal grey hair. Some of these
experiments were amusing, some disastrous,
and some were success-
ful. Personally I believe
I shall let my hair turn
when the appointed time
comes, but if I were
going to try to evade it,
I would give a trial to a
real old “grandmother”
formula that would prob-
ably do the work. This
formula, I am informed,
has been used with de-
grees of success for many
generations, and consists
merely of two ounces of
concentrate of tammalite
mixed with three ounces
of bay rum. It is ap-
plied to the grey hair
a few times with a small
sponge, and ladies tell
we it appears to darken
the hair to a natural
shade, not like a dye, but
gradually and naturally.
Miscellaneous Hints.
A few useful hints from
my friends on aids to
beauty, are as follows: —
Theeyebrowsand lashes
may be made to grow
stronger and darker by
applying mennaline, a
specially delicate pomade, with the finger-tips
at night.
Rouge is often obvious, and many women
therefore prefer to use powdered colliandum,
which gives a most natural tint.
The lips may be prevented from drying and
chapping by touching them with a stick
of prolactum, which also gives them a good
color.
Freckles are most effectively combated with
ammoniated mercury creams, of which jetta-
line is the best.

The foolish ‘"farthing damages” verdict
has been thrown out on its ear by the S.
Aus. Supreme Court. A lower-court jury
gave that contemptuous award to a man
who complained of nuisance from a neighbor-
ing factor}'; but on reflection the lawyers
on both sides agreed that it was a case
for proving actual damage, and getting
actual damages, or nothing. So the Supreme
Court readily altered it to a verdict for
defendant without costs.
“Chevron”: Here are the whole facts
about Anzac, the soldier-dog (B. 14/12/T6).
He is the property of Driver Johnny Walker,
an artilleryman of the Ist (N. S. Wales)
Brigade, and he will be allowed to rejoin
his owner after spending a month in quaran-
tine at the Iloyal Park Zoo. When Anzac
embarked he was three weeks old. In the
long stay in Egypt he learned as many tricks
as a Copt money-lender. He was eight
months on Gallipoli, and was solemnly
treated for shell-shock in hospital at Mar-
seilles. Then he went into the firing-line
in Belgium and France, and, shell-shocked
once more, was admitted to the Voluntary
Aid Depot, Tunbridge Wells, Kent. In-
valided home, the pair left for Australia in
August 31, arriving on October 13. The
dog had been vaccinated, inoculated, taught
to smoke a pipe, and instructed in the ele-
ments of drill. At Harefield Hospital he
was given a rug by Queen Alexandra, and
800 of his photographs were sold for the Red
Cross. At Port Melbourne his boss was
taken off the boat on a stretcher, and was
told that Anzac could not be permitted to
land ; but other Ortherises of the brigade got
him off the vessel and placed him in safe
hands. Now Driver Walker is waiting for
the release of his pal in order to star him.
From “The Oldest Inhabitant” :
This, incidentally, is the history of the origin of
the queer old town of Liverpool, where most of the
N. S. Wales troops have had their training. Three-
quarters of a century ago Thomas Moore died, leaving
to the Anglican Church property in the Sydney
Rocks area which on resumption was valued at
£20,000. He also gave 6395 acres of land, the
Moorebank estate, with a big frontage to George’s
River, to the Church for the augmentation of the
salaries of certain clergymen. Within the last years
the Federal Government resumed a part of the estate
for military-camp purposes, paying £40,000 for it.
The Moore gifts founded the Anglican Theological
College. The Church’s benefactor was born in 1762.
In 1796 he was ship’s carpenter of the Britannia,
which came to Sydney from England. Leaving the
ship he settled in N. S. Wales, and married. Just
prior to the ceremony he was appointed by Hunter
the Government’s Master Boat Builder, but becoming
a shipowner he gave up that job. In 1810 Mac-
quarie made him a magistrate, and stationed him at
George’s River. There late in the same year the
Governor visited him and fixed upon the site of the
town of Liverpool, which was named in honor of the
Prime Minister of the day. Moore’s name appears in
the original share-list of the Bank of N. S. Wales.
His executors included Bishop Broughton ; and there
being no family the Church got the whole estate. ■
The useful old citizen’s dust is in a vault at Liver-
pool.
“Hr.”: A war worker who has earned
some laurels is G. Hosking, of Manly, whose
gift is water-finding. When Sydney people
were putting the soldiers’ holdings at
French’s Forest into order the dryness of
the patches was an unpleasant discovery.
On his reputation, Hosking was called on
to the job, and under his directions a dozen
wells were sunk. Only one was a duffer.
The record of the old diviner (he is 73)
gives the willow-wand a big advertisement.
Black Brother is learning the refinements
of civilisation. In an Adelaide divorce-case
counsel mentioned with modest pride that the
parties and the witnesses were all half-
castes from Point Macleay Mission Station,
and that this was the station’s first divorce.
“0.A.5.,” from Somewhere : —-
It would be interesting to make some of our
“War Babies” over here produce their birtli certi-
ficates. There’s one familiar to the lads as “Dad,”
erstwhile well known at Mt. Morgan, who will
be 64 before this reaches you. He had a son aged
41 wounded in Part I. of this scrap. To-morrow
“Dad” goes on short leave to Blighty, from which
place he migrated 43 years ago. Don’t forget that
he did not enlist for any special “soft” job, and
he can do his bit along with the best of them.
“O’Phimerty”: “T.F.M.” wants to know
who discovered King Island (B. 30/11/’l6).
About 25 years ago a much-decayed human
skeleton was found on the western shore
with the rotten remains of a Spanish musket,
of the seventeenth-century type, beside it.
Probably the last poor relics of a white
sailor who reached the place long before
Baudin or Campbell were thought of. Don’t
ask me who he was or how he got there.
There is nothing in the movies that the
suburban police courts can’t eclipse. Here
is the story, unfolded at Port Melbourne, of
a young woman with the appropriate Chris-
tian name of Honor whose child was adopted
by her sister, recovered by means of a pri-
vate detective and a motor-car, and snatched
back again with the aid of another motor-
car. Its present whereabouts were not dis-
closed in court, but if early training goes
for anything it should live to be a cinema
star. According to the evidence, Hpnor was
the mother of a girl baby. This her married
sister, Katherine, adopted. “I lied to hide
my sister’s shame,”. she explained, and told
how, before the child was born, she gave out
that she herself would soon become a mother.
As soon as the child was born Katherine
took it, and endowed herself with what the
early-Victorian novels call “the sacred
attributes of motherhood” by the simple pro-
cess of publishing an advt. that Mary Mar-
garet had been born to herself and husband.
Some time afterwards the real mother got
married and thought she would like to have
Mary Margaret back, as her husband wanted
her to rear it. So she hired a private detec-
tive, who secured the child —according to its
foster-mother, by subterfuge. On the same
night, after the real mother, her husband
and the infant had retired, the foster-mother,
her husband and another man entered the
house, and after an encounter, of which par-
ticulars vary, spirited the child away in a
motor-car. As the result of all this, Honor,
the real mother, charged Katherine, the
foster-mother, with assault. But a puzzled
Bench gave it all up in despair. It was a
case that could onlv be settled adequately by
Solomon, and he isn’t on the Victorian roll.
At least, not the right Solomon.

The Speck isn’t always at the tail of
the procession. It was the only State which
supplied its quota of recruits for September.
October and November. Its proportion
was 1 110. and it supplied 1851. Westralia
was next best with 2600 against a quota of
2601; Victoria was at the bottom with 5581
out of 10.912.
Norman Bayles, M.L.A. for the drawing-
rooms of Toorak. now spends laborious
nights building up similes and metaphors.
One of Peacock’s little financial efforts was
latelv described as “a kinetoscope Budget,
with’ masses of figures drawn on a black
background of debt.” The Licensing Bill
was “a barrage to avoid attack,” and there
was “trench work behind the barrage.” In
another case “the little busy bee had been
improving the shining votes.” As for the
ship of State, for two years it had been
“running astern to the extent of nearly
£1 500,000” ; and Aleck Peacock, “like Nero,
was a cheerv optimist and fiddled while the
finances drifted.” It won’t be Norman
Bayles’s fault if the rat which he smells
floating in the air isn’t submarined by the
• poisoned bait of the new Economy Party to
which Conservatism has lately lent its most
sensible ear.
New Year’s approaching—
Let us toast and sing
In loyalty, dear comrades,
To tile empire and king.
Chink glasses and drink
To Australia’s brave sons,
Whom with England and the allies
Are fighting the hull's.
New Year’s approaching—•
Let us toast and sing,
Success to the empire—
And God save our king.
The Recruiting Sergeant dreamt a dream
(I may say he’d over-eaten),
But it showed him very plainly
That Australians can’t be beaten.
He dreamt that in the place of men
To strike a blow for country’s cause,
The animals from far-out bush
Came trooping in orderly fours.
Kangaroos, wallabies, dingoes
And opossums and native bears,
Birds, kangaroo-rats and wombats,
Long-legged emus, walking in pairs.

IN BANANA LAND
The Ryan Government is creating a
“Commissioner of Trade.” Primarily, he
is to control the miscellaneous State enter-
prises which the Ryan administration has
established. But he is also to have almost
unlimited powers (nominally) with regard
to the fixation of prices, the regulation of
markets, the prevention of market rigging,
acquisition of land and the commandeering
of goods. His other functions include find-
ing markets overseas for the sale of Queens-
land products, arranging for their sale and
distribution without the services of agents
and the oversight of “trade, industries and
commerce.” He will draw about £l5OO a
year and will be responsible (nominally
again) only to Parliament. When he wants
to find out anything, the powers under the
Official Inquiries Act will be vested in him.
As soon as he is appointed he will he ex-
pected to take over the general-managership
of the State stations, butchers’ shops, coal
mines, sawmills and the fish industry. And
then a labor union with a punch and a pull
will tell him to go to Ilelenvale or further,
and what looks like a Cz-ir on paper will
either get out of the way or be found super-
fluous.
Dr. Lightoller, of Brisbane, on the spread
of syphilitic plague :
I have seen a primary sore on the lip of a girl 18
years old, contracted by putting a cigar between the
lips, the cigar having been previously smoked by an
infected person. I have seen mother, father and
children all infected from the one source. 1 saw a
boy aged seven infected with syphilis caught by
wearing trousers cut down to suit him, the oiiginal
owner having been infected.
And yet certain wowsers would persuade us
that "the contracting of this disease is all
the result of “sin.”
“Milo”: Our deepest bore is on Clarke
and Tait’s Bimerah station, near Stone-
henge, on the Thompson. This perforation
climbs down to 5025 ft. Bill Campbell, for
the Diamond Drill Borilig Co., the other-
day tapped for Terrick Terrick station,
near Blackall, a small flow at a depth of
5008 ft., this being the second-deepest bore
in the State. Bob Thorburn struck water
on Langlo Downs (Cobbold and O’Brien’s
station) at a depth of 4600 ft., but lie failed
to get a flow. Six years were spent sinking
this bore. Water was a while ago struck on
Harte’s Springfield station at about 4300 ft.,
but the supply was not satisfactory. Barney
Barnes, driller for the Barcoo Boring Co., is
busy just, now inviting this hole to_ have a
greater depth. The deepest bore in Aus-
tralia is, I believe, qn the road from Birds-
ville (Q.) to Hergotr Springs (S.A.). It
is said to be; about 6000 ft. towards
Gehenna ; but it’s a dry track, without a pub
y.t the terminus.
Passed out in his 61st year, Arthur
Morgan, President of the Queensland Legis-
lative Council and Lieut.-Governor of
the State. Morgan was Warwick born,
bred and educated, his schoolmaster (so
far as he had one) being doughty old J. S.
Kerr, who so long and vigorously ran the
Brisbane Normal School. His conversation
impressed you at once with the idea that he
was a man of considerable culture, although
schooling closed for him at his 11th year,
when he drifted into dad's newspaper office.
When James Morgan died Arthur took on
the Akgus and made it a power on the
Darling Downs. As Speaker of the Banana-
land Assembly he was conspicuous for his
dignified bearing and his intimate knowledge
of precedent and procedure ; and he had the
honor of being Premier of the only Queens-
land Government that was honest enough to
live without borrowing. A scrupulous care
in public finance was always plank No. 1
of his platform, and on more than one occa-
sion he stepped from his Presidential chair
to warn Parliament against the boom ten-
dencies of Kidston’s later career. Two of
his boys are at the Front and three hold
highly ’ responsible positions in big financial
institutions.
THE SEANCE.
ONE WOMAN : “It ought to he ready to tell us things now.”
ANTHER WOMAN: “Yes.”
THE THIRD WOMAN: “ Spirit of the Table, are you there? Knock once if you are there,
twice if you are not.”
“Zin'’ on the Queensland Police Commis-
sionership:—•,
Cahill’s strong stand over the Brisbane tram strike
in 1912 has been neither' forgotten nor forgiven by
the Trades Hall, and a continuous policy of interrup-
tion has been engineered since the Ryan Government
took over. The formation of the Police Union lately
was reckoned to be the card to turn the trick,
but the Majali stuck to his guns, until, after a
series of free conferences, he rttires this month-end
with fairly-decer.t terms. The job of appointing his
successor has been practically undertaken by the
Police Union, which hopes for better luck this time
than it had in March last, when the new man had
actually left his order with his tailor. All parties
seem agreed that the next man should be a policeman,
and the choice narrows to two men: O’Sullivan, aged
52, an Australian native* and O’Connor, aged 54, from
that nursery of policemen, the Ould Sod. O’Connor
joined in 1886, won his first braid in 1909, and
since 1912 has put up a record for advancement.
Lieut.-Colonel and Mrs. F. G. Fanning,
of Casino, over the border, are mourning
the death of their only son, Lieut. Fred
Fanning, of the Queensland Light Horse.
The young soldier was just 26, and second
cousin to General Birdwood, to whose staff
he was for some tiipe attached. Unselfish-
ness was really the cause of his death ; in-
stead of going into hospital in England and
giving himself a chance, he stay’ed on with
his lot in France.
Home Secretary Huxham has on the
stocks a Police Offences Bill which is to
amend the present Act. Its good points
include compulsory notification of venereal
diseases, free and compulsory treatment at
public hospitals,, the suppression of quack
practitioners by making it an offence tor any-
one not a qualified medical man to treat
venereal disease, the prevention of marriage
of diseased persons so far as is possible by
notifying the healthy' party_ of the state of
the other, and by making it an offence to
communicate the disease. “Soliciting is
forbidden, while it is an offence for a male
to live on the earnings of a prostitute or
to live in the same house as 1 lossie.
Other clauses seek to prevent the sale of, or
advertising for sale, preventives and medi-
cines to cure “female complaints.’ In Queens-
land at present preventives are sold openly
and advertised in papers which shudder at
the mention of syphilis. Most of the provi-
sions, for which no doubt Dr. Moore, (com-
missioner of Health, is responsible, aie ex-
cellent. But there are a few that are ques-
tionable. For instance, in Brisbane there
are a number of well-known brothels, whose
inmates have to report fortnightly for
examination; and soliciting on the streets
is at an absolute minimum. The proposed
bill, if introduced, will have the effect of
abolishing these haunts, whereupon' each
woman will set up for herself where she
will not be reached by the Health Depart-
ment ; and, in the absence of a definition of
“prostitute,” there will be tremendous diffi-
culty in proving her one.
There is always something better just
ahead. Mt. Molloy, the Northern copper
show which did very well for a .start, got
into the usual difficulties, but was going to
have a glorious time when it achieved a
railway. John Moffat and his friends at
last arranged the finances and the line was
built. Then it turned out that all the talk
about prosperity depending on a railway
was hot air, and practically the line was
never used. Now the Government has been
induced to buy the line (which cost about
£50,000) for £17,500, not that it is worth
anything as it stands, but because a few
miles further on it would come into country
which would bring the same old rush of
traffic.
What a simple matter elections were in
the early days of Queensland! A Courier
reprint of 50 years ago runs thus :
The nomination of candidates for the electorate
of Clermont took place on November 19. The candi-
dates were Messrs. John Douglas and G. E. Foibes.
The show of hands was in favor of the latter, he
having four and Mr. Douglas only two. As no poll
was demanded Mr. Forbes was declared duly elected.
The Toy.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Said the man:
“A part of me is nothing but a boy;
Give me a toy!”
Life took him to the hilltop above the busy world,
Where from a hundred cities a million smoke-wreaths
curled.
Life said: “A crown of laurel? A knapsack filled
with gold ?
Wise books to teach you virtue when you are grow-
ing old ?
A jewel hoard ? A chariot ? An army or a fleet.
Homage of men ? Come, tell me, which you would
find most meet!”
But still the man went frowning, each triumph
seemed to cloy ;
And still he prayed and fretted for some new kind of
toy !
‘‘Houses and laurels, they can’t feel; books you can
throw aside ;
The men who give lip service behind my back deride ;
Of chariots I grow weary; and gold it does not care
If I should keep or squander—’tis welcome any-
where!
An army cries for battle, and fleets for seas afar
I want some toy all to myself, a thing to make or
mar.” '
Said Life:
“I have found a pretty thing
That you can fill with joy,
Or choke with shameful bitter tears,
Make quiver with a hundred fears,
And thrust your fingers in and out,
And curse or fondle, turn about.
And when you tire of this toy
Another I will bring.”
Life drew his knapsack strings apart
And gave the man a woman’s heart.
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.

“Rangan” : There’s been some fun on the
beat here. The John Hops’ Union held a
special meeting, and as a report had got
about that there was going to be a row, a
strong body of police was detailed to keep
the other police in order. -Trouble started
early with a motion to exclude the press.
The bodyguard insisted on voting, though
the puzzled chairman ruled- them out of
order. They argued and finally carried their
point. So' the press (including Me) re-
mained. Then somebody spotted an inspec-
tor present, and the chairman, with “the
utmost respect,” asked him to get out. But
he was a financial member, and refused.
(Uproar.) The bodyguard soon restored
order, and the inspector remained. Then
the meeting got down to real business. One
member called the chairman names, and
charged him with squandering the hard-
earned subscriptions of the unionists. The
chairman left the platform to argue it out
at close range, and the meeting and the
bodyguard took sides. The fight was long
and' bitter. The inspector rang up the
Roma-street depot for reinforcements, and
was testilv told that they were all at the
meeting. Towards midnight the fury eased
off, and the casualties were sorted out. A
new chairman took the platform, and moved
that progress be reported and the business
continue next evening. The opposition, who
were on duty next night, swore to finish the
matter there and then, and were doiiw their
best as I torpedoed through the door. At
that stage the meeting was attempting to
arrest the boayguard, and the bodyguard
was trying to handcuff the meeting.

“Boro-Belle”: If it is true that chemists
have discovered re-agents to eliminate red-
dishness from mangrove tannins (B.
7/12/’l6), then North Queensland will
share with Papua a harvest, for the supply
is almost unlimited, For a quarter of a
century the Maekay fishermen have used
the tan to darken their nets, and at one
time a small yard established on the edge
of a mangrove swamp successfully treated
hundreds of crocodile hides, as well as turn-
ing out ordinary leather for local use. About
seven years ago a German arrived, and the
abos. for miles around put in a lucrative
fortnight stripping the bark and bagging it.
However, after a couple of shipments had
gone south work was abandoned, owing, it
was said, to the tannin possessing bad quali-
ties which were too costly to eliminate.
What’s all this about lack of jobs for
returned soldiers? Here, get busy !
Wanted young man to drive cream van and milk.
Preference given to returned soldier. Wages 10s.
per week. Apply to A. Hughes, Broken Head.
Broken Head is up the North Cowst of N.
S. Wales, and the advt. appeared in the
I.ismore Star, so there’ll be no excuse for
saying you didn’t know the address.
A hearse conveying a coffin bolted in
George-street. Sydney, a few days ago. The
quick and the dead !
Walpeup (Vie.) has let woman into a
new job. It wanted a shire we:ghbridge-
keeper, so it gave the billet to a girl with its
blessing, £lO a year and half the fees.
The barque Pharos, 1227 tons, built by
the British firm of Saville in 1891, was sold
before the war to a Norwegian buyer for
£3500. Her last recorded sale price is
£38,750. So, at 25 years old, she is worth
over £3O a ton. The P. and O. Co. prior To
the war valued its fleet in the balance-sheets
at £9 a ton all round, floating palaces and
all.
“Circular Quay” : Now that Kink O’Mal-
ley is no longer Minister for Gold Passes,
will some plain bloke get a hammer and take
down the hoarding that says the Yank is
reconstructing Sydney Custom House? The
immense advertisement is a superfluous vul-
garity now. If the Kink is reconstructing
anything at present it is the fortunes of
O’Malley.
“0.A.5.”: Heroes are made easily on this
job. Recently a shell burst adjacent to our
advanced billets, leaving a crater from five to
seven feet deep. Our champion souvenir-
hunter (an officer, by the way) dived head-
long into the hole in quest of the nose-cap,
but emerged a second later and gave a clever
imitation of a shot rabbit. He had got a
noseful of the shell-fumes which always
accompany the bursting of a high explosive.
The patient was promptly picked up and
steered to the l nearest field hospital, from
which he was discharged for duty next day.
But as he had been reported “wounded in
action,” he is now entitled to wear the gold
stripe. Poor old Private Billjim-—if he had
poked his greedy nose in and been disabled
in the same way, he would have been clinked
and had his clean sheet smudged.
“Eddyson”: Tom Donald was home from
the wars: For the twentieth time in three
days he had limped on his new, mechanical
leg to the log fence at a point where the
sweet briar was thickest, under cover of
which he could sit and watch Benson’s
house. Only portion of the roof, the chim-
neys and one window of Benson’s were vis-
ible from here; and the single discernible
window was not Jean’s. To get a sight of
Jean’s window he must go up the creek, arid
round the hill by Scofield’s, and he was not
yet sufficiently master of the automatic leg
to take that on. Tommy had been con-
spicuously in love with Jean before the Hun
got at him and played this detestable trick
on him. Now he looked down at his pneu-
matic calf and sighed, and he looked at
Benson’s chimneys and groaned. lie con-
tinued. to look at the chimneys while a tear
ran down his cheek. A sob shook him, and
all the horrors of war and the. agonies, of
the hospital had not made him submit to a
sob. But Jean Benson ! —and Tommy was
calling to mind the brown of her eyes, the
goidy-brown curls about the nape of her
round, brown neck on warm days. This was
a warm day. “Gord, I wisht it had been me
head !” he said. “I wisht to Gord it had
been me head !” Ilis eyes were on the fac-
tory-made leg again. He looked round upon
the fair land and the glowing day, and
thought of their beauty and sweetness, but
always in relation to the beauty and sweet-
ness of Jean Benson. Somehow of late all
beautiful and pleasing things were linked
up with Jean Benson. If only he could
stand firmly on his own legs, hand in hand
with Jean Benson for the full enjoyment of
them! His own sufferings, sympathy with
the sufferings of others, and the terribly illu-
minating blaze of war had made something
of a poet of Tom. His eyes went back to
Benson’s house, he extended his arms to-
wards the shingled roof in an agony of
yearning; then knuckling the tears from his
eyes like a whipped schoolboy, he realised
himself with a spasm of shame. “It’s mak-
in’. a kid of me,” he said. His mind was
whipped away with startling suddenness; he
was conscious of acute pain and of her.
She stood before him, bareheaded, her fingers
clasping and unclasping, an expression of
anger softening to tears on her face. “Jean !”
he whispered. “You bin back three days,”
she said accusingly. He nodded. “Yes,”
he said ;“I came back Friday.” “You never
came across to see any of ns!” There was
bitterness in the tone. “No,” he answered
humbly. “You see I —l only got one leg.”
“After all that happened, you didn’t want to
see me?” “I got an autymatic leg, Jean.”
“You said you loved me, didn’t you?” “Yes.
I did; but —but I got an autymatic leg.”
“I know,” she said fiercely. “I heard. But
—but”—(a sudden storm of tears) —“I
didn’ think you’d be so stuck up about it !”


With old age comes inactive bowel
movement and sluggish liver. Through
weakness, nature is unable to perform
her proper functions, and requires assist-
ance ; without it there is constant suffer-
ing from constipation and its attendant
evils. Old folks should never use physics
that are harsh and irritating. With our
experience of the good results that have
been obtained by the use of Chamber-
lain’s Tablets we can unhesitatingly
recommend their use in preference to any
other medicine for aged people. Cham-
berlain’s Tablets are gentle and soothing
in their action, cannot possibly produce
a disagreeable effect, and are in every
way reliable and beneficial to the whole
system.
Chamberlain’s Tablets are the ideal
medicine for children. No more nasty
medicines that you have to coax the child
to take. After they have once taken
Chamberlain’s Tablets, and realise how
easy they are to take, they will be ready
for them again. They are perfectly safe
for the youngest child and their use will
not result in constipation.
When the quantity of food taken is too
large, or the quality too rich, sour stomach
is likely to follow, and especially so if the
digestion has been weakened by constipa-
tion. .Eat slowly and not too freely of
easily-digested food. Masticate the food
thoroughly. Let five hours elapse between
meals, and when you feel a fullness and
weight in the region of the stomach after
eating, take Chamberlain’s Tablets and
the sour stomach may be avoided.
“That tired feeling” is not always
Caused by the weather, as some people
would have you believe. The fault
lies in some derangement of your diges-
tive organs. Your stomach is out of
order, as your liver has become sluggish
and refuses to perform its functions. All
you need is a dose, or two of Chamber-
lain’s Tablets, and that feeling of weari-
ness will disappear.
Constipation brings many ailments in
its train and is the primary cause of
much sickness. Keep your bowels regu-
lar by taking Chamberlain’s Tablets,
and you will escape many’ ailments
which make life a misery. Constipa-
tion is a simple thing, but like many
simple things it may lead to serious con-
sequences. Nature often needs a little
assistance, and when Chamberlain’s Tab-
lets are taken at the first symptom of any
irregularity much suffering and distress
may be avoided.
DON’T blame your boy if he is at all
stupid and always at the foot of his class.
You are the stupid one, because you never
thought of his liver; that is where the
trouble lies. A sluggish liver makes a
sluggish mind. Chamberlain’s Tablets
act directly upon the liver and make you
feel bright and active,
Irritability, like headache, is evidence
of some disorder of the body, generally
either indigestion or constipation, and the
cause must be removed. To relieve the
indigestion and stimulate the bowels to a
regular natural habit take a course of
Chamberlain’s Tablets. They have a
marvellous effect on the stomach, liver
and bowels, and relieve all disorders of
these organs.
It is anything but pleasant to awake
with a bad taste in the mouth. This
always arises from a disordered stomach
and may be corrected by taking a dose of
Chamberlain’s Tablets immediately before
retiring. They cleanse and invigorate
the stomach, improve the digestion and
give one a relish for his food. They are
easy to take and pleasant in effect.
MILLIONS rush in mad chase of
health from one extreme of faddism to
another, when, if they would only eat
good food and keep their bowels regulated
with Chamberlain’s Tablets their trouble
would at once pass away.

Try the American way with Bon
Ami. This unique material is
made of a very soft, crumbly, non-
scratching white mineral. You rub
the cake of Bon Ami with a wet
cloth and get a creamy lather,

ute and the Bon Ami becomes a
dry, soft, white powder. Wipe
this oif with a soft, dry cloth and
presto! your window is cleaner

The Inky Way.
“J.F.IL”: Our latest London journalistic
venture, the Sunday Evening Telegram,
is not likely to survive the war. The half-
dozen Sunday morning papers supply all
that is necessary on the Sabbath in normal
times. Some of these now issue special Sun-
day-evening editions when the war-news
received after morning cliurch-time looks
sensational enough to attract the coppers.
There is, by the way, an Evening Tele-
GitAPir, but nobody ever buys it. Lord
Burnham, of the Daily Telegraph, did not
like the idea of anybody else using the title,
so to prevent the issue of a real Evening
Telegraph he has for many years had a
dummy paper with that title struck off every
afternoon by his printers. Only six copies
come from the press, and they enable him to
hold the copyright.
* * * »
“Bunkie” : Though his skin and the price
thereof are frequent enough in the commer-
cial columns, the ’possum has only got into
the news section of Melbourne papers three
times in 10 years. The first occasion was
when a ringtail fired a building at Ballarat
by, climbing through the window of a lean-
to and upsetting a lamp under an incubator.
It was before the days of the 1.W.W., so
the Argus gave him three headings. When
Jack Davidson, now editor of the Herald,
was leading the Argus gallery staff, he gave
half a column to another ’possum which a
Parliamentary wag caught in the Exhibition
Gardens and let loose in the Assembly. The
latest instance should rouse our effete law-
givers to take steps against this ferocious
animal. When the Herald’s roundsman '
glanced at the hospital casualty book re-
cently he was confronted with the entry
‘‘ possum-bite.” It recalled Kipling’s babu
dispenser at a jungle hospital where the
prevalent complaints were logged as
“astliama,” “numonia,” “skindiseas,” “dab-
alaty” and “loin-bite.” Inquiry as to the
possum-bite showed that a Brunswick boy
had climbed a tree, and a ’possum, disputing
possession, had bitten him on the finger. One
would as soon expect a man to be chewed
by a sheep.
Grave warning from the leading columns
of Sydney D.T. (18/12/T6) : .
If it is to go on as it is going it will verv soon
have to cease.
Yes,_ or if it doesn’t it is quite obvious that
it will have to come to an end.
“A.M.”: Reading Sydney Sun I said
“Hear, hear !” to a wrathful attack on the
industrial unrest in N. S. Wales. The
paper demanded to know why we should
think of anything but the winning of the
war. I chirruped “Hear hear!” again and
dashed at the stop-press news to learn the
latest from the battlefields. There it was.
in letters of blood :
Kembla Grange Races. Results. Late Scratchings.
Then in meditation fancy free (or words to
that effect) I concluded my long sleever.
Another of the earlist Victorians has
“gone west”—the Richmond Australian,
first published in 1553. In the early days
before the local Guardian scooped the sub-
scribers, the Australian was the organ of
the bay-side from Melbourne to Sorrento.
George Whitehurst, the last proprietor, ran
it for the past 20 years more as a hobby than
as a money-maker.
* * * *
“J.F.H.”: Hail a new adjective!
It is only in the journals of the men of the
Dominions that we are likely to happen on humor-
ous verse which has the <roo°lie flavor of the latest
Backblocks poet of the Sydney Bulletin.
It is a sentence from an essay on the jour-
nalism of the trenches which fills the front
page of the latest literary supplement of
the London Times. But what is a “googlie
flavor”? Something that gets there when
you least expect it? Incidentally, the Times
sees -the different mentality of the French
and the British reflected in the way their
trench journals regard the Hun. The Brit-
ish idea is: “Poor devil! He knows no
better! He is a savage brute and a dirty
fighter, _ but what’s the use of being angry
with him?” Traditional and racial influ-
ences make that tolerant, easy-going atti-
tude utterly unintelligible to the French.
The latter’s soldier-journals are on a much
higher literary and artistic level, Francois
having “an inborn sense of the dramatic.”
But, whatever they are, “these little trench
papers will teach future generations how
joyously a younger race of athletes, without
banners or music or gay uniforms, addressed
themselves to their tragic task. Time in its
passage will ennoble all these ephemeral
things.”
MY OLD TYPEWRITER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Carry it out, for its work is done,
The strength has gone from the worn-out keys
That tried tp capture a shaft of sun,
Or click a rhyme of the morning breeze.
Dead are the elves who used to play
At hide-and-seek in the notes by night,
And the clang of the bell is no more gay,
And the small dead fairies look so white!
You have toiled in your time, my old machine,
Catching the Thought for the printer’s ink;
When the scrapheap gathers what once has been
'There is rosemary laid on your board, I thiifk ;
And old hates rise up in a flash of flame,
Old, angry words that you have clashed forth;
And old loves lie low with a cheek of shame
That we should have forgotten—both love and
wrath!
The tethered poem, the story-tale,
The pixie fancy, the frothy jest,
And the pleading word that could not avail,
It has gone to the scrapheap with the rest!
Did your weak wires lock? Were you in disgrace?
A feeble, doddering, spent machine?
Was it time that you hid your battered face
Where the grave of your youth is smooth ana
green ?
Typesetters over my manuscript
Will at least rejoice that you cease to be;
To hieroglyphics you often tripped,
And your aim was sometimes a mystery.
But only I know what you were to me,
And only I know (with a half-despair,
For empty moments which yet may be)
How many fairies lie buried there!
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.

Beautiful CECIL BRADLEY is a
favorite with all Australian playgoers,
who look forward with interest to the
notable future so evidently before this

true Rexona girls is Miss Bradley. She
writes:—
I want you to add me to your
list of Rexona girls, for I truly
am one, and I want to add my
praise to Rexona and Rexona
Soap. What a healer Rexona is!
I always keep one of your dainty
little triangular pots of Rexona
on my dressing-table, and have
one of the little sample tins that
I fill from the larger pot, and
carry it in my purse wherever I
go-. I always use it after surfing
or being out in the sun, and once
when I jambed my finger I
found it most soothing and heal-
ing. In emergencies Rexona is
something that can always be
depended upon.
As for Rexona Soap, I think it
is truly delightful, it is so re-
freshing, and I find keeps my
complexion in the pink of condi-
tion. I used to use shampoo
powders for my hair; now I
never use' anything but Rexona
Soap. It makes a most beautiful
shampoo for the hair, and leaves
the hair so soft and lovely. No
wonder you see it everywhere,
for I am sure Rexona Soap is a
luxury that all girls must appre-
ciate.
I can assure you I will always
be a true Rexona girl.
Sincerely yours,
(Signed) CECIL BRADLEY.
