The Furrow.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
SHUT your eyes, and be at peace.
Though the world be not for you ;
Though its song and splendor cease
In an hour, perchance, or two;
Though the silken breezes roam
And the velvet waters flow,
’Tis Home, Home, Home,
With a heart at ease you go.
Though your skies were brazen-arched,
And the lands about you bare,
And your life lame-footed marched
From high hope to grim despair;
Though the’ furrow that you ploughea
Was in starved and stony soil,
Spirit-bent and body-bowed,
Here's an end to all your toil.
Though there’s twilight in the skies.
And the grey trees shadowed bend,
Clear and deep your furrow lies,
True and straight from end to end ;
Though a curlew calls afar
Through the dewy evening air,
Under sun, and moon, and star
It will lie forever there.
Though ’tis little that you brought
To the world’s accomplishment,
Though in barren fields you wrought,
Body-bowed and spirit-bent,
When the ploughmen take their pay
And the final furrow’s turned,
’Twill be found upon that day
That your wage was truly earned.
In the rain-time, in the dew,
In the noontide’s heat and glare,
Came the ploughman Care to you,
And he drove his cruel'share
Through the nodding flowers of life,
Grimly pleased to see them fall.
Oh, the passion and the strife,
Oh, the heartache of it all!
Shut your eyes and be at rest,
Weary toiler, heavy-tasked ;
You have done your little best,
And no more than that was asked;
Though ’tis tired of foot we roam,
Far and near and high and low,
’Tis Home, Home, Home,
With a heart at eas.e you go.
n.s.aV.
RODERIC QUINN.
The Salters.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
EVERYBODY admitted Dr. Gibson’s
skill in the practice of his profession
and almost everybody liked him per-
sonally. The exceptions consisted of a few
who wished to constitute themselves a special
class in the town and expected the medical
man to join them. But he received their
advances coldly: he preferred to take his
friends where he found them, irrespective of
any clique and without regard to whether
they wore dungarees or a. different sort of
clothes. Yet even these select ones could
not dispute his ability as a surgeon; they
only regretted that he was not also a gentle-
man. On the other hand, his best friends
spoke of him as simply a fool in mining
matters. Any old battler had merely to come
to him with a pocket full of gold-flecked
gravel and a tongue .in his head and
The doctor during his two and a half
years in Dingo Flat had spent much money
in his efforts to help the development of the
district, and, incidentally, his own bank
account, but so far had little to show bar-
ring sundry holes in the ground. By the
end of that time all his interests had been
dropped, one after another, until there was
left only Mt. Gibson. His ewe lamb, he
called it. The finders of this show, being
ardent admirers of the medical man, had
named it after him. Subsequently he had
bought them out one by one (there were
three of them) as they became discouraged
or got an attack "of the wandering sickness.
Now therefore he owned it all and was as
incurably sanguine about it as if he had
never had a rebuff.
Dr. Gibson had two men working this
claim for him—Bill and Jack Hamilton,
brothers, whom he had employed in many
other places and who were devoted to him.
They were his good friends, inasmuch as
they acted as a curb to his over-sanguine
temperament and on many occasions had
kept him from throwing good money after
bad. He realised this well and had the
greatest respect for their judgment and
honesty. Bill Hamilton was thirty-two,
short,, thick-set, red, somewhat irascible.
Jack, twenty-seven, was tall, lean, taciturn.
The district had become very unpopular
with investors. In eighteen months four
batteries had been erected within a radius
of ten miles of Dingo Flat, and in each case
the first crushing had been the last. The
Reward, which according to the manager
and sundry “mining experts,” was a two-
ounce show, went two pennyweights. The
Cliffs —ah, yes, the Cliffs. A whole moun-
tain here was to be passed through the bat-
tery for an average of ten ’weights. Then
the battery spoke and it said ten grains.
And so on. Naturally the locality was
banned. Investors or their representatives
still came through on their way to other
fields, but nothing would induce them to
look at anything in the neighborhood. It
had been that way many months. But
lately it had been whispered that a great
improvement had taken place in Mt. Gibson;
and it was understood (had, in fact,
appeared in black and white in the
Clarion) that special efforts on the part
of the owner were likely to result very soon
in a visit from a Kalgoorlie expert. “Now
they’ll see,” we said ; “and once these people
see for themselves what the district really
i 5”.... We were to be over-run with in-
vestors- tumbling over one another to get
into the good things.
At 4 o’clock of a warm Sunday afternoon
the Hamilton brothers sat in their camp
on the side of a hill, smoking. The camp
consisted of large bough shed, with two
sleeping tents in the corners at the rear
end, and a table, with fixed legs, in the
middle. They were evidently engaged dis-
cussing a difficult point.
“Well, we got to do it, an’ that’s all about
it.’’
“No,” said Jack; “we haven’t got to do
it.”
“What else can we do?”
“Nothing!”
“I ain’t goin’ to sit ’ere doin’ nothing
while a good man an’ a good friend to you
an’ me goes down. What’s the odds? These
people can afford it. A few thousand’s
nothing to them.”
“That isn’t the point.”
“It’s-the only point I can see. I tell you
the doctor’s pinched pretty bad. I can see
it. He’s a game ’un. He tries not to
show it. All the same, it’s there.”
“It’s crook work —you can’t get over that.
And we’ve always gone straight. It’s
breaking the law.”
“I’d break fifty flamin’ laws for the old
doctor. ’Tain’t as if we was doin’ it fer
ourselves. An’ don’t forget he saved your
life. You said so often yourself.”.
Jack got up and moved about uneasily.
The other watched him closely. He went
to the door and looked out along the track.
Then he came back and sat down.
“He’s coming,” he said. “All right. I’m
in.”
The other put out his hand and they
gripped silently.
“Good day, Doc.,” said Bill, the next
moment.
“Good day, Bill. Day, Jack. It’s good
to be with you out here in the bush. Don’t
know what I’d do if I hadn’t these trips
into the Mulga to look forward to. How are
you both?”
“I’m first rate, Doctor, but Jack looks a
bit liverish, or else it’s one of his dour days.
Our Scotch blood, ye know. Anyway, he
took one o’ your pills, to be on the safe
side.”
"Well,” said the doctor, smiling, as he
seated himself, “if it doesn’t do him any
good, at least it won’t hurt him. And how
is our little hole in the ground looking?”
“Better than ever. Something fresh here
to show you.”
He went into one of the tents, brought
out a calico bag and emptied the contents
on to the table. The doctor examined the
pieces of quartz, first with the naked eye,
then through a lens the miner handed him.
“What’ll this go? Five ounces?”
“Nearer ten.”
“Much of it?”
“Not all as good as that, of course. That’s
the best. But the reef’ll go over two
ounces where that comes from.”
“You think it’s all right, then?”
“I do, and so does Jack.”
The doctor looked inquiringly towards the
latter.
“Oh, yes! she’s all right. Only it’s not
what we think that counts. It’s got to
satisfy those Boulder fellows, and they want
a deuce of a lot these days.”
“Well, they’ll be in the Flat to-night and
here to-morrow.”
“Couldn’t be better,” said Bill. “We’ll
show ’em something’ll surprise even them.”
“And, mind, you boys have stuck to me
well and have, I know, saved me throwing
away many a pound ; and if this deal comes
off you won’t be forgotten. So long! See
you next Sunday.”
The medical man gone, the brothers set
about getting tea. They intended that it
should be a good solid meal, for they were
going to work a long shift after it. And
it teas solid. As they sat smoking after-
wards, the elder said:
“We must tie those tent doors tight before
going down.”
“Why? Think anyone’s likely to rob us
or^ that it would matter if they did?”
“No, I don’t. But if someone did come
and found the place all opefi, he’d think we
was somewhere about an’ go lookin’ for us.
But if he found it shut up he’d think we’d
gone somewheres an’ he’d go too.”
“Yes! I didn’t think of that.”
. “You wouldn’t think of anything if I
didn’t tell you. Buck up now, an’ don’t look
so miserable. There’s no danger.”
“I'll be all right. Don’t you worry.”
A little after eight that night two figures
were moving along the hillside. Presently
they disappeared and nothing more was to
be seen of them until just before dawn. Then
they reappeared and their faces looked pale
and haggard. Still, there was a look of
subdued triumph, as of something accom-
plished.
“That ought to about fix up ’Awkins,”
said the elder.
i “It would fix him,” said the younger, “if
ne was ten times as clever as they say he
is.”
“Conscience ain’t troublin’ you now?”
“Oh. to hell with my conscience! I knew
from the jump I was in it. anyway. That
was only my way of pretending to be better
than you. You’re worth a dozen of me.”
The other only shook his head and laughed
indulgently.
* * * *
At 10 o’clock “Boulder” Hawkins, as he
was called, and his assistant came on the
scene. They nearly took the brothers’breath
away by informing them that they had
started for the mine the previous night, but
had missed the track and found themselves
at Quartz Hill, whence they had driven
across. They went to business right away,
spending the remainder of that and two
more days breaking stone and dollying and
panning off and collecting samples and
bagging, sealing and labelling them. And
during that time not a word did they let
fall nor a sign did they give that would
hint the least indication of their opinion of
the show. It was only when they were hav-
ing a parting glass with the brothers that
Hawkins spoke.
“I like the look of it,” he said. “Of
course there’s no certainty until we’ve gone
through those”—pointing to the samples.
But it certainly looks promising. Wilkie
thinks so too—which is important. He’s
s<> much more difficult than I am.
(Wilkie smiled his quiet smile.) “Thanks'
boys,” he continued. “You’ve looked after
us well and made our trip very pleasant
whatever comes of it. And when next
you re m Kalgoorlie, if you’ll look ns up
(Continued on next page.)

NO BLADE SO KEEN
NO SHAVE SO CLEAN.
we’ll try to give you a little of your own
back —eh, Wilkie?”
They turned to wave just before disappear-
ing from view.
"Bit rough,” said Bill, scratching his
head, “that you can’t do a good turn to
one man without hurtin’ another.”
"It’s hell, isn’t it?” replied Jack, smiling
ruefully.
* ' * * *
It was again Sunday and the brothers
were sitting in their camp, expectant.
“He’s late,” said Bill. “Bad case most
likely.”
“Either that or the news is bad and he’s
in no hurry to bring it.”
“Oh, the news is all right. Only hope
he’s had the sense to stick out for a thump-
ing deposit. Here he is.”
The doctor looked up and waved a hand,
then bent down again over the handle bars.
“Good news,” said the eldeV, “or I’m no
judge.”
“Looks like it.’i
Presently they were shaking hands. The
doctor kept hold of them, one each, in his
strong grip and beamed.
“Everything’s right, Bill and Jack,” he
said. “Took a six months’ option. They
have to do the work now.”
“What terms?”
“I get 10,000 fully paid-up fl shares.”
“‘But the cash? Ain’t they givin’ you
any cash?”
“No cash, Bill. I take it all in shares.”
“But —don’t tell me there’s no deposit!”
“No ! there’s no deposit.”
“Good God!”
Jack could say nothing—only stared open-
mouthed.
“Why, what’s the matter? It’s only a
few months. Look at the Princess Ida.
Soon after the six months were up the
vendors were offered £3 a piece for their
shares.”
“The Ida people took good care to get a
£2500 deposit.”
“So much the worse. There would have
been 2500 more shares to sell at £3. Come,
don’t look so glum! Hawkins himself is
just as sanguine about it as you were.
Whatever’s changed you? Think I’m short
of ready cash? It’s not that bad yet. And
while they’re proving this you chaps can be
looking around for something else. We’re
partners still, aren’t we?”
“I should think so,” said Bill.
- “Just as long as you have any use for
us, Doc.,” said Jack.
“That’s all right, then. What about that
whisky you were going to ask me to have?”
As he was leaving the doctor handed an
envelope to each of the brothers. When they
opened them they found in each case a signed
transfer of UOO fully paid-up Mt. Gibson
shares. They looked at one another.
“Blanky hard work tryin’ to help a fool,
ain’t it?” said Bill.
Jack could say nothing. He turned to
make up the fire for tea.
LIZARD.
The Phonograph and the
Perfect Cooker.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
AWHILE ago I owned a phonograph
which was cursed with a tempera-
ment. In its gentler moods it was
a thing of joy. Its rendering of “Love
Birds” and “Baby’s Shoes” would jday upon
our very heartstrings. Then there was its
version of-that uplifting thing, “The Kooka-
burras’ Chorus.” It was simply immense
with its performance of “The Mopoke’s
Lament,” and “Father’s Hair Is Thinning
Now” once drew tears from the eyes pf an
agent who was forcing a sale at our door-
stop.
But this phonograph, when in savage
mood, was a thing of jibbing, cantankerous
habit. It would stop en route to one of
Melba’s highest notes to sneeze and whine
like a jackal coughing in a cavern. A
drinking bout of the best oil or a rest cure
would sometimes appease it. There was
one occasion when, a possible, purchaser of
the accursed thing being present, it made
the singer of an aria from “Othello” begin
barking (unless the record lied)“ like a dog.
“Yap! G-r-r-r-YAP! G-r-r-YAP!” it
yelped. So realistic was the imitation that
the buffoon of my household, a person whose
sense of humor has long been my bane,
offered the thing a bone.
For the sake of a possible sale I had
turned the fiasco off as casually as might
be with vague references to defective
records. But the purchaser asked to hear
the aria again. Confound the fellow, he
had no tact! Half-way through the exqui-
site air the barking and snarling was re-
peated, several dogs, by that time, seeming
to have joined in the fight.
The purchaser, with an absent eye and
a jingling of coin in his pocket, said : -“How
much you arskin’ —fer th’ whole box o’
snakes?”
“Three pound ten,” I answered firmly,
“and a bargain too. Phonograph, tripod,
floral horn, two carrying”
“And the dog thrown in!” It was the
buffoon again, deliberately spoiling the deal.
Controlling my voice I continued, “
two carrying-cases and fifty” .
“Cut it,” said the dealer absently, scratch-
ing his ear with a toothpick : “I know all
the items be heart —I sell ’em. Three pun’
ten!” Here he yawned. “H’m, well? The
lies’ I can offer is the half gold-boy. Knock
off the quids.” He tendered the coin as
evidence of his intention to do business.
When pa returned from helping the dealer
off the premises with his boot the machine
was at its best again, bawling “It’s Never
Too Late to Mend.”
Yes, it was a variable instrument, subject
to strange lapses. Its “real” ruby was con-
tinually becoming dull and being replaced
by another —eigliteenpence each, or twelve
shillings the dozen. Its mechanism was ner-
vous too. The slightest movement in its
vicinity would cause it to retrace its notes
and repeat itself indefinitely, as in “Jock’s
Joke.” This was, properly speaking, not
so much a joke as an uncracked nut, owing
to this defect in our machine. The record
brazenly shouted until it reached the in-
quiry :
An’ whaur was Jock? Na, na, ye rogue,
Ye ken that Jock’s
Here the mechanism would interfere -and
rob us of the cream of the item, reiterating
only, ‘’Ye ken that Jock's .— ! Ye ken that
Jock's — !" We listened attentively in the
hope of finding out where he was, but never
could. And one day “Jock’s Joke” was
knocked on to the floor and broken, so now
we will never know.
When the neighbors dropped in o’ nights
for a little music it would take pa most
o? the evening adjusting the jibograph. Its
tripod had to be delicately yet firmly placed
or it would allow the tin horn to suddenly
swing off, leaving the record whispering
mysteriously like a conspirator in an assas-
sination. The slightest movement amongst
the company, too, gave it the shivers,
unpleasant vibrations and quiverings. For
instance, if Caruso was lacking in tremolo,
it more than supplied the want. Then it
would stop for winding or to have its horn
blown, or some other little attention, and pa,
wrestling carefully with the swaying, clank-
ing tinware, conscious that the embarrassed
guests were getting restive, would cuss under
his breath. And generally, by that time,
the “ruby” in the speaker had owned up to
being merely glass, and the machine become
inaudible in consequence. Dad had to put
his head right into the horn to find out
what it was singing about; and by the
time he had found out the guests were look-
ing round for their hats and talking time-
tables, and our musicale was at an end.
We wished to sell the thing; but candid
relations affirmed fhat the man fool enough
to give hard cash for such a fraud as ours
did not exist. So latterly the children made
home a Hades with it. Wrangling over its
adjustment, arguing over its fifty records—•
not one of which has not palled upon us—
the storm of their complaints reached our
ears: “Mum, I can’t get my turn ! They’re
putting ‘Kookaburra’ on again!” “Kooka-
burra” is snatched off before the first jackass
can regain his wind. Then—“ Mum, they’re
putting comics on again!” Next—“ Take
that rotter off, somebody!” Another voice:
“No, you don’t! Not that coon dance—it’s
stale!” And then a voice wailing up the
hall: “Mum ! They’re putting ‘Love Burls’
on again ! They know it makes me sicfc!”
As the complainant cries, the nauseatingly
familiar twitter of these sentimental fowls
is cut short. A ruthless hand interrupts
their wooing. It is pa’s. He is laying
around with a strap. The children, our love
birds, fly to cover. Then pa’s foot catches
in the tripod, the horn immediately swing-
ing off fells him, and he and the tinware
lie upon the floor amid the shouts of the
contending forces.
That day marked an epoch. The awful
thing had to go. Looking down the adver-
tising columns I found that though many
wished to sell talking-machines none wished
to buy. But there my eye caught—
Wanted to exchange a Perfect Cooker, nearly new,
perfect order, eats no wood, for a phonograph.
Apply, etc., etc.
I applied swdftly by post. Then he came,
the being simple enough to take to his bosom
a phonograph, sawny enough to part with
a stove so well trained, so intelligent as to
cook for six on “a handful of chips.” Such
a stove has been my unattainable ideal
through a life poisoned by cookers that ate
half a ton of coal and then blew a half-
incinerated chop into the next parish.
At sight of him —old, honest, guilelessly
telling me of the stove’s one defect (“A wee
sma’ crack in tli’ top-plate. I’ll no deceive
ye, Ma’am, but no detriment to the worrkin’
o’ it!”) —I almost wept. My family pro-
tested at my brutality in taking down the
innocent, but that phonograph had to go.
Our buffoon being absent, I turned it on.
It behaved unexpectedly well —on carefully-
selected records. He favored “comics” as a
relief to life in the Backblocks, whither he
was “just awa’ ” to live with his son. And.
being a Jingoistic old Scot, he uncovered
respectfully at “My King, My Flag,” an
item hitherto reserved for
tious.
Soon after I interviewed the Perfect
Cooker in the presence of its owners in
their poor but honest kitchen. Again he
dilated on the “wee sma’ crack” in the
top-plate. There the defect was—open and
aboveboard —faintly discernible by the dim
light of kerosene. The Innocent’s wife
descended upon rheumatic joints to open
the oven doors, showing smooth, immacu-
late shelves and shining knobs. From a
cupboard she produced a sugary confection
which almost flew away from sheer lightness.
She said it was a shortbread, baked in the
cooker, and gave convincing statistics as to
method, time required for its production, etc.
“Aye, th’ stove’s a good yan, an’ I wadna
pairt wi’ it, but we canna tak it wi’ us, ye
ken.”
The exchange was made.
(Here comes in a long pause for pro-
fanity.)
(Here comes in a terrace of curses.)
(Here yoir can happen on pa’s unbiassed
opinion of the entire human race.)
(Here you meet with Beelzebub.)
Rather more than “a handful of chips”
was needed. When all the kindling was
used pa came out and chopped up the logs.
The oven remained calm and cool. We
made excuses for it, though a sickly feeling
of disillusionment gripped our vitals. Pa
fed it again—wood, and more wood, coal,
coke! A horrid smell of singeing was the
only result, and smoke enough for the burn-
ing of Moscow crept through the “wee sma’
crack” in the top-plate.
Then pa, who has no patience, bellowed,
and with a lighted torch went head-first into
the oven—and unexpectedly through its bed-
plate.
“Damme! Damme!” he yelled, backing
out. “No wonder the stove won’t heat! The
thing has no hot tom, and the back’s burnt
through!”
It was too true. The man whom I had
“taken in,” and who was even then en route
to the fastnesses of Gippsland, had, in the
dim recesses of the Perfect Cooker, covered
up with care and cunning, with paint and
cardboard, the wear and tear of a lifetime.
